The Act vs. the Effect in Divine Will and Power
Nasafi corrects a common misstatement: the act itself is not under will and power, but the effect is.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine act itself is not under will and power, but the created effect is, and how to interpret common phrases like 'He willed to create.'

Core Idea
Will and power attach to the effect, not the act.

Nasafi addresses a subtle point about divine attributes. Some mistakenly think that God's act of creating falls under His will and power. Nasafi corrects this, explaining that it is the created thing—the effect—that is the object of will and power. He also shows how common expressions are figures of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning.
Notice the concise style of Nasafi.
2
Identify the key terms: fi'l and maf'ul
Understand the difference between the act (fi'l) and the effect (maf'ul). This distinction is central to the argument.
3
Understand the claim and its denial
The passage begins with 'what they claim' – that the act falls under will and power – and then denies it with 'mamnu'' (forbidden). Then the correction: 'rather, what falls under them is the effect.'
4
Examine the example of 'willed to create'
The phrase 'He willed to bring the world into existence' is actually a figure of speech (tawassu'); its true meaning is 'He willed the existence of the world.'
The act of bringing into existence (ijad) is not the object of will; the existence (wujud) is.
5
Apply the same reasoning to power
Nasafi concludes that the same applies to power: power relates to the effect, not the act.
This maintains consistency in divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أن فعله تعالى يدخل تحت الإرادة والقدرة. ممنوع، إنما الداخل فيهما المفعول ولو قرن بما هو فعله نحو قولهم أراد إيجاد العالم. فمعناه أراد وجوده غير أنه قرن بالإيجاد توسعا في العبارة، وكذا القدرة على هذا.
And what they claim – that His act (fi'l), exalted be He, falls under will (al-irada) and power (al-qudra) – is denied. Rather, what falls under them is the effect (al-maf'ul). Even if they couple it with what is His act, like their saying 'He willed to bring the world into existence (ijad al-'alam),' its meaning is 'He willed its existence,' except that it is coupled with 'bringing into existence' as an extension (tawassu') in expression. And likewise power (al-qudra) is upon this.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage clarifies a terminological point about divine will and power. The opponent claims that the divine act itself (fi'l) – e.g., the act of creating – is the object of will and power. Imam Nasafi denies this. He explains that what is truly under will and power is the effect (al-maf'ul), i.e., the created thing, not the act of creating. When we say 'God willed to create the world,' this is a figure of speech (tawassu'); the precise meaning is 'God willed the existence of the world.' Similarly, when we say 'God has power to create,' it means He has power over the existence of the creature. This is important for understanding divine attributes: will and power relate to the created things, not to the eternal act of creating.

Key Terms
irada
qudra
fi'l
maf'ul
tawassu'
ijad

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The divine act of creating is itself the object of will and power.
Correction: The object of will and power is the created effect, not the act of creating; expressions like 'willed to create' are figurative.

Checkpoint

A) God willed the act of creation itself.
B) God willed the existence of the world.
C) God willed that the world be created by an intermediary.
D) God willed both the act and the world as one entity.

Key Takeaway
Will and power relate to the created effect (al-maf'ul), not to the divine act of bringing into existence.

Linguistic Derivation of 'Will' (al-Irāda)
Al-Nasafi explains the linguistic derivation of 'al-irāda' from Arabic roots.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of the word 'irāda' (will) and its two possible root meanings in Arabic.

Core Idea
The word 'will' in Arabic stems from physical deviation, revealing a rich conceptual background.

Before discussing divine will, al-Nasafi establishes the linguistic foundation. He shows that 'irāda' derives from a root meaning either 'request' or 'inclination,' both implying a deviation from a straight path. This analysis helps prevent misunderstanding of God's will as implying change.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note the key terms like 'al-rawd', 'al-ṭalab', and 'al-mayl'.
Pay attention to the proverb and the description of the swaying girl.
2
Identify the Two Meanings
The linguists say 'irāda' is from 'al-rawd', which can mean 'request' or 'inclination'. Find the evidence given for each meaning.
The evidence includes the term 'rāʻid' (seeker of herbage) and the description of a girl swaying.
3
Consider the Two Possibilities
Al-Nasafi presents two possibilities: either the root is inclination but used for request, or the root is request but used for inclination. Understand his reasoning for each.
This shows the interplay between physical movement and abstract intent.
4
Reflect on the Purpose
Ask yourself why al-Nasafi begins with a linguistic analysis before discussing God's will. This sets the stage for a precise theological discussion.
Linguistic clarity prevents misinterpretation of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع
۱ - الكلام (۱) في أن الإرادة صفة الله تعالى أزلية:
أن (۲) الواجب علينا أن نقدم بيان حد الإرادة، واشتقاق هذه اللفظة من اللغة على ما هو المقصود من الباب، ليصير ذلك ذريعة يتوصل بها إلى ما هو المقصود
فنقول - والله الموفق
اشتقاق لفظ الإرادة]:
إن أهل اللغة قالوا: إن الإرادة مشتقة من الرود، والرود يذكر ويراد به الطلب، ولهذا سمي طالب الكلأ (۳) المتقدم على قومه المنتحين القاصدين مساقط الغيث رايدا، يقال في المثل السائر : لا يكذب الرايد أهله. ويقال: إنه الميل ومنه قولهم: جارية رودا وجوار رود، ويقال للواحد أيضا ،رود، وهي التي تتمايل في مشيتها للين أطرافها ورطوبة أعطافها . ثم من الجائز أن يكون الأصل فيه هو الميل، إلا استعمل في الطلب، لما أن الطالب للشيء لا يمشي على سنن الاستقامة، بل يميل عنها تارة إلى اليمنة وتارة إلى اليسرة، وكذا الطالب كلأ يميل لا محالة عن سواء الطريق تارة إلى هاهنا وتارة إلى هناك، لينظر أي (٤) الأمكنة أكثر كلأ وأوفر خصبا. ومن الجائز أن يكون الأصل فيه هو الطلب إلا أنه استعمل في الميل، لأن الميل عن الاستقامة في العادات الجارية لن يكون إلا لطلب شيء يأملونه لقضاء ما يحتاجون إليه، فسمي الميل رودا لأنه اسم ما هو المقصود منه. (هذا هو (۹)) مأخذ هذه اللفظة في اللغة (٦) .
Chapter Four: Discussion on that the will is an attribute of God Almighty, eternal. It is obligatory upon us to present an explanation of the definition of the will, and the derivation of this word from language, as is the purpose of the chapter, so that this becomes a means to arrive at what is intended. So we say – and God is the grantor of success: Derivation of the word 'will': The linguists said: 'Will' is derived from 'al-rawd', and 'al-rawd' is mentioned and intended for request. For this reason, the seeker of herbage who precedes his people, heading towards the places of rainfall, is called 'ra`id'. One says in the common proverb: 'The ra`id does not lie to his family.' And it is said: it means inclination, from which comes their saying: 'A girl is rawda' and 'plural rawd', and it is said also for the singular 'rawd', which is she who sways in her walk due to the softness of her limbs and moisture of her sides. Then it is possible that the root of it is inclination, but it is used for request, because the seeker of something does not walk on the path of straightness, but rather inclines from it sometimes to the right and sometimes to the left. Likewise, the seeker of herbage inevitably inclines from the straight road sometimes to here and sometimes to there, to see which place has more herbage and richer fertility. And it is possible that the root of it is request, but it is used for inclination, because inclination from straightness in customary habits will not be except for seeking something they hope for to fulfill what they need. So inclination was called 'rawd' because it is the name of what is intended from it. This is the origin of this word in the language.
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic analysis of the word 'will' (al-irāda) by al-Nasafi. It begins with the heading 'Chapter Four' and announces the topic: the will as an eternal attribute of God. The author states that it is necessary to first present the definition (ḥadd) and linguistic derivation (ishtiqāq) of the word, as a means to the intended goal. Then he provides the derivation. The linguists say 'irāda' is derived from 'al-rawd' (الرود), which means request (ṭalab). As evidence, they mention that the one who seeks herbage (al-kala‘) and goes ahead of his people to find places of rainfall is called 'rā‘id' (رايد). The proverb 'the rā‘id does not lie to his family' illustrates this. Alternatively, some say 'rawd' means inclination (mayl). From this comes the description of a swaying girl as 'rawda' or 'rawd'. The author then notes two possibilities: either the root is inclination but used for request, because a seeker physically inclines left and right; or the root is request but used for inclination, because inclination usually occurs to seek something. In either case, the word 'irāda' carries the notion of deviation from a straight path, whether physical or metaphorical. Al-Nasafi concludes by stating that this is the origin of the word in language.

اللغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'irāda' is a purely abstract concept with no physical connotation.
Correction: The linguistic root of 'irāda' ('rawd') implies either request or inclination, both of which involve deviation from a straight path. This shows that the term carries a sense of movement or direction, which has implications for how we understand divine will—it does not imply literal change in God but rather a relational attribute.

Checkpoint

D) Creation (khalq)

Key Takeaway
The word 'irāda' (will) linguistically derives from a root meaning either 'request' or 'inclination', both implying deviation from a straight path, which is foundational for discussing God's will without anthropomorphism.

Al-Nasafi prioritizes linguistic definition before theological discussion because it 'becomes a means to arrive at what is intended'. Etymology of the Word Irada
Nasafi explains the linguistic derivation of the term irada (will).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic roots of the term irada (will) and how it relates to seeking and inclination in classical Arabic.

Core Idea
The word irada comes from seeking or inclination—both rooted in physical movement.

This lesson explores how the classical Arabic word for will (irada) is derived from concrete experiences of seeking pasture and physical swaying. By examining the linguistic origins, we see how abstract theological concepts are built on everyday language. The passage from Nasafi clarifies the two potential meanings and their interrelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's discussion of linguistic derivation sink in.
Notice that Nasafi presents two possible root meanings before discussing priority.
2
Identify the two proposed root meanings
Find the two meanings: seeking (talab) and inclination (mayl). Note the evidence for each—the scout proverb and the swaying woman.
These are concrete, physical examples.
3
Examine the two priority relations
Nasafi suggests two possibilities: either inclination was original and applied to seeking, or seeking was original and applied to inclination. Understand both arguments.
Both are linguistically plausible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشتقاق لفظ الإرادة]: إن أهل اللغة قالوا: إن الإرادة مشتقة من الرود، والرود يذكر ويراد به الطلب، ولهذا سمي طالب الكلأ المتقدم على قومه المنتحين القاصدين مساقط الغيث رايدا، يقال في المثل السائر : لا يكذب الرايد أهله. ويقال: إنه الميل ومنه قولهم: جارية رودا وجوار رود، ويقال للواحد أيضا ،رود، وهي التي تتمايل في مشيتها للين أطرافها ورطوبة أعطافها . ثم من الجائز أن يكون الأصل فيه هو الميل، إلا استعمل في الطلب، لما أن الطالب للشيء لا يمشي على سنن الاستقامة، بل يميل عنها تارة إلى اليمنة وتارة إلى اليسرة، وكذا الطالب كلأ يميل لا محالة عن سواء الطريق تارة إلى هاهنا وتارة إلى هناك، لينظر أي الأمكنة أكثر كلأ وأوفر خصبا. ومن الجائز أن يكون الأصل فيه هو الطلب إلا أنه استعمل في الميل، لأن الميل عن الاستقامة في العادات الجارية لن يكون إلا لطلب شيء يأملونه لقضاء ما يحتاجون إليه، فسمي الميل رودا لأنه اسم ما هو المقصود منه. (هذا هو) مأخذ هذه اللفظة في اللغة.
The derivation of the word irada (will): The people of language said that irada is derived from al-rawd (seeking), and al-rawd is mentioned and is intended to mean seeking (talab). For this reason, the seeker of herbage who goes ahead of his people, the ones seeking meadows and heading for rainfall areas, is called a rayid (scout). It is said in the common proverb: 'The scout does not lie to his family.' And it is said that it [rawd] means inclination (al-mayl), and from it is their saying: 'a swaying girl (jariya rauda) and swaying girls (jawaarin rud),' and it is also said for the singular: 'rud' (swaying), and she is the one who sways in her walk due to the softness of her limbs and the tenderness of her sides. Then it is possible that the root in it is inclination, but it is used to mean seeking, because the seeker of something does not walk on the path of straightness, but rather inclines from it sometimes to the right and sometimes to the left. Similarly, the seeker of herbage inevitably inclines from the middle of the road sometimes here and sometimes there, to see which places have more herbage and are more abundant. And it is possible that the root in it is seeking, but it is used to mean inclination, because inclination from straightness in customary behavior is only for seeking something they hope for to fulfill their needs. So inclination is called rawd because it is the name of what is intended from it. This is the derivation of this word in the language.
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic analysis of the word 'irada' (will). The author, al-Nasafi, begins by stating that the people of language (ahl al-lugha) say that 'irada' is derived from 'al-rawd'. 'Al-rawd' means seeking (talab). As evidence, the term 'rayid' (scout) is used for the person who goes ahead of a group to seek pasture and water. The proverb 'The scout does not lie to his family' is cited to show the reliability of the one who seeks. Then al-Nasafi mentions a second possible meaning: inclination (al-mayl). This is supported by the description of a swaying woman as 'rauda' or 'rud', due to her softness. He then presents two possible priority relations: (1) inclination is the original meaning, but it was used for seeking because a seeker's path is not straight but inclines; (2) seeking is the original, but it was used for inclination because in custom, inclination is done for the sake of seeking something. Ultimately, the author presents these as possibilities, concluding that this is the derivation of the word. The purpose is to clarify the linguistic foundation before discussing the technical meaning in kalam.

الرود (al-rawd)
الطلب (al-talab)
الميل (al-mayl)
الرايد (al-rayid)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think irada only means 'will' in a philosophical sense, disconnected from physical experience.
Correction: The passage shows that the linguistic root of irada is derived from concrete physical actions: seeking and inclination, which are grounded in everyday experience.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Irāda as Specification of Actions
Imam al-Nasafi gives another definition of irāda as the meaning that specifies actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second definition of irāda as the divine attribute that necessitates the specification of actions in particular times, forms, and qualities, and how this is evidenced by the order and variety in creation.

Core Idea
Irāda is the divine specification that makes creation orderly and varied.

Imam al-Nasafi presents another definition of irāda, describing it as the meaning that necessitates the specification of an action in one manner rather than another. This lesson unpacks this definition, its proof from the diversity of creation, and why this attribute is called irāda (will). Understanding this helps grasp how God's will relates to the particularities of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that the passage defines irāda in terms of specification.
2
Identify the proof structure
Find the conditional reasoning: if there were no irāda, all actions would be uniform; because we see variety and order, irāda must exist.
The proof relies on the observation that no time or form is inherently preferable.
3
Connect specification to the term 'irāda'
Understand why this meaning is called irāda: because the agent 'seeks' (talaba) or 'inclines' (māla) to a particular manner for each action.
This shows how the technical term aligns with the linguistic sense of 'will' as desire or choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قيل في تحديد الإرادة: إنها معنى توجب اختصاص المفعول بوجه دون وجه، إذ لولا الإرادة لوقعت المفعولات كلها في وقت واحد على هيئة واحدة وصفة واحدة خصوصا عند تجانس المفعولات، فإذا خرجت على الترادف والتوالي، وعلى النظام والاتساق، وعلى الهيئات المختلفة والصفات المتباينة على حسب ما تقتضيه الحكمة البالغة، كان ذلك دليلا على اتصاف الفاعل بالإرادة، لما كان وقت لوجوده أولى من وقت، ولا هيئة أولى من غيرها، ولا صفة ولا كمية ولا كيفية أولى مما سواها. ثم إن هذا المعنى الذي يحصل به هذه المعاني سمي إرادة، لما أن الفاعل إذا فعل شيئا في وقت وعلى هيئة وصفة فقد طلب هذا الوجه لهذا المفعول دون ما سواه من الوجوه، أو مال إلى تخصيصه بهذا الوجه دون غيره من الوجوه ثم إن المراد بلفظة الإرادة عند المتكلمين هذا المعنى الذي بينا وعرفناه بأثره وهو ما مر من كون الموصوف بها مختارا في فعله، أو خروج مفعولاته على وجه دون
And it has been said regarding the definition of irāda: it is a meaning that necessitates the specification of the effected thing in one manner rather than another. For if there were no irāda, all effected things would occur at one time, in one form, and one quality, especially when the effected things are homogeneous. So when they occur in succession and sequence, in order and consistency, and in various forms and different qualities according to what perfect wisdom requires, that is evidence that the agent is described with irāda, since no time was more deserving for its existence than another time, nor any form more deserving than another, nor any quality, quantity, or modality more deserving than what is other than it. Then this meaning by which these meanings are obtained is called irāda, because when the agent does something at a time and in a form and quality, he has sought this manner for this effected thing to the exclusion of other manners, or inclined to specify it with this manner rather than other manners. Then what is intended by the term irāda according to the theologians is this meaning which we have explained and recognized by its effect, namely what has passed: that the one described with it is choiceful in his action, or that his effected things emerge in one manner rather than another.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage presents another definition of irāda (will) and provides an argument for it. The definition is: irāda is a meaning that necessitates the specification (ikhtiṣāṣ) of an action (mafʿūl) in one particular manner (wajh) rather than another. The proof is: without irāda, all actions would occur at the same time, in the same form and quality, especially when the actions are homogeneous. But in reality, we observe actions occurring in sequence and order, with different forms and qualities, according to perfect wisdom. This variation indicates that the agent possesses irāda, because there is no inherent reason for one time or form to be preferred over another—that preference comes from irāda. The passage then explains why this attribute is called irāda: because when an agent does something at a specific time and in a specific form, he has 'sought' (ṭalaba) that particular manner for that action, or 'inclined' (māla) to specify it. Finally, it states that this is the technical meaning of irāda among the theologians (mutakallimūn), and it is the same as the earlier definition that the one described with irāda is choiceful in his action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Irāda only concerns choice between good and evil, not general specification.
Correction: This passage shows that irāda primarily concerns specification of any action in a particular manner, whether good or neutral.

Checkpoint

A) The existence of evil in the world.
B) The variety and order of actions that are otherwise indifferent.
C) The inability of matter to organize itself.

Key Takeaway
Irāda is the divine attribute that specifies the particular manner of each action, and the observed order and diversity in creation prove its existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every detail of your life—your birthplace, family, talents, trials—was specified by Allah's will, then why do you ever feel entitled to complain about any of it? What does this reveal about your trust in His wisdom?
?
When you consider the order and complexity in your own body, from the sequence of your heartbeat to the timing of your breath, how can you claim that your existence is mere chance? What will you say to your Creator when He asks why you denied His will's fingerprints on your own life?
?
The passage says that without irāda, all things would occur at once. But you live in a world of time and sequence. Every moment of delay or waiting is a sign that Allah is choosing what is best for you. How many times have you cursed a delay instead of seeing it as a token of His specification? Repent.

Linguistic Definitions of Wisdom and Folly in Arabic
Presents four linguistic definitions of hikma and an analysis of safah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various lexicographical definitions of hikma (wisdom) and safah (folly), and how they relate to knowledge, precise action, and self-restraint.

Core Idea
Wisdom in Arabic encompasses knowledge, precise action, and self-restraint; folly is ignorant action without deliberation.

Before theologians debate whether God is wise, we must first understand what 'wisdom' means in the Arabic language. This lesson explores the diverse definitions of hikma and safah given by early philologists, revealing a rich concept that includes not only knowledge but also precise action and moral restraint. The passage also clarifies the opposite of wisdom depending on whether it is viewed as knowledge or action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its structure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the author states the chapter has two sections: one on linguistic meanings, the other on theological meanings. This lesson covers only the first.
The passage is a lexical classification with multiple opinions.
2
Understand the first definition: hikma as knowledge
Ibn al-Aʿrābī defines ḥikma as knowledge (al-ʿilm) and ḥakīm as the knower (al-ʿālim). Note the derivation from ḥukm and ḥākim.
3
Understand the second and third definitions
Other philologists define ḥakīm as 'one who makes things precise' (muḥkim), and some derive it from the bit of a horse, implying self-restraint from whims and evil.
The second definition uses the faʿīl pattern meaning mufʿil.
4
Understand the fourth comprehensive definition
Wisdom is 'knowledge of things by their realities and placing them in their proper places', encompassing both knowledge and action. Its opposite is ignorance if wisdom is knowledge, or folly if wisdom is action.
5
Analyze the term safah (folly)
Safah linguistically means movement and agitation, as illustrated by the verse. A fool (safīh) acts lightly due to joy or anger, without deliberation or reflection on consequences. Any action done ignorantly while ignoring outcomes is called safah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في بيان أن صانع العالم حكيم . يقع الكلام في هذا الباب في موضعين: أحدهما في بيان معنى الحكمة والحكيم في اللغة. والثاني في بيان معنى الحكمة والحكيم والسفه والسفيه على رأي المتكلمين. معنى الحكمة والحكيم في اللغة. أما الكلام في الفصل الأول فنقول : اختلف في ذلك أهل اللغة قال ابن الأعرابي: الحكمة العلم والحكيم العالم يقال : حكم الرجل بحكم إذ تناهى في علمه وعقله، القاضي حكما ، وحاكما لعلمه وعقله . وقال آخرون: الحكيم هو المحكم للشيء، وهو فعيل بمعنى مفعل كأليم بمعنى مؤلم، وسميع بمعنى مسمع. وقال بعضهم: الحكيم هو الذي يمنع نفسه عن هواها أو عن القبائح مأخوذ من حكمة الفرس والحكمة سميت بذلك لأنها ترد من غربة فرس. وقيل: الحكمة معرفة الأشياء بحقائقها ووضعها مواضعها. فكانت شاملة على العلم والفعل جميعا، فمن جعل الحكمة علما جعل ضدها الجهل. ومن جعلها الفعل، جعل ضدها السفه، وهو في حقيقة اللغة: تحرك واضطراب يصيب الشيء قال ذو الرمة: جرين كما اهتزت رماح تسفهت أعاليها من الرياح النواسم أي حركت ويسمى الرجل سفيها لما تعتريه خفة إما من الفرح وإما من الغضب، فتبعثه على فعل من غير روية ولا عرض على العقل ليتأمل في العاقبة. وسمي كل فعل كان عملا بالجهل مع الإعراض عن النظر في العواقب ليوقف على الحميدة منها والوخيمة سفها. هذا هو الكلام فيها من حيث اللغة.
The discourse concerning the proof that the Maker of the world is Wise. The discourse in this chapter occurs in two places: one is concerning the explanation of the meaning of wisdom (al-ḥikma) and the wise (al-ḥakīm) in language; the second is concerning the explanation of the meaning of wisdom, the wise, folly (al-safah) and the fool (al-safīh) according to the opinion of the theologians. The meaning of wisdom and the wise in language. As for the discourse in the first section, we say: The philologists differed concerning that. Ibn al-A‘rābī said: Wisdom is knowledge, and the wise is the knower. It is said: the man ruled (ḥakuma) with judgment (ḥukm) when he reached perfection in his knowledge and intellect, so the judge is called ḥakam and ḥākim due to his knowledge and intellect. Others said: The wise (al-ḥakīm) is the one who makes things precise (al-muḥkim), and it is fa‘īl in the sense of muf‘il, like alīm in the sense of mu’lim, and samī‘ in the sense of musmi‘. Some said: The wise is the one who prevents his soul from its whims or from ugly deeds, derived from the bit of the horse (ḥikmat al-faras); and wisdom is so named because it restrains from the error of the horse. And it is said: Wisdom is knowledge of things by their realities and placing them in their proper places. So it encompasses knowledge and action together. So whoever made wisdom knowledge made its opposite ignorance; and whoever made it action made its opposite folly. And folly (al-safah) in the true linguistic sense is movement and agitation that affects a thing. Dhū al-Rumma said: 'They ran as lances shaken, their tops ruffled by gentle winds' — meaning they moved. And a man is called a fool (safīh) because of a lightness that afflicts him, either from joy or from anger, and it impels him to act without deliberation or reference to the intellect to reflect on the outcome. And every action that is done ignorantly while turning away from considering outcomes to know the praiseworthy and the harmful is called folly. This is the discourse concerning them from the linguistic perspective.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage is a lexical classification of the terms 'wisdom' (ḥikma) and 'folly' (safah). The author states that the chapter has two sections: one on linguistic meanings, the other on theological meanings. In the linguistic section, he presents four opinions. First, Ibn al-A‘rābī defines ḥikma as knowledge (al-‘ilm) and ḥakīm as the knower (al-‘ālim), citing the derivation of ḥukm and ḥākim. Second, others define ḥakīm as muf‘il (one who makes something precise), with examples like alīm (painful) and samī‘ (making hear). Third, some derive it from the bit of a horse (ḥikmat al-faras), describing the wise person as one who restrains himself from whims and evil. Fourth, a comprehensive definition: ḥikma is knowing things by their true realities and placing them in correct contexts. The passage then notes that ḥikma encompasses both knowledge and action, so its opposite can be ignorance (jahl) if it is knowledge, or folly (safah) if it is action. The term safah is linguistically analyzed as movement and agitation, illustrated by a verse of Dhū al-Rumma. A fool (safīh) is one who acts lightly due to joy or anger, without deliberation or rational consideration of consequences. Thus, any action done ignorantly while ignoring outcomes is called safah. This concludes the linguistic discussion.

Correction: The passage shows that ḥikma also includes precise action and self-restraint, and can have opposite terms depending on whether it is considered knowledge or action.

Key Takeaway
The Arabic term ḥikma has multiple nuanced meanings in classical lexicography, covering knowledge, precision, and moral restraint, while safah denotes ignorant action without deliberation.

Lexical Definitions of Hikmah and Safah
The author gives lexical definitions of ḥikmah and safah with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical meanings of ḥikmah and safah and how they relate as opposites depending on whether wisdom is considered as knowledge or action.

Core Idea
Wisdom restrains like a bridle; foolishness is impulsive action without deliberation.

This lesson establishes the linguistic foundations for two key terms in Islamic theology: ḥikmah (wisdom) and safah (foolishness). Before diving into technical theological discussions, the author clarifies their root meanings in Arabic, including a poetic example. Understanding these lexical definitions helps grasp the later theological usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning of the definitions of ḥikmah and safah.

2
Identify the two definitions of ḥikmah
The passage gives two main definitions of ḥikmah: one as knowledge of things by their realities, and another that encompasses both knowledge and action. Note how each leads to a different opposite (ignorance vs. foolishness).
This distinction is crucial for later theological discussions.
3
Understand the definition of safah
Safah is defined linguistically as movement and agitation, and then applied to a person who acts without deliberation. Recognize that safah becomes the opposite of ḥikmah when wisdom is viewed as action.
The poetic example by Dhul-Rummah illustrates the root meaning of movement.

Some say: The wise (al-ḥakīm) is the one who prevents his soul from its desires or from ugly deeds, derived from the bridle (ḥikma) of a horse; wisdom (al-ḥikma) is named thus because it restrains from the error of a horse. And it is said: wisdom is knowledge of things according to their realities and placing them in their proper places. So it encompassed both knowledge and action. Whoever made wisdom knowledge made its opposite ignorance. And whoever made it action made its opposite foolishness (al-safah). And in the true meaning of language, it is: movement and agitation that afflicts something. Dhul-Rummah said: 'They ran as if shaken by spears whose tops are agitated by the gentle winds' – meaning, moved. And a man is called a fool (safīh) because of the lightness that befalls him, either from joy or from anger, driving him to act without deliberation and without presenting to the intellect to ponder the outcome. And every deed that is an act of ignorance with disregard for considering outcomes to distinguish praiseworthy from harmful is called foolishness. This is the discussion about them from the perspective of language.

Commentary
This passage presents lexical definitions of ḥikmah (wisdom) and safah (foolishness) as a foundation for the theological discussion. The author first notes that some define the ḥakīm (wise person) as one who restrains himself from desires and ugly acts, deriving the term from the horse's bridle (ḥikmat al-faras) which restrains. Two main definitions of ḥikmah are given: (1) knowledge of things by their realities and placing them correctly, and (2) that it encompasses both knowledge and action. Consequently, the opposite of ḥikmah depends on which aspect is emphasized: if ḥikmah is knowledge, its opposite is ignorance; if action, its opposite is safah. The author then defines safah linguistically as movement and agitation, citing a poetic verse from Dhul-Rummah. This meaning leads to describing a safīh as someone prone to lightness from joy or anger, acting without deliberation or reflection on consequences. Any act characterized by ignorance and disregard for outcomes is termed safah. The passage concludes as a purely linguistic discussion before moving to technical theological usage.

Definitions of Hikmah and Safah in Kalam
The author enumerates three definitions of wisdom and foolishness from the major kalam schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three major definitions of wisdom and foolishness in Islamic theology and how they reflect different theological methodologies.

Core Idea
Wisdom and foolishness are defined differently by Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, and Maturidi schools, revealing their core principles.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents the definitions of hikmah (wisdom) and safah (foolishness) as understood by the three main theological schools: the Ash'aris, the Mu'tazila, and the Maturidis. These definitions are foundational for discussions about God's actions and justice (ta'dil and tajwir). Understanding them helps clarify the broader debates in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a feel for the three definitions.
Notice the structure: first Ash'ari, then Mu'tazila, then Maturidi.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the terms hikmah, safah, qasd (intention), naf' (benefit), and aqibah hamidah (praiseworthy outcome).
These terms are central to each definition.
3
Compare the definitions
Contrast the three definitions: Ash'ari focuses on intention, Mu'tazila on benefit, Maturidi on outcome.
Think about what each school emphasizes about God's actions.
4
Reflect on the broader context
The author mentions that these definitions relate to ta'dil and tajwir, which are big topics. Consider why.
This passage lays groundwork for later discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكمة والسفه على رأي المتكلمين (4) (فزعم الأشعري ) ( ) ( أن الحكمة) (6) في الفعل وقوعه على قصد فاعله والسفه وقوعه على خلاف قصد فاعله. وقالت المعتزلة الحكمة كل فعل فيه نفع إما للفاعل وإما لغير الفاعل والسفه كل فعل خلا عن المنفعة إما للفاعل وإما لغير الفاعل. وعندنا الحكمة ما له عاقبة حميدة، والسفه ما خلا عن العاقبة الحميدة، والكلام في بيان الصحيح من هذه الأقاويل والفاسد منها يطول جدا إذ له شعب كثيرة وفروع جمة، والكلام في مسائل التعديل والتجوير يدور على ذلك. ولا يحتمل كتابنا هذا - مع شرطنا الإيجاز - في التكلم في ذلك، ونذكر طرفا منه إذا انتهينا إلى مسائل التجوير والتعديل - إن شاء الله تعالى - فأما الإشباع فيه والمبالغة فقد وجدا في تصنيف لي في ذلك مفرد لم أتفرغ بعد لإتمامه، وأنا أسأل الله تعالى أن يوفقني لذلك بعد فراغي من هذا الكتاب (1).
And wisdom (al-ḥikmah) and foolishness (al-safah) according to the opinion of the theologians (al-mutakallimūn): Al-Ashʿarī claimed that wisdom in an action is its occurrence according to the intention of its doer, and foolishness is its occurrence contrary to the intention of its doer. The Muʿtazila said: Wisdom is every action that contains benefit, either for the doer or for someone else, and foolishness is every action devoid of benefit, either for the doer or for someone else. And according to us (the Māturīdīs): Wisdom is that which has a praiseworthy outcome, and foolishness is that which is devoid of a praiseworthy outcome. And the discussion about clarifying the correct and incorrect among these statements is very lengthy, for it has many branches and numerous offshoots, and discourse on issues of declaring actions just or unjust (al-taʿdīl wa-l-tajwīr) revolves around this. And this book of ours, with our condition of brevity, cannot accommodate discussing that. We will mention some of it when we reach the issues of al-tajwīr and al-taʿdīl, God willing. As for fullness and exaggeration in it, they are found in a separate composition of mine on that topic which I have not yet found time to complete, and I ask God Almighty to grant me success in that after finishing this book.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage presents the definitions of ḥikmah and safah as understood by the major theological schools (mutakallimūn). It is a comparative classification: First, Abūl-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī defines wisdom in action as occurring according to the doer's intention (qaṣd), and foolishness as occurring contrary to that intention. Second, the Muʿtazila define wisdom as any action that yields benefit (nafʿ) either to the doer or another, and foolishness as action devoid of benefit. Third, the author states the Māturīdī position: wisdom is that which has a praiseworthy outcome (ʿāqibah ḥamīdah), and foolishness lacks such an outcome. The author then notes that evaluating these positions is lengthy and relates to broader issues of taʿdīl and tajwīr (declaring God's actions just or unjust). Because this book aims for brevity, the author will only touch upon this later when discussing those issues. He also mentions a separate work he intends to write on the subject, asking God's help.

Correction: The Ash'ari definition actually focuses solely on the conformity of the action to the doer's intention, not on benefit or outcome.

Refutation of Conditions for Vision of Accidents
Refutation of Mu'tazili conditions for vision as applied to accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conditions for vision proposed by the Mu'tazila (distance, ray, impression, removal of veils) do not apply to the vision of accidents, and how the author argues this from their own premises.

Core Idea
Accidents are seen without the physical conditions required for seeing bodies.

The Mu'tazila held that vision requires certain physical conditions such as distance, a ray connecting the seer and seen, impression of the seen in the eye, and removal of veils. The author refutes these by noting that accidents are seen without these conditions, even according to the Mu'tazila themselves. This passage shows the internal inconsistency of the Mu'tazili position and establishes that vision of Allah need not meet such conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
Notice the repetitive structure: each condition is introduced and then refuted.
2
Identify the four conditions
Find the four conditions the author lists: distance, ray connection, impression, removal of veils. Also note the condition of being spatial.
These are the conditions the Mu'tazila considered necessary for vision.
3
Understand the key argument
The author's main point is that the Mu'tazila themselves concede that accidents are seen. Therefore, these conditions cannot be necessary for all vision.
This is a powerful internal critique.
4
Examine each refutation
Look at how the author refutes each condition: ray connection is inconceivable for accidents, impression is impossible because accidents have no image, removal of veils only applies to bodies.
Each refutation is specific to the nature of accidents.
5
Reflect on theological implication
Consider that if these conditions are not necessary for seeing accidents, they cannot be used to argue against seeing Allah, who is not a body.
This supports the Maturidi position that Allah can be seen in the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجعلهم المسافة بين الرائي والمرئي واتصال الشعاع وانطباع المرئي في عين الرائي وارتفاع الحجب شرطا للرؤية فاسد، وكذا اشتراطهم كون المرئي متحيزا فاسد، لأنهم سلموا لنا رؤية الأعراض، ومن لم يسلم منهم ذلك ألزمناه بالدليل، واتصال الشعاع بالأعراض غير متصور ، وكذا إدراكها بالمماسة على ما قررنا. ومن مذهب النظام أن الشعاع جسم يماس المرئي والعرض لا يماس ومع دل أن هذا باطل وكذا انطباع المرئي، لأن المرئي لا ينطبع ولكن ذلك يرى يخلق الله تعالى مثل صورة المرئي في المرآة، ولا صورة للأعراض. وكذا ارتفاع الحجب تكون فيما بين الأجسام، فأما في حق الأعراض فلا يقال: ارتفعت الحجب إلا أن ترتفع عن محلها. وقد بينا قبل هذا أن ارتفاع الحجب عن محالها لا أثر له في حقها، على أنا بينا أن الحجاب لا أثر له في المنع عن الرؤية فلم يكن لارتفاعه أثر في إثبات الرؤية.
Their making distance between the seer and the seen, connection of the ray, impression of the seen in the eye of the seer, and removal of the veils a condition for vision is invalid. Likewise their conditioning that the seen be spatial is invalid, because they conceded to us the vision of accidents; and whoever among them does not concede that, we compel him by proof. Connection of the ray to accidents is inconceivable, and likewise their perception by touch as we have established. And from the doctrine of al-Nazzam is that the ray is a body that touches the seen, and the accident does not touch, and with that it indicates that this is false. Likewise the impression of the seen, because the seen does not imprint; but that vision, Allah the Exalted creates the like of the image of the seen in the mirror, and there is no image for accidents. Likewise the removal of veils occurs between bodies; as for accidents, it cannot be said that the veils are removed unless they are removed from their locus. And we have shown before this that removal of veils from their loci has no effect with respect to them, and we have shown that the veil has no effect in preventing vision, so its removal has no effect in establishing vision.
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazili conditions for vision (distance, ray connection, impression, removal of veils) by showing they only apply to bodies, while accidents are seen without them. The author first states that these conditions are invalid, then argues that even the Mu'tazila concede that accidents are seen, and if not, they are compelled by proof. He then refutes al-Nazzam's view that the ray is a body that touches, noting that accidents are not touched. The impression of the seen is impossible because God creates a likeness in the mirror, but accidents have no image. Finally, removal of veils only makes sense for bodies, not accidents, and the veil itself does not prevent vision, so its removal does not cause vision.

Key Takeaway

Challenge to the Analogy of Foolishness
Imam Nasafi challenges the opponents' analogy of foolishness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi dialectically challenges the opponent's analogy from the witnessed world to God's actions, exposing the flaw in assuming that what is foolish in humans is necessarily foolish for God.

Core Idea
An analogy fails if it assumes the same rule applies across all agents and contexts.

This passage captures a classic kalam dialectical move: the opponent claims God's action is 'foolish' based on analogy with human foolishness. But Imam Nasafi responds not by denying the analogy outright, but by questioning whether the property 'foolish' transfers universally across all agents and all contexts. This forces the opponent to either abandon the analogy or prove its universal applicability.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialectical exchange
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the back-and-forth argument. Notice that it begins with the opponents' claim and then a response with two questions.
2
Identify the two conditional questions
Find the two key questions posed to the opponents. The first asks about universality across worlds, the second about universality across agents.
3
Understand the dilemma
See how the two possible answers ('yes' or 'no') lead to either invalidation of the analogy or a demand for proof. This is a classic logical trap.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : لم قلتم : إن هذا سفه؟ فإن قالوا: هذا في الشاهد سفه فكذا يكون في الغائب سفها. قيل : أكل ما كان في الشاهد من فاعل سفها، كان في الغائب سفها؟ وكذا ما كان من فاعل سفها أكان من غيره سفها؟ فإن قالوا: لا أبطلوا دليلهم، وإن قالوا: نعم، طولبوا بالدليل.
It was said to them: Why did you say that this is foolishness? If they say: This in the witnessed is foolishness, so likewise it is in the absent foolishness. It was said: Is everything that was in the witnessed from an agent foolishness, also in the absent foolishness? And likewise, if something from an agent is foolishness, is it also from another foolishness? If they say no, they have invalidated their evidence, and if they say yes, they are asked for proof.
Source pages p. 673

Commentary
This passage presents an initial objection and response in a dialectical argument. It begins with the opponents' claim that a particular action of God is foolish (safah) based on an analogy (qiyas) from the witnessed world (shahid) to the absent world (gha'ib). The responder challenges this analogy by asking two questions: (1) Is every act that is foolish in the witnessed world also foolish in the absent world? (2) Is an act that is foolish from one agent also foolish from another agent? These questions force the opponents into a dilemma: if they answer 'no', they have invalidated their own analogy; if they answer 'yes', they must provide proof that this universal transfer holds. The argument thus exposes the weakness of the initial claim.

Key Terms
سفه
شاهد
غائب
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The responder concedes that God could be described as foolish.
Correction: The responder does not concede the attribute; he only challenges the logical validity of the analogy as a method.

Checkpoint
What is the main challenge posed to the opponents in this passage?
A) They must define 'foolishness' clearly.
B) They must show that every foolish act in the witnessed world is also foolish in the absent world and from any agent.
C) They must prove that God's actions resemble human actions.
D) They must admit that God cannot be described as foolish.

Key Takeaway
The analogy from the witnessed to the absent is not automatically valid; the opponent must demonstrate that the property in question transfers universally across worlds and agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself questioning God's decisions by comparing them to human standards—like thinking a tragedy is 'unfair' or a blessing is 'undeserved'—which part of this argument calls you to stop and reconsider the validity of your analogy? How can you train your heart to submit to divine wisdom even when it seems foolish by human logic?
?
This lesson exposes the danger of assuming that our limited understanding of 'foolishness' applies to God. In what area of your life have you unconsciously made God's actions conform to your expectations? Will you commit today to examine that assumption and ask God to give you trust beyond reasoning?
?
The responder shifts the burden of proof onto the opponents. When you are quick to judge God's decree, do you realize that you are the one who needs to prove your analogy? How can you turn your complaint into a moment of humble silence and reliance on His perfect wisdom?

The Five Categories of Impossibility
Al-Nasafi enumerates the five forms of impossibility (mustahilat).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories of impossibility in kalam and how they are used to exclude certain logically and physically impossible claims about God.

Core Idea
Kalam defines five forms of impossibility to distinguish what God cannot do.

Al-Nasafi begins his discussion of the attributes of God by establishing what is impossible for Him. This classification is crucial because it sets the boundaries of rational discourse about the divine. Before we can affirm what is necessary for God, we must first understand what cannot be attributed to Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: each category is introduced with 'one of them is...'.
Pay attention to the examples given for each category.
2
Identify the Five Categories
List the five categories of impossibility as described by Al-Nasafi. For each, note the defining characteristic and the example provided.
The categories are: logical impossibility, mutual exclusion, incapacity of claimed agent, agent's deficiency, and lack of knowledge.
3
Reflect on Theological Purpose
Consider why this classification is important in kalam. How does it protect the concept of God from inappropriate attributions?
This classification prevents attributing contradictions or absurdities to God.
4
Test Your Understanding
Try to think of another example for each category. Could you apply this classification to a modern claim?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صور المستحيلات: أحدها: أن ذلك الأمر الذي أخطر بالبال وجوده يكون مستحيل الثبوت ممتنع الوجود كوجود الشريك والصاحبة والولد للصانع. والثاني: أن يكون شيء ما مانعا من وجوده وثبوته كوجود البياض في محل قام به السواد وكذا غيرهما من الأضداد المتعاقبة. والثالث: أن يكون ذلك الأمر عظيمًا في نفسه لفوت قدرة من يدعي إيجاده كالإحياء والإماتة من جهة العباد، والإتيان بالشمس من المغرب على ما حاج إبراهيم عليه السلام اللعين وبهته وأفحمه. والرابع: أن يكون الخلل في جهة الفاعل بأن كان ممن يتعذر عليه نوع ذلك الفعل. والخامس: أن يكون ذلك الأمر أمرًا محكما متقنا في نفسه فلا يتصور حصوله ممن لا علم لربه.
Forms of impossibilities: First: that the matter whose existence occurs to the mind is impossible of affirmation, impossible of existence, like the existence of a partner, consort, or child for the Maker. Second: that something prevents its existence and affirmation, like the existence of whiteness in a place where blackness has occurred, and likewise other mutually exclusive opposites. Third: that the matter is great in itself due to the incapacity of the one who claims to bring it about, like giving life and causing death on the part of servants, and bringing the sun from the west, as in the argument of Ibrahim, peace be upon him, with the accursed one who was confounded and silenced. Fourth: that the deficiency is in the agent, that he is one from whom that kind of act is impossible. Fifth: that the matter is firmly established and perfected in itself, so its occurrence from one who has no knowledge of it is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 759

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration: Al-Nasafi defines the five categories of impossible things (mustahilat). He begins with a headline ('Forms of impossibilities'), then lists each type with an example. The first is logical impossibility, such as God having a partner, consort, or child. The second is physical impossibility due to the mutual exclusion of opposites, like whiteness and blackness in the same place. The third relates to the incapacity of the agent, giving examples of miracles that only God can perform, such as giving life and death or bringing the sun from the west (alluding to Ibrahim's debate with Nimrod). The fourth is inability due to the agent's own deficiency in performing that kind of act. The fifth is the impossibility of a well-ordered, perfect act coming from one who lacks knowledge. This classification is foundational in kalam for determining what is possible or impossible for God.

Key Terms
مستحيل
ممتنع
أضداد متعاقبة
فوت قدرة
محكم متقن
صانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can do absolutely anything, including logical contradictions.
Correction: Kalam maintains that certain things are inherently impossible (e.g., a partner for God) and are not within the realm of divine power.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the first category of impossibility?
A) Bringing the sun from the west
B) Existence of a partner for God
C) Whiteness in a black place
D) Giving life to the dead

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's five categories of impossibility provide a framework for understanding what cannot be attributed to God, safeguarding divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about what is impossible for God, do you ever unconsciously limit God by imagining He could do things that contradict His perfection, like having a child or a partner? What does that reveal about how you perceive His oneness?
?
In the story of Ibrahim and Nimrod, Ibrahim used the impossibility of bringing the sun from the west to silence his opponent. How often do you rely on such clear rational proofs to defend your faith, and why might that be important in your own conversations?
?
The fifth category says a perfectly ordered act cannot come from someone without knowledge. Look at the order in your own life—your body, your circumstances. Are you living as if that order points to a Knowing Creator, or are you attributing it to chance? What would change if you truly internalized that every detail reflects God's perfect knowledge?

The Jabriyya Denial of Real Human Action
Al-Nasafi presents the Jabriyya denial of real human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jabriyya position, as articulated by Jahm ibn Safwan, that humans have no real action and actions are only attributed to them as loci.

Core Idea
The Jabriyya reduce human action to mere location, not agency.

This lesson introduces the foundational issue of the creation of human acts and the first extreme position: the Jabriyya. We see how Jahm ibn Safwan denied any real human act, setting the stage for the broader debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening framing
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that al-Nasafi presents this issue as foundational for questions of divine attributes and justice.
The phrase 'الأصل في هذه المسائل' signals its importance.
2
Analyze Jahm's claim
Jahm states that no real action belongs to the servant; actions are attributed to humans only as locations (محال), not as originators. Understand the analogy of attributing things to their loci.
3
Identify the Jabriyya sect
The last sentence tells us that Jahm's companions, the Jabriyya, support this view. Note that 'Jabriyya' comes from 'jabr' (compulsion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل في هذه المسائل مسألة خلق أفعال العباد وهي مبنية على ما يتعلق بصفات الله تعالى ويدخل تحتها على ما نبين. واختلف الناس فيها فزعم جهم بن صفوان أن لا فعل للعبد في الحقيقة وما يضاف إليهم يضاف على حسب إضافة الأشياء إلى محالها دون إضافتها إلى محصلها، وساعده على هذه المقالة أصحابه وهم الجبرية. وقال من سواهم من فرق الأمة بأن العبد مكتسب على الحقيقة وله فعل إلا الأشعري، فإنه زعم أن العبد يسمى مكتسبا عاملا ولا يسمى فاعلا، والفاعل في الحقيقة هو الله تعالى. ثم القائلون بأن العبد له فعل وكسب وعمل اختلفوا من موجد أفعال العباد فزعمت القدرية أن موجدها على الحقيقة هو العبد ولا صنع الله تعالى ولا تصرف في فعل العبد البتة بوجه من الوجوه، غير أن أوائلهم كانوا لا يتجاسرون على إطلاق اسم الخالق على العباد، وكانوا يقولون إنه موجد فعله ومحدثه ولا يقولون خالقه، وكانوا يساعدون أهل الحق على أن لا خالق إلا الله تعالى إلى أن نشأ بينهم الجبائي فرأى أن لا فرق بين اسم الخالق والموجد فزعم أن كل ما دب ودرج خالق لفعله الاختياري، فلما انتهت نوبة رياستهم إلى أبي عبد الله البصري الملقب بجعل زعم أن الخالق على الحقيقة هو العبد والله تعالى يسمى خالقا على مجاز القول دون الحقيقة. وقال أهل الحق إن موجدها وخالقها على الحقيقة هو الله تعالى والعبد مكتسب له فاعل له، وساعدهم عليه متكلمو أهل الحديث والنجارية.
The foundation in these matters is the issue of the creation of the acts of servants, and it is built on what relates to the attributes of God Almighty, and it includes what we will explain. And people differed concerning it: Jahm ibn Safwan claimed that there is no real act for the servant, and what is attributed to them is attributed in the manner of attributing things to their loci, not attributing them to their originator. And his companions, who are the Jabriyya, supported him in this opinion. And those other than him among the sects of the community said that the servant is truly an acquirer and has an act, except al-Ash'ari, for he claimed that the servant is called an acquirer and a doer but not an agent, and the agent in reality is God Almighty. Then those who say that the servant has an act, acquisition, and work differed over who originates the acts of the servants: the Qadariyya claimed that their originator in reality is the servant, and God has no making or disposal in the servant's act at all in any way, except that their early ones did not dare to apply the name creator to servants, and they used to say he is the originator of his act and its innovator, and they did not say creator of it, and they were assisting the people of truth that there is no creator but God Almighty, until al-Jubba'i arose among them and saw no difference between the name creator and originator, so he claimed that everything that creeps and walks is the creator of its voluntary act. Then when the turn of their leadership reached Abu Abd Allah al-Basri, nicknamed Ju'ul, he claimed that the creator in reality is the servant, and God Almighty is called a creator in a metaphorical sense, not in reality. And the people of truth said that its originator and creator in reality is God Almighty, and the servant is an acquirer of it, an actor of it, and they were supported by the mutakallimun of the Ahl al-Hadith and the Najjariyya.
Source pages p. 784

Commentary
This passage presents a survey of the major theological positions on the creation of human actions (khalaq af‘āl al-‘ibād). The author begins by stating that this is the foundational issue for questions of divine justice and injustice. He then lists the positions in order:
1. Jahm ibn Safwan and the Jabriyya: They deny any real human action; actions are merely attributed to humans as locations, not as agents.
2. The majority of sects affirm that the servant has an act and is a true acquirer (muktasib), except al-Ash'ari who says the servant is called an acquirer and a doer but not an agent (fa‘il); the true agent is God.
3. The Qadariyya: They assert that the servant alone originates his own actions; God has no involvement. The early Qadariyya hesitated to call man 'creator', but later al-Jubba’i and Abu Abd Allah al-Basri explicitly claimed man is the true creator.
4. The people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq): They maintain that God is the sole originator and creator of actions, while the servant is the acquirer and actor (muktasib, fa‘il). This position is endorsed by the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith and the Najjariyya. The author presents the spectrum without explicit favoritism, but the structure implies the Ahl al-Haqq position as the correct one.

Key Terms
خلق أفعال العباد
جبرية
جهم بن صفوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if humans have no real action, then they are not morally responsible.
Correction: The passage does not directly address moral responsibility, but the Sunni view (not presented here) affirms human acquisition as the basis for accountability, even while denying that humans create their own actions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does Jahm ibn Safwan describe the attribution of actions to humans?
A) Humans are the true originators of their actions.
B) Actions are attributed to humans as loci, not as originators.
C) Humans acquire actions while God creates them.
D) Humans are agents in a metaphorical sense.

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya, led by Jahm ibn Safwan, deny any real human action, viewing humans only as locations for God's creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a mistake or sin, how often do you find yourself internally shifting blame to circumstances or even to God's decree? The Jabriyya made that a theology. But look at your life: in the past week, can you name one moment where you truly felt like a passive 'locus' for an action you now regret? Write down that moment. Now, alongside it, write the truth: you are an agent responsible before God. Commit to seeking forgiveness with full ownership.
?
If you were to fully adopt the Jabriyya view for just one day, how would your worship change? Would you still exert effort in salah, or would you rationalize laziness as God's will? Be honest: does the idea of being a mere location appeal to you because it relieves you of the burden of striving? This lesson shows that the Jabriyya are wrong, but their appeal is real. What in your heart still craves that escape from responsibility? Pray to Allah to strengthen your sense of agency for the sake of your afterlife.

The Ash'ari Position on Acquisition and Agency
Al-Nasafi presents al-Ash'ari's unique terminology for human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's nuanced terminology: the servant is an acquirer (muktasib) and a doer ('āmil), but God alone is the true agent (fā'il).

Core Idea
Ash'ari affirms human acquisition but reserves true agency for God.

After the Jabriyya denial, we now encounter the majority view that humans truly acquire actions. Al-Ash'ari introduces a subtle distinction: the servant can be called an acquirer and a doer, but not an agent in the absolute sense. This maintains human responsibility while upholding divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the majority affirmation
Notice that most sects (من سواهم) affirm that the servant is a true acquirer (مكتسب على الحقيقة) and has an act. This is in contrast to the Jabriyya.
2
Identify al-Ash'ari's exception
Al-Ash'ari agrees that the servant is called an acquirer and a doer, but he refuses to call the servant an agent (فاعل). Understand the difference.
The Arabic term for 'agent' here is فاعل, which implies origination.
3
Reflect on the terminology
Why does Ash'ari maintain this distinction? It safeguards God's exclusive role as the originator of actions while giving humans a real role as acquirers. This becomes the basis for the concept of kasb (acquisition).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you truly believe that every action you 'do' is ultimately created by God? When you succeed or fail, whose agency do you credit or blame? What would change if you consistently acknowledged that God alone is the true agent?
?
Al-Ash'ari calls the servant a 'doer' but not an 'agent'. Does this distinction feel like a mental trick, or does it free you from the burden of being the ultimate cause of your deeds? How might embracing this view impact your reliance on God and your sense of responsibility?
?
If God is the only real agent, then your choices—good or bad—are still yours as an acquirer. Do you use this understanding to excuse yourself or to humbly seek God's help? Where in your life do you need to stop acting as if you are independent and instead practice constant dependence on Him?

The Qadariyya and the People of Truth on Action Origination
Al-Nasafi contrasts the Qadariyya and Ahl al-Haqq on the origin of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qadariyya claim that humans originate their own actions and the Ahl al-Haqq position that God is the sole originator.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya elevates human action to absolute creation, while Ahl al-Haqq affirms God's sole creative power.

This lesson covers the second major split: among those who affirm human action, some (the Qadariyya) claimed the servant alone originates acts, eventually declaring humans true creators. The people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) countered that God is the sole creator, with the servant as acquirer and actor. We also trace the historical development of Qadariyya thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the Qadariyya initial claim
The Qadariyya assert that the servant is the real originator of his actions, and God has no part in it. Note the strong language: 'لا صنع الله تعالى ولا تصرف'.
2
Trace the historical development
The early Qadariyya hesitated to call man 'creator', but al-Jubba'i equated 'originator' and 'creator', and Abu Abd Allah al-Basri (Ju'ul) explicitly said man is the real creator and God metaphorically. Observe the escalation.
Identify the three stages: early Qadariyya, al-Jubba'i, and al-Basri.
3
Contrast with Ahl al-Haqq
The people of truth state that God is the true originator and creator, while the servant is an acquirer and actor. Notice the terminology: 'خالق', 'موجد', 'مكتسب', 'فاعل'.
4
Note the supporters
Ahl al-Haqq were supported by the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith and the Najjariyya. This shows the broad acceptance of this middle position.
Najjariyya are followers of al-Najjar, a Mu'tazili-influenced group that agreed on kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل في هذه المسائل مسألة خلق أفعال العباد وهي مبنية على ما يتعلق بصفات الله تعالى ويدخل تحتها على ما نبين. واختلف الناس فيها فزعم جهم بن صفوان أن لا فعل للعبد في الحقيقة وما يضاف إليهم يضاف على حسب إضافة الأشياء إلى محالها دون إضافتها إلى محصلها، وساعده على هذه المقالة أصحابه وهم الجبرية. وقال من سواهم من فرق الأمة بأن العبد مكتسب على الحقيقة وله فعل إلا الأشعري، فإنه زعم أن العبد يسمى مكتسبا عاملا ولا يسمى فاعلا، والفاعل في الحقيقة هو الله تعالى. ثم القائلون بأن العبد له فعل وكسب وعمل اختلفوا من موجد أفعال العباد فزعمت القدرية أن موجدها على الحقيقة هو العبد ولا صنع الله تعالى ولا تصرف في فعل العبد البتة بوجه من الوجوه، غير أن أوائلهم كانوا لا يتجاسرون على إطلاق اسم الخالق على العباد، وكانوا يقولون إنه موجد فعله ومحدثه ولا يقولون خالقه، وكانوا يساعدون أهل الحق على أن لا خالق إلا الله تعالى إلى أن نشأ بينهم الجبائي فرأى أن لا فرق بين اسم الخالق والموجد فزعم أن كل ما دب ودرج خالق لفعله الاختياري، فلما انتهت نوبة رياستهم إلى أبي عبد الله البصري الملقب بجعل زعم أن الخالق على الحقيقة هو العبد والله تعالى يسمى خالقا على مجاز القول دون الحقيقة. وقال أهل الحق إن موجدها وخالقها على الحقيقة هو الله تعالى والعبد مكتسب له فاعل له، وساعدهم عليه متكلمو أهل الحديث والنجارية.
The foundation in these matters is the issue of the creation of the acts of servants, and it is built on what relates to the attributes of God Almighty, and it includes what we will explain. And people differed concerning it: Jahm ibn Safwan claimed that there is no real act for the servant, and what is attributed to them is attributed in the manner of attributing things to their loci, not attributing them to their originator. And his companions, who are the Jabriyya, supported him in this opinion. And those other than him among the sects of the community said that the servant is truly an acquirer and has an act, except al-Ash'ari, for he claimed that the servant is called an acquirer and a doer but not an agent, and the agent in reality is God Almighty. Then those who say that the servant has an act, acquisition, and work differed over who originates the acts of the servants: the Qadariyya claimed that their originator in reality is the servant, and God has no making or disposal in the servant's act at all in any way, except that their early ones did not dare to apply the name creator to servants, and they used to say he is the originator of his act and its innovator, and they did not say creator of it, and they were assisting the people of truth that there is no creator but God Almighty, until al-Jubba'i arose among them and saw no difference between the name creator and originator, so he claimed that everything that creeps and walks is the creator of its voluntary act. Then when the turn of their leadership reached Abu Abd Allah al-Basri, nicknamed Ju'ul, he claimed that the creator in reality is the servant, and God Almighty is called a creator in a metaphorical sense, not in reality. And the people of truth said that its originator and creator in reality is God Almighty, and the servant is an acquirer of it, an actor of it, and they were supported by the mutakallimun of the Ahl al-Hadith and the Najjariyya.
Source pages p. 784

Commentary
This passage presents a survey of the major theological positions on the creation of human actions (khalaq af‘āl al-‘ibād). The author begins by stating that this is the foundational issue for questions of divine justice and injustice. He then lists the positions in order:
1. Jahm ibn Safwan and the Jabriyya: They deny any real human action; actions are merely attributed to humans as locations, not as agents.
2. The majority of sects affirm that the servant has an act and is a true acquirer (muktasib), except al-Ash'ari who says the servant is called an acquirer and a doer but not an agent (fa‘il); the true agent is God.
3. The Qadariyya: They assert that the servant alone originates his own actions; God has no involvement. The early Qadariyya hesitated to call man 'creator', but later al-Jubba’i and Abu Abd Allah al-Basri explicitly claimed man is the true creator.
4. The people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq): They maintain that God is the sole originator and creator of actions, while the servant is the acquirer and actor (muktasib, fa‘il). This position is endorsed by the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith and the Najjariyya. The author presents the spectrum without explicit favoritism, but the structure implies the Ahl al-Haqq position as the correct one.

Misconception: Some think the Qadariyya always held the extremist view that man is the creator.
Correction: The passage shows that early Qadariyya were more moderate and even agreed with Ahl al-Haqq that there is no creator but God. The extreme view developed later.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the early Qadariyya believe about calling man a creator?
A) They openly called man creator.
B) They did not dare to apply the name 'creator' to servants.
C) They said God is the creator metaphorically.
D) They denied any human role in actions.

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya evolved from a moderate position to declaring man the real creator, while Ahl al-Haqq consistently maintained God as the sole creator with man as acquirer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed, do you ever feel that it was your own doing, as if God had no part in it? How does the early Qadariyya's hesitation to call man 'creator' challenge you to check your own heart? Commit to saying 'All praise is due to God' before claiming any accomplishment.
?
The Qadariyya gradually slipped from a moderate position to outright declaring man the real creator. In your own life, have you slowly begun to attribute more of your actions to your own power while forgetting God's constant involvement? What specific area of your life do you need to surrender to God's sole creative control today?
?
The people of truth maintained that God is the sole creator and the servant is an acquirer. Do you truly live as though God creates every action, even your sins and failures? How does affirming God's sovereignty in your mistakes change the way you seek forgiveness and seek to change?

Definition of Istita'a and Its Two Types
The author defines istita'a and divides it into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam definition of istita'a and its division into two distinct types: the preceding capacity of sound means and the concomitant accident that causes the act.

Core Idea
Istita'a is not one thing—it has two distinct meanings in kalam.

In classical kalam, the term 'istita'a' (capacity) is foundational for discussions of human agency. But what exactly does it mean? The author begins by clarifying that istita'a is nearly synonymous with other terms like qudra and quwwa. He then presents a critical distinction: istita'a refers to two different realities—one a pre-existing condition of sound means, the other an accident created at the time of the act. This lesson unpacks that division.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's statement that the terms are close in meaning and treated as synonyms in kalam.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the two divisions
The author divides istita'a into two types. Locate the description of the first type (sounding of causes and integrity of instruments) and the second type (an accident created by Allah).
The second type is defined negatively: cannot be pointed to except as relating to the act.
3
Understand the theological debate
Note that the second type's causal role is affirmed by the author and the Baghdadi Mu'tazila but denied by the Basri Mu'tazila. This reflects a key intra-Mu'tazili dispute.
The author sides with the Baghdadi view that it is a cause (ʿilla).
4
Reflect on the definition
The first type is defined as 'readiness to execute the act upon the will of the chooser.' Consider how this definition clarifies that it is a condition, not a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الاستطاعة والقوة والقدرة والطاقة متقاربة المعاني، وفي مصطلح أهل الكلام أنهم يريدون بها كلها شيئا واحدًا إذا أضافوها إلى العباد ويجعلونها في عرفهم بمنزلة الأسماء المترادفة كالأسد والليث وأشباه ذلك. ثم الأصل أن المسمى باسم القدرة والاستطاعة عندنا قسمان: أحدهما سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات وهي قد تتقدم الأفعال، وحقيقتها ليست بمحصولة عللاً للأفعال وإن كانت الأفعال لا تقوم إلا بها لكنها نعم من الله تعالى يكرم بها من يشاء ثم ( ) يستأديهم (٦) شكرها عند احتمالهم العلم بالنعم وبلوغ عقولهم الوقوف عليها، وهذا النوع من الاستطاعة يحد بأنها : التهيؤ لتنفيذ الفعل عند إرادة المختار . والقسم الثاني : معنى لا يمكن تبين حده بمعنى يشار إليه سوى أنه ليس إلا للفعل، وهو عرض يخلقه الله تعالى في الحيوان يفعل به أفعاله الاختيارية وهو علة للفعل (١) عندنا، وساعدنا عليه البغدادية من المعتزلة، وأنكرت البصرية ذلك وزعمت أنه سبب، وفي الجملة يحصل المحدث فاعلاً به.
Know that istita'a (capacity), quwwa (power), qudra (ability), and taqa (energy) have close meanings. In the terminology of the people of kalam, they mean by all of them one thing when they attribute them to servants, and they make them in their convention like synonymous names, such as 'asad' and 'layth' and the like. Then the principle is that what is named by the name of qudra and istita'a with us is two divisions: One of them is soundness of causes and integrity of instruments, and it may precede actions; its reality is not obtained as a cause for actions, although actions are not established except through it, but it is a blessing from Allah the Exalted with which He honors whom He wills, then ( ) He seeks from them (6) its gratitude when they bear the knowledge of blessings and their intellects reach to comprehend them. This type of istita'a is defined as: readiness to execute the act upon the will of the chooser. The second division: a meaning whose definition cannot be clarified by a meaning that is pointed to, except that it is only for the act, and it is an accident that Allah the Exalted creates in the living being by which it performs its voluntary actions, and it is a cause for the act (1) with us; the Baghdadiyya from the Mu'tazila agreed with us on it, and the Basriyya denied that and claimed that it is a means, and in summary, the contingent agent becomes an agent by it.
Source pages p. 786

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the term 'istita'a' as used in kalam. The author begins by noting that the terms istita'a, quwwa, qudra, and taqa are nearly synonymous in kalam discourse, treated as interchangeable when applied to human servants. Then he presents the foundational division of istita'a into two types. The first type is 'soundness of causes and integrity of instruments' – such as health and availability of tools – which can exist before the act. It is not a causal efficient factor but a necessary condition and a divine blessing, meriting gratitude. It is defined as 'readiness to execute the act when the chooser wills.' The second type is a distinct meaning that cannot be defined except as relating solely to the act; it is an accident created by Allah in the living being enabling voluntary actions. According to the author, this second type is truly a cause for the act. He notes that the Baghdadi Mu'tazila agreed on this causal role, but the Basran Mu'tazila denied it, claiming it is merely a means. In summary, the created being becomes an agent (fā'il) through this second type of capacity.
