Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That istita'a always means a power that exists simultaneously with the act.
Correction: The author teaches that there are two types: one that precedes the act (soundness of means) and one that is present with the act (created accident).

Checkpoint
What is the definition of the first type of istita'a according to the author?
A) The power that accompanies the act and is its cause.
B) Readiness to execute the act upon the will of the chooser.
C) An accident created at the time of the act.
D) The act itself.

Key Takeaway
Istita'a in kalam comprises two distinct concepts: the pre-existing soundness of means (a blessing) and the concomitant accident (a cause) created by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first type of istita'a is a blessing from Allah—sound health and means. When you wake up each morning, do you recognize this capacity as a divine gift and use it to obey Him? Or do you take it for granted and exhaust it in disobedience?
?
The second type of istita'a is created by Allah in you at the moment of action. Have you ever reflected that your ability to choose—to pray, to give charity, to control your anger—is a creation of Allah that He gives you at that very moment? How does this awareness change your sense of reliance on Him?
?
The author affirms that the created capacity is a cause; the Basran Mu'tazila say it is only a means. Which view leads you to feel more dependent on Allah? Does acknowledging that your actions are caused by a power Allah creates in you inspire you to humbly ask Him for that power before every action?

Introduction to the Impossibility of Accidents' Permanence: A Survey of Views
Nasafi lists schools' views on accidents' permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of Islamic theological positions on whether accidents can persist, and the key arguments each school uses.

Core Idea
The question of whether accidents persist is foundational to debates on human ability and divine action.

This passage from Imam Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla introduces a crucial theological issue: can accidents (temporary attributes) persist over time? The answer divides schools of kalam and has implications for understanding God's continuous creation and human free will. Nasafi sets the stage by enumerating six distinct positions before presenting his own proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Gain a sense of the structure: an introduction followed by a list of views.
The passage is densely packed with technical terms; don't get bogged down yet.
2
Identify each school and its position
List the six views mentioned: (1) 'Our companions' (Maturidi/Ash'ari), (2) agreeing Qadariyya scholars, (3) al-Nazzam, (4) Karamiyya, (5) Abu al-Hudhayl, (6) al-Iskafi's report about sukun. Note what each says about permanence.
Pay close attention to al-Nazzam's unique definition of 'accident'.
3
Compare the definitions of 'accident'
Notice that different scholars define 'accident' (al-'arad) differently. For al-Nazzam, only motion is an accident; colors etc. are bodies. For others, the definition is broader. How does this affect their view on permanence?
4
Understand the implications
Reflect on why the permanence of accidents matters for the larger kalam discourse on human ability (istita'a) and God's continuous creation. The author mentions this in the opening sentence.
This lesson is foundational for later arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقبل أن نشتغل بإيراد دلائلنا في المسألة نقدم دلائل استحالة القول ببقاء الأعراض إذ الكلام في المسألة يدور عليه. يستحيل القول ببقاء الأعراض: فنقول: اختلف الناس في جواز بقاء الأعراض واستحالته قال أصحابنا رحمهم الله: إن بقاء الأعراض مستحيل لمن يتصور بقاء شيء منه بل يوجد ثم ينعدم في الثاني من زمان وجوده وساعدنا عليه من جملة القدرية أبو القاسم الكعبي وأحمد بن علي الشطوي وأبو حفص الصيمري وقال النظام أيضا باستحالة بقاء الأعراض غير أنه لا عرض عنده إلا الحركة، وبقاؤها مستحيل عنده، وأما الألوان والطعوم والروائح والأصوات والخواطر فهي عنده أجسام جائزة البقاء. وزعمت الكرامية أن جميع الأعراض جائزة البقاء ، وقالوا: إن حدوث كل حادث في العالم بقول الله تعالى: "كن" وإرادته لحدوثه وعدم كل شيء بقوله تعالى له: "افن"، وإرادته عدمه، فإذا خلق جسمًا أو عرضاً بقى إلى أن يريد عدمه ويقول له: "افن". وقال أبو الهذيل من الأعراض ما لا يبقى ومنها ما يبقى، والذي لا يبقى الحركة والإرادة والذي يبقى الألوان والطعوم والروائح والتأليف والحياة والعلم والقدرة. وحكى الإسكافى عنه: أن سكون الحى لا يبقى وسكون البيت باق،
And before we begin to present our proofs on the matter, we present proofs of the impossibility of the statement that accidents persist, because the discussion on the matter revolves around it. The statement that accidents persist is impossible. We say: People differed concerning the permissibility of the permanence of accidents and its impossibility. Our companions (may God have mercy on them) said: The permanence of accidents is impossible; it can be imagined that nothing of it persists, rather it exists then ceases to exist in the second moment of its time of existence. And there agreed with us among the Qadariyya: Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi, Ahmad ibn Ali al-Shatwi, and Abu Hafs al-Saymari. And al-Nazzam also said that the permanence of accidents is impossible, except that in his view there is no accident except motion, and its permanence is impossible for him. As for colors, tastes, smells, sounds, and thoughts, they are in his view bodies that are permissible to persist. And the Karamiyya claimed that all accidents are permissible to persist. They said: The coming into existence of every originated thing in the world is by the saying of God Almighty: 'Be' and His will for its existence, and the non-existence of everything is by His saying to it: 'Perish' and His will for its non-existence. So when He creates a body or an accident, it persists until He wills its non-existence and says to it: 'Perish'. And Abu al-Hudhayl said: Among accidents, some do not persist and some persist. That which does not persist is motion and will, and that which persists is colors, tastes, smells, composition, life, knowledge, and power. And al-Iskafi reported from him: The rest of a living being does not persist, but the rest of a house persists.
Source pages p. 794

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the issue of whether accidents (al-a'rad) can persist (bagha'). The author states that before presenting his own proofs, he will give proofs of the impossibility of accidents persisting because the entire discussion of ability (al-istita'a) depends on this. Then he lists the positions of various groups on the permanence of accidents:

1. The Ash'ari/Maturidi position ('our companions'): Accidents cannot persist; they exist only for a moment and then cease.
2. Some Qadariyya (the Mu'tazilite scholars al-Ka'bi, al-Shatwi, and al-Saymari) agreed with this view.
3. Al-Nazzam: He also held that accidents cannot persist, but he defined accident very narrowly—only motion is an accident. Colors, tastes, etc., are bodies (ajsam) and can persist.
4. The Karamiyya: They held that all accidents can persist. They believed that every originated thing comes into existence by God's command 'Be' (kun) and is annihilated by His command 'Perish' (ifn). An accident persists until God wills its annihilation.
5. Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf: He made a distinction: some accidents (motion, will) do not persist, while others (colors, tastes, life, knowledge, power, etc.) do persist.
6. Al-Iskafi reported that Abu al-Hudhayl held that the 'rest' (sukun) of a living being does not persist, but the rest of an inanimate object (like a house) does persist.

This passage sets the stage for the author's own proofs that accidents cannot persist, which is crucial for his argument about human ability.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all Islamic theological schools agreed on the question of accidents' permanence.
Correction: This passage shows a wide spectrum of views, from complete impermanence to selective permanence to universal permanence, revealing deep disagreements in kalam.

Checkpoint

A) Color
B) Motion
C) Sound
D) Life

Key Takeaway
The debate over accidents' permanence reveals fundamental differences in how theologians understood the nature of created things and God's causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every accident—including your feelings of faith, sincerity, or desire to obey—ceases to exist in the very moment it arises, what does that mean for your reliance on God for your next good deed? Will you ask Him to create it anew?
?
The Karamiyya believed that God personally commands each thing to 'Be' and 'Perish'. Reflect on a specific sin you struggle with. Do you see it as something that God could simply command to perish in your life? What would it take for you to implore Him to do so?
?
Abu al-Hudhayl distinguished between the rest of a living being (which does not persist) and the rest of a house (which does). What does this say about the transient nature of your own peace and stillness? Have you been seeking permanent rest from creation rather than the Creator who alone grants lasting tranquility?

The Impossibility of Accident Subsistence: Three Early Views
Nasafi outlines the debate on accident subsistence and presents three differing opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central debate on whether accidents can subsist and the three main early positions in kalam.

Core Idea
Accidents perish instantly; only bodies endure, yet some theologians disagreed.

The issue of accident subsistence is a foundational problem in kalam, affecting how we understand the nature of creation and divine action. Before presenting his own arguments, Imam Nasafi maps out the existing views, showing the diversity of thought. This lesson introduces the problem and the three major positions: the impossibility of subsistence (mainstream), al-Nazzam's unique restriction of accidents to motion, and the Karramiyya's allowance of subsistence for all accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and the English translation. Note the structure: Nasafi first declares the impossibility, then reports the disagreement and three views.
The phrase 'يستحيل القول ببقاء الأعراض' sets the tone.
2
Identify the three positions
List the three views: 1) Ashabuna/al-Ka'bi – accidents cannot subsist; 2) al-Nazzam – only motion is accident and it cannot subsist, but colors etc. are bodies that can; 3) Karramiyya – all accidents can subsist until God annihilates them.
Note the supporting figures: Abu al-Qasim, al-Shatawi, al-Saymari for the first view.
3
Compare the definitions of 'accident'
Notice that al-Nazzam redefines what counts as an accident. This shows how definitions affect the debate.
For al-Nazzam, only motion is an accident; other qualities are bodies.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why this debate matters: if accidents persist, God's constant recreation is less emphasized; if they perish, each moment is a new creation.
This connects to broader discussions of divine power and causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقبل أن نشتغل بإيراد دلائلنا في المسألة نقدم دلائل استحالة القول ببقاء الأعراض إذ الكلام في المسألة يدور عليه. يستحيل القول ببقاء الأعراض: فنقول: اختلف الناس في جواز بقاء الأعراض واستحالته قال أصحابنا الكعبي: إن بقاء الأعراض مستحيل لمن يتصور بقاء شيء منه بل يوجد ثم ينعدم في الثاني من زمان وجوده وساعدنا عليه من جملة القدرية أبو القاسم وأحمد بن علي الشطوي وأبو حفص الصيمري وقال النظام أيضا باستحالة بقاء الأعراض غير أنه لا عرض عنده إلا الحركة، وبقاؤها مستحيل عنده، وأما الألوان والطعوم والروائح والأصوات والخواطر فهي عنده أجسام جائزة البقاء. وزعمت الكرامية أن جميع الأعراض جائزة البقاء ، وقالوا: إن حدوث كل حادث في العالم بقول الله تعالى: "كن" وإرادته لحدوثه وعدم كل شيء بقوله تعالى له: "افن"، وإرادته عدمه، فإذا خلق جسمًا أو عرضاً بقى إلى أن يريد عدمه ويقول له: "افن".
And before we occupy ourselves with presenting our proofs on the issue, we present proofs of the impossibility of the claim of the subsistence of accidents, since the discussion of the issue revolves around it. The claim of the subsistence of accidents is impossible: So we say: People disagreed on the permissibility and impossibility of the subsistence of accidents. Our companions (as-habuna) and al-Ka'bi said: The subsistence of accidents is impossible; it is not conceivable that anything of them subsists, rather it exists and then ceases to exist in the second moment of its time of existence. And among the Qadariyya, Abu al-Qasim, Ahmad ibn Ali al-Shatawi, and Abu Hafs al-Saymari agreed with us on that. And al-Nazzam also said that the subsistence of accidents is impossible, except that according to him there is no accident except motion, and its subsistence is impossible according to him; as for colors, tastes, smells, sounds, and thoughts, they are according to him bodies that are permissible of subsistence. And the Karramiyya claimed that all accidents are permissible of subsistence, and they said: The coming into existence of every originated thing in the world is by the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'Be' and His will for its coming into existence, and the non-existence of everything is by His saying to it: 'Perish' and His will for its non-existence. So when He creates a body or an accident, it subsists until He wills its non-existence and says to it: 'Perish'.
Source pages p. 794

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to a discussion on the subsistence of accidents. Nasafi states that before presenting his own proofs, he will first give proofs for the impossibility of the claim that accidents can subsist, because the whole discussion revolves around that. He then declares the impossibility. He then reports that people differed on the permissibility and impossibility of accident subsistence. He first mentions the view of his own school (Ashabuna) and al-Ka'bi: accidents are created and instantly cease to exist in the second moment of their existence. They are supported by some Qadariyya figures. Next, al-Nazzam holds a unique view: only motion is an accident, and it cannot subsist; other qualities like colors and tastes are actually bodies and can subsist. Finally, the Karramiyya claim all accidents can subsist, explaining that every event is created by God's command 'Be' and annihilated by 'Perish', so an accident remains until God wills its annihilation.

Key Terms
بقاء الأعراض
استحالة
الأعراض
الأجسام
الحركة
الكرامية
القول ببقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All early kalam scholars agreed that accidents cannot subsist.
Correction: This passage shows clear disagreement: al-Nazzam had a different definition, and the Karramiyya fully allowed subsistence.

Checkpoint
What is al-Nazzam's position on the subsistence of colors and tastes?
A) They are accidents that cannot subsist.
B) They are bodies that can subsist.
C) They are accidents that can subsist.
D) They are non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Early kalam scholars held diverse views on accident subsistence, from total impossibility to total possibility, highlighting the complexity of theological reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that your very existence is renewed every moment by God's command—like the Karramiyya claim—would you live more mindful of His presence? What one habit would you change to reflect that dependence?
?
Al-Nazzam saw only motion as an accident—fleeting and impermanent. What aspects of your inner life (beliefs, feelings, intentions) do you treat as permanent that might actually be as transient as motion? How would your self-assessment change if you recognized their fleeting nature?
?
The passage shows that early scholars disagreed deeply on this issue. Are there theological questions you avoid because of discomfort with uncertainty? What would it mean for you to engage sincerely with a view that challenges your current convictions?

Abu al-Hudhayl's Doctrine on Accident Subsistence
Abu al-Hudhayl's classification and theory of subsistence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Hudhayl's classification of accidents into subsisting and non-subsisting, and his unique theory that subsistence is a separate entity (baqa') commanded by God.

Core Idea
Abu al-Hudhayl claimed accidents subsist by a separate divine command 'Remain'.

Abu al-Hudhayl was a pioneering Mu'tazili theologian who developed a detailed theory of accident subsistence. He classified accidents into those that persist and those that do not, and introduced an innovative concept: a distinct 'subsistence' (baqa') that is not in a locus, identified with God's command. This lesson presents his views as reported by al-Iskafi.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu al-Hudhayl's classification
Read the passage carefully and identify the two categories of accidents: those that do not subsist (motion, will) and those that subsist (colors, tastes, etc.).
2
Understand his view on rest (sukun)
Notice that Abu al-Hudhayl distinguishes between the rest of a living being (non-subsisting) and the rest of an inanimate object like a house (subsisting). Also, the rest of the inhabitants of Paradise and Hell subsists perpetually.
3
Explore the concept of baqa' (subsistence)
Understand that he claims subsistence is due to a separate 'subsistence' that is not in a locus, and this entity is the command of Allah 'Remain'. This is a unique metaphysical entity.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this theory links the continued existence of accidents directly to a divine command, raising questions about God's continuous creative activity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو الهذيل من الأعراض ما لا يبقى ومنها ما يبقى، والذي لا يبقى الحركة والإرادة والذي يبقى الألوان والطعوم والروائح والتأليف والحياة والعلم والقدرة. وحكى الإسكافى عنه: أن سكون الحى لا يبقى وسكون البيت باق، والمشهور عنه أن سكون أهل الجنة وسكون أهل النار في آخر الأمر باق على الدوام، وكان يزعم أن ما يبقى من الأجسام والأعراض إنما يبقى من أجل بقاء لا في محل وذلك البقاء هو قول الله تعالى له: "ابق". وقال بشر بن المعتمر : إن السكون كله باق لا يفنى إلا بخروج الجسم منه إلى حركة ، وكذا كل لون باق لا يفنى إلا بخروج الجسم منه إلى ضده، وأحال محمد ابن شبيب بقاء الحركة والسكون. وقال الجبائي وابنه إن الصوت والآلام والحركات والفكر والإرادات والكراهات والفناء أعراض غير باقية وأجاز إبقاء الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة والاعتماد والتأليف والألوان والحياة والقدرة والعجز والعلوم والاعتقادات وقال الجبائي : السكون الذي يفعله الحي في نفسه وكل ما يفعله في نفسه مباشرًا غير باق، وأجاز بقاء الكلام ومنع ابنه من بقاء الكلام، وقال ضرار بن عمرو والنجاري أن الأعراض التي هي أبعاض الجسم عندهما باقية وما سواها من الأعراض مستحيل البقاء . وأجمع العقلاء: أن العرض لا يجوز أن يبقى ببقاء هو معنى زائدًا على الذات الباقي إذ قيام المعاني بالأعراض مستحيل لاستحالة قيامها بذاتها وافتقارها إلى محل تحله ويستحيل كونها محال بأغيارها غير أن أبا الهذيل يقول بأنها تبقى ببقاء هو صفة.
And Abu al-Hudhayl said: Among accidents, some do not subsist and some subsist. Those that do not subsist are motion and will; those that subsist are colors, tastes, smells, composition, life, knowledge, and power. And al-Iskafi reported from him: The rest of a living being does not subsist, but the rest of a house subsists; and the well-known from him is that the rest of the people of Paradise and the rest of the people of Hell in the final state subsist perpetually. And he used to claim that what subsists of bodies and accidents only subsists because of a subsistence (baqa') that is not in a locus, and that subsistence is the saying of Allah the Exalted to it: 'Remain'. And Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir said: All rest (sukun) subsists and does not perish except by the body moving out of it into motion; likewise every color subsists and does not perish except by the body moving out of it into its opposite. And Muhammad ibn Shabib deemed the subsistence of motion and rest impossible. And al-Jubba'i and his son said: Sound, pains, motions, thought, wills, aversions, and annihilation (fana') are non-subsisting accidents; and they permitted the subsistence of heat, cold, moisture, dryness, dependence (i'timad), composition, colors, life, power, inability, sciences, and beliefs. And al-Jubba'i said: The rest (sukun) that a living being creates in itself and everything it creates in itself directly is non-subsisting; and he permitted the subsistence of speech, but his son forbade the subsistence of speech. And Dirar ibn Amr and al-Najjar said: The accidents that are parts of the body according to them are subsisting, and what is other than them of accidents is impossible of subsistence. And the rational people agreed: It is not permissible for an accident to subsist by a subsistence that is an additional meaning (ma'na) beyond the subsisting essence, because the subsistence of meanings (ma'ani) in accidents is impossible due to the impossibility of their subsistence by themselves and their need for a locus in which they inhere; and it is impossible for them to be loci for other things. However, Abu al-Hudhayl says that they subsist by a subsistence that is an attribute (sifa).
Source pages p. 794

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the survey of opinions on accident subsistence. It begins with Abu al-Hudhayl's classification: some accidents (motion, will) do not subsist; others (colors, tastes, etc.) do subsist. Al-Iskafi reports that Abu al-Hudhayl distinguished between the rest of a living being (non-subsisting) and the rest of an inanimate object like a house (subsisting), and that the rest of the inhabitants of Paradise and Hell subsist perpetually. He also held that subsistence is due to a separate 'subsistence' (baqa') which is not in a locus, identified as God's command 'Remain'. Next, Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir held that all rest and all colors subsist until the body moves out of them. Muhammad ibn Shabib denied subsistence for motion and rest. Al-Jubba'i and his son detailed many accidents that do not subsist (sound, pains, etc.) and those that do (heat, cold, etc.); al-Jubba'i permitted subsistence of speech but his son forbade it. Dirar and al-Najjar held that only accidents that are parts of the body subsist. Finally, the passage states the rational consensus (ijma' al-'uqala'): an accident cannot subsist by a subsistence that is an additional meaning (ma'na) beyond the subsisting essence, because meanings cannot inhere in accidents due to their own dependence on a locus. However, Abu al-Hudhayl's view is noted as an exception, claiming subsistence is an attribute (sifa) of the accident itself.

Key Terms
أبو الهذيل
بقاء
سكون
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that for Abu al-Hudhayl, all accidents subsist by themselves without any external cause.
Correction: Actually, he held that subsistence is due to a separate entity (baqa') which is God's command, so accidents do not subsist intrinsically.

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Hudhayl, which of the following accidents are classified as non-subsisting?
A) Colors and tastes
B) Life and knowledge
C) Motion and will
D) Rest of a house

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Hudhayl held that accident subsistence is not inherent but depends on a divine command 'Remain'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abu al-Hudhayl believed that the rest of a house subsists because of a divine command 'Remain'. Do you live as though your very existence is held in place moment by moment by God's command, or do you take your continuing existence for granted? What would change if you truly internalized this?
?
Consider the 'rest' of the people in Paradise and Hell—the final, permanent state. If you died today, which 'rest' would you be heading toward? What does this passage demand you to change in your life right now?
?
The subsistence of an accident depends on a separate entity, not its own nature. In what ways do you depend on external supports to remain steadfast in your faith? Are you relying on God's command or on your own effort? What would it mean to rely entirely on His command 'Remain' for your spiritual perseverance?

Diverse Mu'tazili Views on Accident Subsistence
Survey of Mu'tazili opinions on accident subsistence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions of Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, Muhammad ibn Shabib, al-Jubba'i and his son, and Dirar and al-Najjar on which accidents subsist and why.

Core Idea
Early Mu'tazili scholars disagreed widely on which accidents subsist and under what conditions.

After presenting Abu al-Hudhayl's theory, Nasafi records a spectrum of opinions from other prominent Mu'tazili theologians. Each scholar offered a distinct classification of subsisting and non-subsisting accidents, reflecting deeper metaphysical commitments. This lesson surveys these views to show the richness of early kalam debate.

Guided Path
1
Read Bishr's and Muhammad ibn Shabib's views
Bishr held that all rest and colors subsist until the body moves out of them. Muhammad ibn Shabib denied subsistence for motion and rest entirely.
2
Examine al-Jubba'i and his son's detailed list
Al-Jubba'i and his son enumerated many accidents: non-subsisting (sound, pains, motions, etc.) and subsisting (heat, cold, etc.). They differed on speech.
3
Understand Dirar and al-Najjar's criterion
For Dirar and al-Najjar, only accidents that are parts of the body subsist; other accidents cannot persist.
4
Compare the diversity
Reflect on how each view ties to a broader theory of accident ontology and causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?
The scholars listed disagreed on basic metaphysical facts about the world. Do you ever assume that learned scholars all hold the same views? How does seeing their real diversity challenge you to investigate issues for yourself rather than blindly following a single opinion?
?
Al-Jubba'i considered your thoughts (fikr) to be non-subsisting accidents—temporary and not enduring. How often do you treat your own passing thoughts as permanent truths? Will you today take a thought you are holding onto and examine it: is it truly reliable, or is it just a fleeting accident?

Rational Consensus Against Subsistence by an Additional Meaning
Rational consensus against subsistence via additional meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational consensus (ijma' al-'uqala') that accidents cannot subsist by a subsistence that is an additional meaning (ma'na) beyond the essence, and Abu al-Hudhayl's exception.

Core Idea
The consensus holds that subsistence via an additional meaning is impossible because meanings cannot inhere in accidents.

After surveying diverse views, Nasafi presents what he considers the rational consensus (ijma' al-'uqala') which sets a logical boundary: no accident can subsist by a separate entity (ma'na) added to its essence. This principle grounds the impossibility of meanings inhering in accidents. However, Abu al-Hudhayl's view that subsistence is an attribute (sifa) is noted as a deviation.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus statement
Read the passage from 'And the rational people agreed' to the end. Identify the core claim: an accident cannot subsist by a subsistence that is an additional meaning.
2
Understand the justification
The reason given: meanings cannot subsist in accidents because accidents themselves depend on a locus and cannot be loci for other things.
3
Note Abu al-Hudhayl's exception
Abu al-Hudhayl breaks this consensus by claiming subsistence is an attribute (sifa) of the accident, not a separate meaning.
4
Reflect on the significance
This principle prevents an infinite regress of subsistences and maintains the ontological dependence of accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And Abu al-Hudhayl said: Among accidents, some do not subsist and some subsist. And al-Jubba'i said: The rest (sukun) that a living being creates in itself and everything it creates in itself directly is non-subsisting; and he permitted the subsistence of speech, but his son forbade the subsistence of speech. And Dirar ibn Amr and al-Najjar said: The accidents that are parts of the body according to them are subsisting, and what is other than them of accidents is impossible of subsistence. And the rational people agreed: It is not permissible for an accident to subsist by a subsistence that is an additional meaning (ma'na) beyond the subsisting essence, because the subsistence of meanings (ma'ani) in accidents is impossible due to the impossibility of their subsistence by themselves and their need for a locus in which they inhere; and it is impossible for them to be loci for other things. However, Abu al-Hudhayl says that they subsist by a subsistence that is an attribute (sifa).
Source pages p. 794

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the survey of opinions on accident subsistence. It begins with Abu al-Hudhayl's classification: some accidents (motion, will) do not subsist; others (colors, tastes, etc.) do subsist. Al-Iskafi reports that Abu al-Hudhayl distinguished between the rest of a living being (non-subsisting) and the rest of an inanimate object like a house (subsisting), and that the rest of the inhabitants of Paradise and Hell subsist perpetually. He also held that subsistence is due to a separate 'subsistence' (baqa') which is not in a locus, identified as God's command 'Remain'. Next, Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir held that all rest and all colors subsist until the body moves out of them. Muhammad ibn Shabib denied subsistence for motion and rest. Al-Jubba'i and his son detailed many accidents that do not subsist (sound, pains, etc.) and those that do (heat, cold, etc.); al-Jubba'i permitted subsistence of speech but his son forbade it. Dirar and al-Najjar held that only accidents that are parts of the body subsist. Finally, the passage states the rational consensus (ijma' al-'uqala'): an accident cannot subsist by a subsistence that is an additional meaning (ma'na) beyond the subsisting essence, because meanings cannot inhere in accidents due to their own dependence on a locus. However, Abu al-Hudhayl's view is noted as an exception, claiming subsistence is an attribute (sifa) of the accident itself.

Key Terms
إجماع العقلاء
معنى
صِفَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the consensus completely denies accident subsistence, but it only denies subsistence via an additional meaning.
Correction: The consensus allows that accidents may subsist by themselves (intrinsically) or as attributes, but not by a separate entity.

Checkpoint
Why does the rational consensus consider it impossible for an accident to subsist by an additional meaning?
A) Because accidents are eternal
B) Because meanings cannot inhere in accidents due to their own dependence on a locus
C) Because God commands directly
D) Because accidents are simple

Key Takeaway
The rational consensus establishes that accident subsistence cannot be via an additional meaning, but Abu al-Hudhayl's alternative view frames subsistence as an attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If accidents cannot subsist by themselves, what does that imply about the things you rely on daily? Do you ever treat material objects as if they have independent permanence, forgetting they are sustained by Allah each moment? How can you cultivate a state of constant awareness of Allah's preservation?
?
The passage shows rational consensus that an accident cannot have an additional meaning for its subsistence. How does this strengthen your understanding that only Allah is self-subsisting? Are there any aspects of your life where you mistakenly attribute self-subsistence to created things, such as your health, wealth, or relationships? Write down one specific area and commit to turning to Allah alone for its continuity.
?
Abu al-Hudhayl's differing view reminds us that even scholars can err. When you encounter a disagreement among scholars, do you humble yourself to the consensus (ijma') of early Muslims, or do you follow your own reasoning? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to prefer novel opinions over established truth? Reflect on a recent issue where you chose a minority view—what was the basis of your choice, and how can you better respect the collective understanding of the Salaf?

Diverse Views on Accident Permanence
Enumerates various scholars' views on accident permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major theological positions on the permanence of accidents and bodies among early kalam scholars.

Core Idea
Accident permanence was a deeply debated topic among early Muslim theologians.

This passage from Al-Tabsira showcases the rich diversity of views on whether accidents can endure. Several prominent scholars offer contrasting opinions, revealing the complexity of kalam metaphysics. Pay attention to how each scholar categorizes different types of accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the different opinions. Note the names and their positions.
The passage is dense with names; don't worry about memorizing all at once.
2
Identify each scholar's view
For each scholar mentioned, note which accidents they consider permanent (bāqī) and which not. Make a list: Abu al-Hudhayl (or the unnamed figure), Bishr b. al-Muʿtamir, Muḥammad b. Shabīb, al-Jubbāʾī and his son, Ḍirār b. ʿAmr and al-Najjār.
Some scholars allow permanence for certain accidents but not others.
3
Compare their criteria
Notice the reasoning behind each view. For example, Bishr connects permanence to the absence of change, while al-Jubbāʾī distinguishes between accidents produced directly and indirectly.
Consider how these views relate to the nature of accidents as dependent entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمشهور عنه أن سكون أهل الجنة وسكون أهل النار في آخر الأمر باق على الدوام، وكان يزعم أن ما يبقى من الأجسام والأعراض إنما يبقى من أجل بقاء لا في محل وذلك البقاء هو قول الله تعالى له: "ابق".
وقال بشر بن المعتمر : إن السكون كله باق لا يفنى إلا بخروج الجسم منه إلى حركة ، وكذا كل لون باق لا يفنى إلا بخروج الجسم منه إلى ضده، وأحال محمد ابن شبيب بقاء الحركة والسكون
وقال الجبائي وابنه إن الصوت والآلام والحركات والفكر والإرادات والكراهات والفناء أعراض غير باقية وأجاز إبقاء الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة والاعتماد والتأليف والألوان والحياة والقدرة والعجز والعلوم والاعتقادات وقال الجبائي : السكون الذي يفعله الحي في نفسه وكل ما يفعله في نفسه مباشرًا غير باق، وأجاز بقاء الكلام ومنع ابنه من بقاء الكلام، وقال ضرار بن عمرو والنجاري أن الأعراض التي هي أبعاض الجسم عندهما باقية وما سواها من الأعراض مستحيل البقاء .
وأجمع العقلاء: أن العرض لا يجوز أن يبقى ببقاء هو معنى زائدًا على الذات الباقي إذ قيام المعاني بالأعراض مستحيل لاستحالة قيامها بذاتها وافتقارها إلى محل تحله ويستحيل كونها محال بأغيارها
And the famous [view] from him is that the rest (sukūn) of the people of Paradise and the rest of the people of Hellfire in the end remains permanent (bāqin) perpetually, and he claimed that whatever remains of bodies (ajsām) and accidents (a‘rāḍ) only remains because of a permanence (baqā’) not in a locus (lā fī maḥall), and that permanence is the saying of Allah the Exalted to it: 'Abide (ubqu)'. And Bishr ibn al-Mu‘tamir said: Indeed, rest (sukūn) all of it remains, not perishing except by the exit of the body from it to motion, and likewise every color remains, not perishing except by the exit of the body from it to its opposite. And Muḥammad ibn Shabīb deemed impossible the permanence of motion and rest. And al-Jubbā’ī and his son said: Indeed, sound, pains, motions, thought, volitions, aversions, and annihilation (fanā’) are non-enduring accidents, and they allowed the causing to endure (ibqā’) of heat, cold, moisture, dryness, inclination (i‘timād), composition (ta’līf), colors, life, power, incapacity, knowledges, and beliefs. And al-Jubbā’ī said: The rest that the living being produces in himself and everything he produces in himself directly are non-enduring, and he allowed the permanence of speech (kalām), and his son prohibited the permanence of speech. And Ḍirār ibn ‘Amr and al-Najjār said: The accidents that are parts (ab‘āḍ) of the body according to them are enduring, and other than them from among accidents are impossible of permanence. And the rational people (‘uqalā’) concurred: That the accident (al-‘araḍ) may not endure by a permanence (baqā’) that is a meaning (ma‘nā) superadded (zā’id) to the enduring essence (al-dhāt al-bāqī), because the subsistence (qiyām) of meanings (ma‘ānī) in accidents is impossible due to the impossibility of their subsisting in themselves and their need for a locus (maḥall) to contain them, and it is impossible for them to become loci for other accidents.
Source pages p. 795

Commentary
This passage presents the opinions of several theologians on the permanence (baqā’) of accidents (a‘rāḍ) and bodies (ajsām). It begins by stating the famous view of an unnamed figure (likely Abu al-Hudhayl) that the rest (sukūn) of the people of Paradise and Hellfire remains permanent perpetually, and that bodies and accidents remain due to a permanence (baqā’) that is not in a locus, which is the effect of God's command 'Abide'. Then it lists the views: Bishr ibn al-Mu‘tamir says that rest (sukūn) and colors remain until the body moves or changes color; Muḥammad ibn Shabīb denies the permanence of motion and rest; al-Jubbā’ī and his son allow some accidents to endure (like heat, cold, etc.) but not others (like sound, pain, etc.); al-Jubbā’ī distinguishes rest produced by a living being as non-enduring; his son differs on speech; Ḍirār and al-Najjār say accidents that are parts of the body endure, others not. Then the passage states the consensus of rational people: an accident cannot endure by a permanence that is a superadded meaning to the enduring essence, because it is impossible for meanings to subsist in accidents, as accidents cannot serve as loci for other accidents. This consensus refutes any view that attributes permanence to accidents by a separate attribute.

أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think all early theologians agreed that accidents cannot endure.
Correction: The passage shows many scholars allowed certain types of accidents to endure, often depending on their nature or cause.

Checkpoint
According to Bishr ibn al-Mu‘tamir, when does a color perish?
A) When the body moves
B) When the body changes to its opposite color
C) When time passes
D) When God wills

Key Takeaway
Early kalam scholars held diverse views on accident permanence, often distinguishing between different types of accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that early scholars like al-Jubbai and his son disagreed even on the permanence of speech, does that ever shake your trust in the intellectual foundations of Islamic theology? How do you reconcile such diversity with your personal conviction?
?
The passage shows that some theologians held that mental acts like thought and volition are temporary accidents. If your thoughts are fleeting, how does that affect the weight you give to your own inner dialogues and intentions before Allah?
?
Do you treat your own moments of spiritual rest (sukūn) as permanent or as something that can be lost? What would change if you saw your state of faith as an enduring accident sustained only by God's command?

Consensus: Accidents Cannot Bear Superadded Permanence
States the consensus that accidents cannot endure by a superadded permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus argument that accidents cannot endure by a superadded attribute of permanence.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot support a separate attribute of permanence because they cannot host other accidents.

After presenting various opinions, the passage concludes with a crucial consensus among rational thinkers. This consensus rejects the idea that accidents can endure through a superadded meaning (ma‘nā zā’id), based on the nature of accidents themselves. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus statement
Read the Arabic and translation of the final sentence. Note the key terms: 'ijmā‘ al-‘uqalā’' (consensus of the rational), 'baqā’' (permanence), 'ma‘nā zā’id' (superadded meaning).
This is a definitive position, not just one opinion among many.
2
Understand the argument
The consensus declares that an accident cannot endure by a permanence that is a superadded meaning (i.e., a separate attribute). Why? Because that would require the accident to become a locus (maḥall) for that meaning, which is impossible since accidents themselves subsist in a locus (body) and cannot bear other accidents.
The reasoning is based on the nature of accidents as dependent entities.
3
Reflect on its significance
This consensus effectively refutes any view that posits a separate attribute of permanence for accidents. It clarifies that if an accident is to endure, it must be because of its own nature or God's continuous creation, not a superadded accident.
This is a key metaphysical principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Objection and Response: Change of Substance and Attributes
Nasafi answers an objection about whether change requires a new meaning if the attribute was always present.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that an attribute's necessity does not prove it is essential, and his proof that a new meaning is required for any change.

Core Idea
Even permanent attributes require a meaning beyond the essence.

Nasafi responds to an objection that if an attribute is never absent from a substance, then change does not require a new meaning. He argues that impossibility of the attribute at the first moment proves it is not from the essence, so a meaning is still needed. This lesson walks through his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opponent's claim: if a substance can never be without a description, then change in the second moment does not require a new meaning.
The opponent assumes that necessity of the attribute makes it essential.
2
Analyze Nasafi's response
Nasafi dismisses the objection and presents a classification: being described is either due to the essence itself or due to a meaning. If the essence exists and the attribute is impossible, it cannot be from the essence.
Even if the attribute is always present, its initial impossibility proves it is not essential.
3
Grasp the reductio ad absurdum
Nasafi provides an example: if blackness could exist in a substance without it being described as black, then later become black, that would be absurd. This shows the opponent's logic fails.
This illustrates why the attribute must be from a meaning, not the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسأل بعضهم: إن تغير الجوهر عما كان عليه إنما يوجب التغير بوجود معنى زائد على الذات إذا جاز وجود الجوهر متعريًا عن ذلك الوصف أوقاتًا، فأما إذا لم يجز تعريه عنه ولا وقتا واحدًا ووجب تغيره في الثاني من حال حدوثه لا محالة لم يجب به حدوث معنى فيه. قلنا هذا كلام من لا يدري ما يقول، وهذا لأن الاتصاف إما أن يكون لأجل الذات، وإما أن يكون لمعنى، وإذا وجد الذات واستحال الاتصاف علم أن الاتصاف ليس من موجبات الذات، فعلم أنه من موجبات المعنى لأن الذات لو وجد وانعدم جواز الاتصاف كان الاتصاف من موجبات المعنى دون الذات، فإذا مع كان الاتصاف مستحيلاً مع وجود الذات كان أولى أن يكون الاتصاف موجب معنى وراء الذات لا موجب الذات. يحققه أن الاتصاف لو كان من موجبات الذات وقد وجدت الذات واستحال الاتصاف لكان فيه القول باستحالة ثبوت الحكم، وإن تحققت العلة وهذا ظاهر الفساد ولو جاز هذا لجاز أن يقوم السواد بذات ويستحيل اتصافه بأنه أسود ثم في الزمان الثاني يجب اتصافه به وهذا محال لم يقل به أحد من العقلاء.
And some of them asked: The change of the substance from what it was is only necessitated by the existence of an additional meaning over the essence if it is permissible for the substance to be divested of that description at some times. But if it is not permissible for it to be divested of it even for a single moment, and its change in the second [moment] from the state of its occurrence is inevitable, then it is not necessitated by it the occurrence of a meaning in it. We said: This is the speech of one who does not know what he says. This is because the attribute is either on account of the essence, or on account of a meaning. When the essence exists and the attribute is impossible, it is known that the attribute is not from the necessities of the essence. Thus it is known that it is from the necessities of the meaning, because if the essence existed and the possibility of the attribute was absent, the attribute would be from the necessities of the meaning, not the essence. So when the attribute is impossible together with the existence of the essence, it is even more appropriate that the attribute is a necessitation of a meaning beyond the essence, not a necessitation of the essence. He substantiates it: If the attribute were from the necessities of the essence, and the essence existed and the attribute was impossible, then that would contain the statement of the impossibility of the subsistence of the judgment, even though the cause is realized, and this is apparent corruption. And if this were permissible, then it would be permissible for blackness to subsist in an essence and for its description as black to be impossible, then in the second moment its description as black must occur, and this is impossible; no rational person has said it.
Source pages p. 798

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from an opponent regarding the change of a substance (جوهر) and the need for an additional meaning (معنى). The opponent argues: if a substance can be divested of a description at some times, then when it acquires that description, it requires a new meaning. But if the substance could never be without that description even for a moment, then its change in the second moment does not require a new meaning because the attribute was necessary from the start. Nasafi responds sharply: this is ignorance. He explains that being described (اتصاف) is either due to the essence itself or due to a meaning. If the essence exists and the attribute is impossible, then it is not from the essence; hence it is from a meaning. Even if the substance was never without it, the impossibility of the attribute at the initial moment proves it is not essential. He then proves this by a reductio: if the attribute were from the essence, it would be impossible for the attribute to be absent when the essence exists. This would allow absurdities like a black substance being not black at first and then black later, which no rational person accepts.

Key Terms
جوهر
ذات
معنى
اتصاف
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an attribute is always present, it must be from the essence itself (ذاتي).
Correction: Nasafi shows that even a permanent attribute requires a meaning because at the first moment it was impossible for the substance to have it (e.g., before it existed or before the attribute applied).

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's argument in this passage, what proves that a substance's attribute is not from the essence (ذات)?
A) The attribute is sometimes absent.
B) The essence exists but the attribute is impossible at the first moment.
C) The substance changes over time.
D) The attribute is always present.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi proves that any attribute of a substance, even if always present, requires a meaning (معنى) beyond the essence (ذات).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own good qualities, do you assume they are simply part of your nature (ذات) or do you recognize they depend on causes (معنى) beyond you? How does this affect your gratitude towards the One who bestows them?
?
In this lesson, Nasafi shows that even permanent attributes require a separate cause. Have you ever taken a constant blessing for granted, thinking it is just 'how you are', without acknowledging the One who continually gives it? Will you now consciously attribute every good in you to its true source?
?
The opponent's mistake was thinking that if something is always present, it must be from the essence. Are there beliefs or habits you have never questioned because they have always been with you? This lesson invites you to examine: are they truly from your core, or are they conditioned by something else? What would it mean to discover their true cause and then respond to it?

The Criterion for Attributes of Meaning
Nasafi argues against distinguishing temporary and permanent divestment of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion for an attribute being a 'description of meaning' and why the duration of divestment is irrelevant.

Core Idea
A description of meaning is known by its absence while the essence exists.

Nasafi begins by questioning his opponents about attributes that a substance can sometimes lack. He pushes them to clarify why they consider such attributes 'descriptions of meaning' and whether duration matters. This argument sets the foundation for the subsequent discussion on attribute theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic aloud, then follow the translation. Notice the question-and-answer structure.
The passage is a dialectical argument, so track the opponent's implied response.
2
Identify the key question
Nasafi asks: why is an attribute that a substance can sometimes be without a 'description of meaning'? He gives two possible reasons.
The two options are: (1) because its absence with the essence shows it is not essential, or (2) for another reason.
3
Analyze Nasafi's refutation
If the opponent chooses the second reason, they must produce it – impossible. If they choose the first, they admit the criterion is absence with existence, not duration. Hence temporary and permanent divestment are equal.
This refutes the view that one moment of divestment is different from many.
4
Grasp the core idea
The key takeaway: the attribute's absence while the essence remains shows it is not from the essence. This is the defining characteristic of a 'description of meaning'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: الوصف الذي كان يجوز كون الجوهر متعريًا عند أوقاتا لم كان وصف المعنى؟ ألأجل أنه إذا انعدم مع وجود الذات علم أنه ليس من موجبات الذات أم لأجل معنى آخر؟ فإن قال بالثاني، كلف إبرازه ولا قدره له عليه، وإن قال بالأول فقد أقر بما يوجب بطلان سؤاله هذا، وظهر أن لا فرق بين جواز التعري وقتا واحدًا وبين جوازه أوقاتا كثيرة. ثم يقال: لو كان بغير الوصف مع جواز بقائه متعريا عنه يوجب حدوث معنى كان بغيره مع استحالة بقائه متعريًا عنه أولى، ولولا حدوث المعنى الذي اضطر إلى تغير وصفه لجاز وجوده على ما كان عليه قبله. ثم يقال لهم: إذا استحال كون الجوهر متحركًا أو ساكنا في حال الحدوث واستحال بقاؤه عليه متعريًا عنهما بعد لم يحتج إلى حدوث معنى من أجله صار متحركًا أو ساكنا، فإذا كان هذا مستحيلاً ولم يكن جائزا عند أهل التوحيد ولم يكن بد من القول بوجود الحركة أو السكون دل ذلك على بعض ما قلتموه. فإن قيل: هذا الاستدلال مبني على مقدمة مجحودة وهي: أنكم ادعيتم استحالة الوصف بالبقاء
Then it is said to them: The description which it was permissible for the substance to be divested of at times, why was it a description of meaning? Is it because when it is absent with the existence of the essence, it is known that it is not from the necessities of the essence, or for another meaning? If they say the second, they are required to produce it and they have no power over that. And if they say the first, then they have admitted what necessitates the invalidity of this question of theirs, and it becomes clear that there is no difference between the permissibility of divestment for one moment and its permissibility for many moments. Then it is said: If its being without the description, with the permissibility of its remaining divested of it, necessitates the occurrence of a meaning, then its being without it with the impossibility of its remaining divested of it is even more appropriate. And were it not for the occurrence of the meaning which compelled the change of its description, its existence would be permissible as it was before. Then it is said to them: If it is impossible for the substance to be moving or stationary in the state of origination, and it is impossible for it to remain after that divested of both, then it does not need the occurrence of a meaning on account of which it becomes moving or stationary. If this is impossible and not permissible according to the people of tawhid, and there is no escape from stating the existence of motion or stillness, then that indicates some of what you have said. Then it is said: This inference is based on a disputed premise: that you have claimed the impossibility of description with permanence.
Source pages p. 799

Commentary
This passage contains a series of further arguments by Nasafi. First, he asks the opponent: why do you consider a description that the substance can sometimes be without as a 'description of meaning' (صفة المعنى)? Is it because when the description is absent while the essence exists, it shows it is not from the essence? Or for some other reason? If the opponent says the latter, he must produce that reason, which he cannot. If he says the former, he admits that the criterion is the absence with existence, not the length of time. Hence there is no difference between divestment for one moment or many. Second, he argues: if being without an attribute when it is possible to remain divested of it necessitates a meaning, then being without it when it is impossible to remain divested of it is even more in need of a meaning. Third, he discusses the case of motion and rest: if a substance cannot be moving or still at its origination, and cannot remain without both afterward, then it must acquire one of them through a meaning. This is accepted by the people of tawhid. Then a new objection is raised: this inference is based on a disputed premise that you claim the impossibility of description with permanence (but the text cuts off).

Key Terms
وصف
معنى
تعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an attribute being divested for a short time vs. a long time makes a difference in its nature.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the only criterion is absence with existence; duration is irrelevant.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what determines whether an attribute is a 'description of meaning'?
A) The length of time the substance is divested of it.
B) The attribute can be absent while the essence remains.
C) The attribute cannot be divested at all.
D) The attribute is always present.

Key Takeaway
The defining mark of an attribute of meaning is that its absence is possible while the essence exists, regardless of duration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own character, do you treat your flaws as essential to you (thinking 'that's just who I am') or as accidental attributes that can be removed while you remain you? How does Nasafi's criterion—absence while essence exists—challenge your view?
?
You may have sinned briefly but told yourself it was 'just once,' as if the brevity makes it less damaging to your soul. How does this lesson's logic—that no distinction exists between momentary and prolonged divestment—force you to reconsider the weight of a single sin?
?
Think of a time you judged someone harshly for a recurring fault but excused yourself for a single lapse. How does the principle that duration is irrelevant to the nature of an attribute expose your double standard, and what will you change about how you evaluate others?

Further Arguments on Attributes and Motion
Nasafi gives a fortiori and motion/rest arguments, then an objection is raised.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's additional arguments about the necessity of a meaning when divestment is impossible, and his example of motion and rest.

Core Idea
If being without an attribute requires a meaning, then being without it permanently requires that meaning even more.

Nasafi continues his refutation by presenting two more arguments. First, he argues that if temporary divestment needs a meaning, then permanent divestment is even more in need. Second, he uses the example of motion and rest to show that a substance cannot exist without either, so they must arise from a meaning. The passage ends with an objection that the argument relies on a disputed premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the logical structure: 'if... then...' arguments.
The last sentence is incomplete; focus on what is present.
2
Understand the 'if... then' argument
Nasafi says: if being without an attribute while it is possible to remain divested necessitates a meaning, then being without it when it is impossible to remain divested is even more appropriate.
This is a stronger argument from the lesser to the greater (a fortiori).
3
Analyze the motion and rest example
A substance cannot be moving or resting at origination, and it cannot remain divested of both afterward. So it must acquire one via a meaning. This is agreed by the people of tawhid.
This shows that attributes like motion are not self-explanatory; they need an underlying meaning.
4
Consider the objection
The opponent might say this inference rests on a disputed premise: that permanence cannot be a description. The passage cuts off, but the argument is that the necessity of a meaning is not conceded.

Source Text

?

The Impossibility of Accidents Persisting: Refutation Based on Opposites
Nasafi refutes the claim that accidents can persist using the problem of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's refutation of the claim that accidents can persist, including the trilemma about the timing of a new accident and the resulting implications for the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
If accidents persisted, nothing could ever annihilate them, leading to the absurdity of an eternal world.

This lesson presents a sophisticated argument from Imam al-Nasafi against the Mu'tazilite view that accidents (a'rāḍ) can persist (baqā') by themselves. The argument proceeds through a logical trilemma: if a new accident is said to cause the non-existence of a persistent one, when does it do so? Each possible answer leads to contradiction. The conclusion is that if accidents persisted, they could never cease to exist, which contradicts our observation of change. The argument also has far-reaching consequences for the possibility of annihilating bodies and the eternity of the world.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the initial principle
The author begins by stating a logical principle: it is easier to repel what is not established than to remove what is established. This frames the argument.
This principle is used to highlight the absurdity of the opponent's claim.
2
Follow the trilemma
The key question: when does a new accident (al-ṭāri') cause the non-existence of a persistent one? Before, during, or after its existence? Each option is examined.
The trilemma forms the backbone of the refutation.
3
Examine each impossible option
First, before existence is impossible because the non-existent cannot act. Second, during existence: if in same substrate, opposites coexist; if in different substrate, no effect; if no substrate, impossible for accidents. Third, after existence leads to infinite regress.
Each option is systematically dismantled.
4
Consider the observed proof
The author points to the clear succession of opposites in substrates as empirical evidence that accidents do not persist.
Observation confirms the theoretical conclusion.
5
Understand the final implication
If accidents persisted, annihilation of bodies would also be impossible, leading to the conclusion that the world is eternal - which all rational people reject.
This shows the dangerous consequence of the Mu'tazilite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرفع الثابت المتقرر ، والباقي يرفع ما لم يتقرر بعد ولم يثبت بعد، ولا شك أن دفع ما لم يثبت أسهل من رفع ما تقرر ، فكان رفع الثابت المتقرر لما لم يثبت بعد أولى في عقل من له عقل من رفع ما ليس بثابت في نفسه لما هو ثابت متقرر. والذي يقطع شغب هؤلاء الجهلة أن يقال لهم : إن الطارئ متى يوجب عدم الباقي قبل وجوده أم في حال وجوده أم بعدما وجد ؟ لا جائز أن يقال يوجب عدم الباقي قبل وجوده لأنه قبل وجوده معدوم، والمعدوم لا يوجب عدم الموجود، فكان انعدام الباقي قبل وجود الطارئ لعلة سوى وجود الطارئ، ولا جائز أن يوجب عدمه في حال وجوده لأنا نقول : يوجد في هذا المحل أم لا في محل (أم في محل) آخر؟ فإن قال : يوجد في هذا، فهذا محال، لأن وجوده في هذا المحل قبل ارتفاع الأول عن المحل محال؛ لأنه يوجب البقاء للضدين في الوجود، وإذا التقيا في الوجود لم يكونا ضدين وإن وجد لا في محل فوجود العرض لا في محل محال أيضا، ووجوده لا في محل هذا الضد لا يوجب عدمه؛ لأن وجوده ضد لا في محل ضده لا يوجب عدم ضده إن وجد في محل آخر، فذاك لا يوجب عدم العرض الباقي لأن السواد الموجود في المحل لا يوجب عدم البياض الثابت في محل آخر إلى أن ينتقل إلى هذا المحل، ولا يتصور انتفاء له إلى هذا المحل لاستحالة الانتقال من محل إلى محل على الأعراض، ولأن هذا العرض الطارئ عندهم علة لانعدام الباقي، ومن أصلهم أن العلة تكون سابقة على المعلول، فيكون وجود الطارئ سابقا على انعدام الموجود ثم لا يوجب عدمه ما لم ينتقل إليه لما مر، فينتقل إليه ثم يعدمه في الثاني فيؤدي إلى اجتماع الضدين في محل الضد الأول، ولا جائز أن يوجب عدمه في الثاني لأنا نقول: وجد في أي محل حتى يوجب عدم العرض الباقي عن المحل في الثاني، ويقسم الكلام ويبطل كل قسم على ما ذكرنا في الفصل المتقدم؟ ولأنه لو جاز وجودهما في زمان واحد جاز أيضا في الثاني والثالث إذ ليس تنافيهما في الثاني أولى من تنافيهما في الأول، ولأن عدم الباقي في الثاني لوجود ضده ليس بأولى من عدم ضده في الثاني لوجوده، والحالة الثانية لكل واحد منهما حالة البقاء، فثبت بما قررنا أن العرض لو كان باقيًا لذاته لما تصور عدمه، وحيث رأينا تعاقب الأعراض المتضادة على المحال شاهدة علمنا بطلان قولهم ضرورة إلا أن يجحدوا عدم الأعراض وادعوا فيها الظهور والكمون فيلتحقون حينئذ بإخوانهم الدهرية، وبطلان ذلك قد مر، مع أنهم لا يتجاسرون على إظهار ذلك خوفا من معرة السيف، وكذا على مذهبهم لا يمكن القول بانعدام الأجسام، إذ من مذهب من يناظرهم من الجبائية والبهشمية إذ انعدام الأجسام لا تكون إلا بحدوث الفناء وهو عرض حادث لا في محل مضاد للأجسام والجواهر، فيوجب عدمها في الثاني، وحدوث العرض لا في محل مستحيل، ولا طريق للإعدام عندهم إلا هذا فإذا تعذر القول بجواز انعدام الأجسام والجواهر والأعراض جميعًا، ولهذا أحال الجاحظ منهم عدم الأجسام، وأجمع العقلاء سوى الجاحظ أن كل موجود يمتنع عليه العدم فهو قديم، فصار قولهم مؤديا إلى القول بقدم العالم
He removes the established and settled, and he removes that which has not yet been settled nor established, and there is no doubt that repelling what has not been established is easier than removing what has been settled. So removing the established and settled for that which has not yet been established is more fitting in the mind of one who has intellect than removing that which is not established in itself for that which is established and settled. And what cuts off the confusion of these ignorant ones is that it be said to them: when does the arriving (al-ṭāri') cause the non-existence of the persistent (al-bāqī): before its existence, or in the state of its existence, or after it has existed? It is not permissible to say that it causes the non-existence of the persistent before its existence, because before its existence it is non-existent, and the non-existent does not cause the non-existence of the existent. So the non-existence of the persistent before the existence of the arriving is due to a cause other than the existence of the arriving. And it is not permissible that it causes its non-existence in the state of its existence, for we say: does it exist in this substrate (maḥall) or not in a substrate (or in a substrate) other? If he says: it exists in this, then this is impossible, because its existence in this substrate before the removal of the first from the substrate is impossible, because it entails the persistence of two opposites (ḍiddayn) in existence. And if they meet in existence, they are not opposites. And if it exists not in a substrate, then the existence of an accident not in a substrate is also impossible. And its existence not in a substrate of this opposite does not cause its non-existence, because its existence as an opposite not in the substrate of its opposite does not cause the non-existence of its opposite if it exists in another substrate. That does not cause the non-existence of the persistent accident, because blackness existing in a substrate does not cause the non-existence of whiteness established in another substrate until it transfers to this substrate. And its transfer to this substrate is inconceivable due to the impossibility of transfer from substrate to substrate for accidents. And because this arriving accident according to them is a cause for the non-existence of the persistent, and it is from their principle that the cause precedes the effect. So the existence of the arriving precedes the non-existence of the existent, then it does not cause its non-existence as long as it has not transferred to it for the reason mentioned. Then it transfers to it and then makes it non-existent in the second moment, leading to the conjunction of two opposites in the substrate of the first opposite. And it is not permissible that it causes its non-existence in the second moment, for we say: it existed in any substrate such that it causes the non-existence of the persistent accident from the substrate in the second moment? And it divides the statement and refutes every division as we mentioned in the previous chapter. And because if their existence in one time were permissible, it would also be permissible in the second and third, since their mutual opposition in the second is not more appropriate than their mutual opposition in the first. And because the non-existence of the persistent in the second moment due to the existence of its opposite is not more appropriate than the non-existence of its opposite in the second moment due to its existence. And the second state for each of them is a state of persistence. So it is established by what we have laid down that if an accident were persistent by itself, its non-existence would be inconceivable. And since we see the succession of opposing accidents in substrates as a witness, we know the invalidity of their statement necessarily, unless they deny the non-existence of accidents and claim for them appearance and concealment (al-ẓuhūr wa al-kumūn). Then they would join their brothers the materialists (al-dahriyya). And the invalidity of that has already passed, moreover they do not dare to show that for fear of the disgrace of the sword. And likewise, according to their school, it is not possible to affirm the annihilation of bodies, for it is from the school of those who debate with them among the Jubba'iyya and Bahshamiyya that the annihilation of bodies occurs only by the occurrence of annihilation (al-fanā') which is a newly occurring accident not in a substrate, opposite to bodies and atoms, so it causes their non-existence in the second moment. And the occurrence of an accident not in a substrate is impossible. And there is no way for annihilation according to them except this. So if it becomes impossible to affirm the permissibility of the annihilation of bodies, atoms, and accidents all together, and for this al-Jāḥiẓ among them considered the annihilation of bodies impossible, and all rational people besides al-Jāḥiẓ agree that every existent to which non-existence is impossible is eternal (qadīm). So their statement leads to affirming the eternity of the world.
Source pages p. 803

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that accidents (a'rāḍ) can persist (baqā') by themselves, a position held by some Mu'tazilites. The argument proceeds in several steps. First, the author notes a logical principle: it is easier to repel something not yet established than to remove something already established. He then presents a decisive argument: when a new accident (al-ṭāri') is said to cause the non-existence of a persistent accident (al-bāqī), we ask: does it cause non-existence before its own existence, during its existence, or after? It cannot be before, because the non-existent cannot act. If during its existence, we ask: does it exist in the same substrate as the persistent accident? If so, that would entail the simultaneous existence of two opposites in one substrate, which is impossible. If it exists in a different substrate, its existence there cannot affect the persistent accident. If it exists not in a substrate at all, that is also impossible for accidents. Thus the new accident cannot cause the non-existence of the persistent one. Moreover, if it were possible for them to coexist for a moment, there would be no reason to prevent it in subsequent moments, leading to an infinite regress. The conclusion is that if accidents persisted by themselves, they could never cease to exist. But we observe the successive destruction of opposites in substrates, proving that they do not persist. If opponents resort to denying annihilation and claiming concealment (kumūn), they become materialists. Furthermore, the author shows that the same reasoning leads to the impossibility of annihilating bodies, since that would require a self-subsisting accident of annihilation, which is impossible. Hence, their view leads to the eternity of the world, which is absurd.

Key Terms
عرض
ضد
بقاء
طارئ
محل
عدم
قديم
دهرية
جبائية
بهشمية
جاحظ
فناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two opposites can coexist in the same substrate for an instant before one is removed.
Correction: The definition of opposites precludes simultaneous existence in the same substrate; if they coexisted, they would not be true opposites.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what would be the result if a new accident (al-ṭāri') were to cause the non-existence of a persistent accident (al-bāqī) in the same substrate?
A) The two opposites would coexist, which is impossible.
B) The persistent accident would cease to exist in the second moment.
C) The new accident would transfer to another substrate.
D) The persistent accident would become eternal.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's argument proves that accidents cannot persist, because if they did, nothing could annihilate them, leading to the absurdity of the world being eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You witness the constant succession of opposites—day and night, health and illness, joy and sorrow. Yet do you still act as if your current state will last forever? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that nothing in creation persists except its Creator?
?
The argument reveals that if accidents persisted, the world would be eternal and independent of God. Do you ever live as if the world is self-sufficient, forgetting that every moment you exist is by His will? What concrete step can you take today to remind yourself of your absolute dependence on Allah every second?
?
When confronting a logical refutation of your assumptions, do you submit to the truth or seek escape like the materialists who hid their denial? What is the state of your heart when you encounter evidence that contradicts your desires—are you willing to surrender your intellect to the truth revealed in this lesson?

First Opponent's Dilemma on the Timing of Persistence
Author presents first opponent's question about whether persistence occurs at origination or later.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed by opponents regarding when the persistence attribute (بقاء) originates relative to the substance.

Core Idea
The opponent's dilemma: persistence either starts too early or too late.

In defending their doctrine that persistence is caused by an attribute, the Maturidis face objections. The first opponent's dilemma questions the timing of this attribute's origination. Understanding this objection is crucial before examining the author's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
Read the Arabic passage carefully with the translation. Note that this is not the author's own view but an objection.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The dilemma presents two possibilities: either the persistence attribute originates simultaneously with the substance, or in a subsequent moment.
Look for the words 'حال حدوث' and 'ثاني'.
3
Understand the implications
Consider the logical consequences: if simultaneous, the substance persists from the start; if later, it did not persist in the first moment. This creates a problem for the theory.
The opponent argues neither case supports the theory properly.
4
Recognize this as a dilemma
A dilemma forces a choice between two undesirable options. Here, the opponent claims both options undermine the persistence attribute theory.
The author will later respond by showing the flaw in this dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر أسئلتهم التي يعولون عليها في إبطال قولنا، فمن ذلك قولهم: إن الباقي لو كان باقيًا ببقاء لم يخل البقاء من أن يكون حادثا في حال حدوثه أو في الثاني، فإن حدث في حال حدوث الجوهر وجب أن يكون باقيا فيه لوجود البقاء له، وإن كان حادثا في الثاني لم يبق به من الأول.
Then we mention their questions which they rely on to invalidate our statement. Among these is their saying: If the persistent [thing] were persistent by a persistence [attribute], then the persistence would not be free from being originated at the moment of its origination or in the second [moment]. If it occurs at the moment of the substance's origination, then it must be persistent in it due to the existence of persistence for it. And if it is originated in the second [moment], then it did not persist by it from the first [moment].
Source pages p. 805

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section where the author lists the opponents' arguments against the Maturidi theory that a persistent entity persists by means of a separate attribute called 'baqa' (persistence). The first dilemma is about the timing of the origination of this attribute. If the persistence attribute is originated at the exact same moment that the substance (jawhar) itself is originated, then the substance would immediately be persistent from that moment onward. But if the persistence attribute only arises in the second moment (i.e., after the substance exists), then the substance was not persistent in the first moment—contradicting the claim that it persists. The opponents present this as a dilemma: either the substance persists from the start (if the attribute is simultaneous) or it did not persist in the first moment (if the attribute comes later). The author does not answer this question in the provided text; it is simply the first in a series of objections.
