The Objection Against Motion and the Response
Author presents atheist objection about motion and the response equating it with persistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological dilemma posed by atheists regarding motion and the Maturidi response that equates motion with persistence as a solution.

Core Idea
Motion and persistence are both new accidents created at the moment of change.

This passage presents a classic dilemma from atheists: if motion occurs while the body is still in its first place, it doesn't move; if it occurs after arrival, motion is redundant. The answer, attributed to Abu al-Hudhayl and al-Ash'ari, resolves this by equating motion with persistence as accidents created anew in the second state.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the objection is presented as a dilemma with two horns.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection states that the motion of a moving body must occur either while the body is still in the first place or after it has arrived at the second place. Both lead to contradictions.
3
Examine the response
The response equates motion with persistence. Just as persistence is a new accident occurring in the second time, motion is a new accident occurring in the second place. This resolves the dilemma by locating motion only at the destination.
This view is attributed to Abu al-Hudhayl, a group of Mu'tazila, and al-Ash'ari.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: why would the atheist's objection undermine the reality of accidents? How does equating motion with persistence preserve the contingency of creation?
This connects to the broader Maturidi commitment to the temporality of all attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عنه : أن هذا سؤال مأخوذ من سؤال الملحدين في نفي الأعراض، وهو قولهم : لو كان الجسم متحركاً بحركة لم تخل حركتها من أن تكون حادثة في المكان الأول. أو تكون حادثة وهو في المكان الثاني، فإن حدثت الحركة والجسم في المكان الأول لكانت الحركة موجودة والجسم ليس بمتحرك ولم يجب خروجه عن مكانه لوجودها، وإن حدثت وهي في المكان الثاني استغن بكونه فيه عن حركة ينتقل بها إليه. ثم من أصحابنا من أجاب عن السؤالين جميعًا بجواب وحد وسوى بين الحركة والبقاء وقال : إن الحركة تحدث والجسم في المكان الثاني والبقاء يحدث في الوقت الثاني، وإنما كان كذلك لأن المتحرك إنما يكون متحركا بخروجه عن الأول وكونه في الثاني، واحتاج إلى حدوث الكون فيه لأن خروجه عن الأول نفى لكونه فيه ولا يحتاج إلى الكون في مكان ليس بكائن فيه. وهذا المذهب في الحركة مذهب أبي الهذيل وجماعة من المعتزلة، وإليه ذهب الأشعري. والباقي يكون باقيًا في الوقت الثاني واحتاج إلى البقاء فيه وليس ببقاء عن الأول إلى الثاني، وإنما يكون محدثًا في الأول غير باق ثم يكون باقيًا في الثاني بحدوث البقاء فيه.
The answer to it is: This is a question derived from the question of the atheists in denying accidents. It is their statement: If a body is moving with a motion, its motion is not free from either being originated in the first place or being originated while it is in the second place. If the motion occurs while the body is in the first place, the motion would exist while the body is not moving, and its departure from its place would not be necessary due to its existence. And if it occurs while it is in the second place, it would be independent of its being there from a motion by which it transfers to it. Then among our companions some answered both questions with one answer and made motion and persistence equal, saying: Motion occurs while the body is in the second place, and persistence occurs in the second time. It is like that because the moving entity is only moving by its departure from the first and its being in the second, and it needs the origination of the kawn in it because its departure from the first negates its being in it, and it does not need the kawn in a place in which it is not present. This school regarding motion is the school of Abu al-Hudhayl and a group of the Mu'tazila, and al-Ash'ari followed it. And the enduring entity endures in the second time and needs persistence in it, and it is not a persistence from the first to the second; rather it is originated in the first, not enduring, then it endures in the second by the origination of persistence in it.
Source pages p. 806

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from atheists regarding the concept of motion. The objection poses a dilemma: if motion occurs while the body is still in the first place, the body would not be moving; if it occurs when the body is already in the second place, no motion is needed. The author then gives the response of some companions who equate motion with persistence. They argue that just as persistence is a new accident created at the second time, motion is a new accident created at the second place. The moving entity departs from the first location and requires a new 'kawn' (state of being) in the second location. This view is attributed to Abu al-Hudhayl and al-Ash'ari. The response resolves the dilemma by asserting that motion exists only in the second place, not in the first, similar to how persistence exists only in the second time.

Motion as Two Accidents (Kawns)
Author presents the two-kawn view of motion with proponents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative kalam view that motion consists of two distinct accidents (kawns) rather than a single accident.

Core Idea
Motion is completed only when two successive 'kawns' exist.

In the previous lesson, we encountered the view that motion is a single accident, which raised questions about its existence across consecutive locations. Here, the author introduces a different approach: motion is not one accident but two, called 'kawns,' each occupying a different location. This view aims to resolve the logical difficulty by explaining motion as a composite process.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author distinguishes this view from the earlier one by redefining motion as two accidents.
The term 'kawn' (كون) means a state of being or occurrence.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the terms 'kawnān' (two kawns), 'ḥarakah' (motion), and 'baqā'' (persistence). Understand how they relate in this view.
3
Understand the argument
The view says motion is a name for two accidents: one in the first place and one in the second. Motion is not considered to exist until the second accident occurs. This avoids the problem of motion being in neither location.
This is a direct response to the earlier question about motion's existence.
4
Note the proponents
The author names Ibn al-Rawandi (later in his life) and Abu al-'Abbas al-Qalanisi and a group of mutakallimun as holding this view.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this view preserves the idea that motion is an accident while addressing the logical difficulty of a single accident spanning two locations.
The author promises further clarification later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من فرق بين الحركة والبقاء، وأجاب عن سؤال الحركة أن ليس باسم لعرض واحد بل هو اسم لعرضين وهما كونان أحدهما في المكان الأول، والآخر في المكان الثاني، فما لم يوجد الثاني لا يكون متحركًا فلا تكون الحركة موجودة في المكان الأول ولا في الثاني بل يتم في الثاني، وإليه ذهب ابن الراوندي في آخر عمره، وهو مذهب أبي العباس القلانسي وجماعة من المتكلمين: يتبين ذلك بعد هذا إن شاء الله تعالى.
Among them is one who differentiated between motion and persistence, and answered the question of motion by saying that it is not a name for a single accident, but rather it is a name for two accidents, which are two kawns: one in the first place and the other in the second place. As long as the second does not exist, it is not moving, so motion does not exist in the first place nor in the second; rather it is completed in the second. Ibn al-Rawandi in his later life adhered to this, and it is the school of Abu al-'Abbas al-Qalanisi and a group of the mutakallimun. This will become clear after this, God willing.
Source pages p. 806

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative view that distinguishes motion from persistence. Unlike the first view that motion is a single accident, this view holds that motion is actually two distinct accidents: one 'kawn' (state of being) in the first location and another in the second. Motion is not considered to exist until the second 'kawn' occurs. This avoids the earlier dilemma because motion is not present entirely in either location alone. The author attributes this view to Ibn al-Rawandi later in life, and to Abu al-'Abbas al-Qalanisi and others.

Abu Mansur's Definitions of Motion and Rest
Author reports Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's definitions of motion and rest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's definitions of motion and rest as single kawns and the reasoning behind them.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are each a single kawn, differing only by what preceded them.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi reports the view of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi on motion and rest. Abu Mansur defines motion as 'zawaal' (departure) and rest as 'qaraar' (settling), and argues that each is a single accident (kawn) rather than two. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the Maturidi position on physical accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the terms zawaal, qaraar, and kawn.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where Abu Mansur defines motion and rest. Note that motion is departure from a place, rest is settling in a place.
These are not mere absence but active attributes.
3
Follow the argument about a single kawn
The passage explains why motion and rest cannot be two kawns. Understand the reasoning that two kawns cannot exist simultaneously.
The conclusion is that each state has only one kawn, classified by its predecessor.
4
Reflect on the school
The verifiers note that this view leans toward the first school. Consider what this implies about Maturidi's position.
This connects to broader kalam debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكثير ما يذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور وأكثر عباراته في هذا: أن الحركة زوال والسكون قرار ، وهو عند المحققين ميل إلى المذهب الأول إذ الزوال كون واحد وهو الحاصل في المكان الثاني إذ به زال عن الأول، وكذا القرار كون واحد إذ به صار مستقرا ، ولو كان السكون كونين وكذا الحركة لما تصور كون ذات ما متحركًا ولا ساكنا لاستحالة وجود كونين في حالة واحدة فإذا لا يوجد في كل حال إلا كون واحد غير أنه إن كان قبله كون آخر في مكان آخر كان الكون الثاني زوالاً ، وإن كان قبله كون آخر في هذا المكان فهو قرار.
Frequently what Shaykh Abu Mansur goes to and most of his expressions in this is: Motion is departure (zawaal) and rest is settling (qaraar). According to the verifiers, this leans towards the first school, since departure is a single kawn, which is obtained in the second place, for by it he departed from the first. Likewise, settling is a single kawn, for by it he became settled. If rest were two kawns, and likewise motion, it would not be conceivable for an entity to be moving or resting, due to the impossibility of two kawns existing in one state. Thus, in every state there exists only one kawn, except that if there was before it another kawn in another place, the second kawn is departure, and if before it was another kawn in this place, it is settling.
Source pages p. 807

Commentary
This passage reports the opinion of Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi regarding motion and rest. He defines motion as 'zawaal' (departure from a place) and rest as 'qaraar' (settling in a place). According to the verifiers, this view actually aligns with the first school (which sees motion as a single accident), because departure is a single 'kawn' in the second location, and settling is a single 'kawn' in the same location. The passage explains that having two 'kawns' simultaneously is impossible, so only one 'kawn' exists per state. The classification of that 'kawn' as motion or rest depends on what preceded it: if a previous 'kawn' was in another place, the current 'kawn' is departure (motion); if the previous 'kawn' was in the same place, it is settling (rest).

سكون
قرار
كون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many students think motion and rest are two separate accidents (kawns).
Correction: Abu Mansur shows they are each a single kawn; the difference is only in what preceded it.

Checkpoint

B) The absence of motion.
C) A single kawn called qaraar (settling).

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest are each a single kawn; the classification depends on the location of the preceding kawn.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What does the preceding 'kawn' — your previous choice or habit — say about your present? Are you satisfied with this classification?

Persistence as a Single Accident and the Objection Answered
Nasafi defines persistence and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of persistence as a single accident and how the author defends this view against an objection.

Core Idea
Persistence is a single accident that describes continuation of existence.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla defines persistence as a single accident and addresses a key objection. The author clarifies that persistence is not present at the first moment but rather describes the second moment. Then he turns the objection back on the deniers of persistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses technical kalam terms like بقاء and عرض.
2
Understand the definition of persistence
Identify that persistence is defined as a single accident, namely perpetual duration and continuation of existence. Note the distinction between the first and second moments.
3
Follow the objection and answer
Read the objection: if persistence is created, why couldn't the body exist without it? Then follow the answer based on the necessity of the description.
4
See the counter-objection
Notice how the author turns the question back: if persistence were by itself, the body could exist without it, which is impossible, so the objection fails.
This is a classic dialectical move in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما في البقاء فهو اسم لعرض واحد وهو الدوام واستمرار الوجود، ولن يوجد الأول في الثاني (ولا) يكون باقيًا في الوقت الأول بل يكون باقيًا في الثاني. ومما سألوا نحو قولهم : إن الجسم لو كان باقيًا ببقاء يخلق له لجاز أن يكون موجودًا وقتين فأكثر، ولا يخلق له البقاء، ويخلق فيه من جنس العرض الذي كان موجودًا في حال الحدوث، ويوجد أوقاتاً غير باق؟ والجواب عنه أن يقال: إذا استحال وجوده وقتين لا يوصف بالبقاء استحال أن لا يخلق فيه المعنى الذي من الوصف له به كالجسم لما استحال تعريه عن الأعراض استحال وجوده وقتًا ولا يخلق فيه شيء منه. ثم يعكس عليهم هذا السؤال فيقال : لو كان الجسم باقيًا لنفسه أو لا لنفسه أو لا لمعنى لجاز أن يكون موجودًا وقتين غير موصوف بهذا الوصف، ولما استحال ذلك عند من نفى البقاء بطل ما أوردوه من السؤال.
As for persistence, it is a name for a single accident, which is perpetual duration and continuation of existence. The first will not exist in the second, and it does not endure in the first time; rather it endures in the second. Among what they asked is their statement: If the body endures by a persistence created for it, it would be possible for it to exist for two times or more without persistence being created for it, and there would be created in it of the same kind of accident that existed at the time of origination, and it would exist times without enduring? The answer to it is to say: If it is impossible for it to exist for two times without being described by persistence, it is impossible that the meaning by which it is described is not created in it, just as when it is impossible for the body to be devoid of accidents, it is impossible for it to exist at a time without something of them being created in it. Then this question is turned back upon them, and it is said: If the body endures by itself, or not by itself, or not by a meaning, it would be possible for it to exist for two times not described by this description. Since that is impossible according to those who deny persistence, what they have asked becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 807

Commentary
This passage first defines persistence as a single accident, namely perpetual duration and continuation of existence. It clarifies that persistence is not present in the first time but only in the second time. Then it presents an objection: If a body endures by a created persistence, it could exist for multiple times without that persistence being created. The answer is that if a body cannot exist without being described by persistence, then it cannot lack the accident that gives it that description. This is similar to how a body cannot exist without accidents. The passage then turns the objection back against those who deny persistence: if the body endured by itself or without a cause, it could exist without being described by persistence, but that is impossible, thus their objection collapses.

عرض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Persistence is not a distinct accident but merely a label for continued existence.
Correction: The passage argues persistence is a real accident that is created, making the body endure in the second moment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does persistence apply to the body?
A) In the first moment of existence.
B) In the second moment of existence.
C) In all moments equally.
D) Only in the third moment.

Key Takeaway
Persistence is a single accident that makes the body endure in the second moment, and denying it leads to logical inconsistency.

?
The author turns the question back on the opponents, showing that denying persistence leads to impossibility. Is there an area of your faith where you have resisted a truth because it felt uncomfortable? Will you let logical consistency and divine revelation guide your heart, even when it challenges your assumptions?

Motion and Rest as Single States
Maturidi defines motion and rest as single states and proves their atomic nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of motion and rest as single instantaneous states (akwān) and why each moment contains only one state.

Core Idea
Every moment contains exactly one state – motion or rest – defined by what came before.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key Maturidi doctrine: motion and rest are not continuous processes but discrete atomic states. He argues that in any given instant, only one state (kawn) exists, and its classification as motion or rest depends on the preceding state. This resolves a conceptual puzzle about how an entity can be described as moving or resting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the key terms: حركة, سكون, كون, زوال, قرار.
2
Understand the core claim
The author attributes to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi the view that motion is leaving (zawāl) and rest is settlement (qarār). Both are defined as a single state (kawn wāḥid).
3
Grasp the argument against two states
If motion or rest were two states, an entity could not be described as moving or resting at a single moment because two states cannot coexist in one condition. Hence only one state exists per moment.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
See the relational classification
The classification of that single state as motion or rest depends on the previous state: if the previous state was in a different place, it is motion (zawāl); if in the same place, it is rest (qarār).
Thus motion and rest are relational but atomic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكثير ما يذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور وأكثر عباراته في هذا: أن الحركة زوال والسكون قرار ، وهو عند المحققين ميل إلى المذهب الأول إذ الزوال كون واحد وهو الحاصل في المكان الثاني إذ به زال عن الأول، وكذا القرار كون واحد إذ به صار مستقرا ، ولو كان السكون كونين وكذا الحركة لما تصور كون ذات ما متحركًا ولا ساكنا لاستحالة وجود كونين في حالة واحدة فإذا لا يوجد في كل حال إلا كون واحد غير أنه إن كان قبله كون آخر في مكان آخر كان الكون الثاني زوالاً ، وإن كان قبله كون آخر في هذا المكان فهو قرار.
And much of what Abu Mansur (al-Maturidi) holds and most of his expressions in this are: that motion is leaving (zawāl) and rest is settling (qarār). And according to the verifiers (al-muhaqqiqīn), it is an inclination toward the first opinion, for leaving (zawāl) is a single state (kawn wāḥid) which occurs in the second place, because by it one departs from the first. Likewise, rest (qarār) is a single state (kawn wāḥid), because by it one becomes settled. And if rest were two states (kawnayn) and likewise motion, it would not be conceivable that an entity be moving or resting, due to the impossibility of two states existing in a single condition. So in every condition, only one state exists, except that if before it there was another state in a different place, the second state is leaving (zawāl), and if before it there was another state in this [same] place, it is rest (qarār).
Source pages p. 807

Commentary
This passage defines motion and rest as single instances or states (akwān). The author begins by attributing the view that motion (ḥaraka) is leaving (zawāl) and rest (sukūn) is settlement (qarār) to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and his school. The author notes that the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) incline toward the first opinion, which treats both as single states (kawn wāḥid). The argument proceeds: leaving is the state that occurs in the second place, since one departs from the first place by it. Rest is similarly a single state by which one becomes settled. If rest or motion were two states, the same entity could not be described as moving or resting simultaneously, because two states cannot coexist in one condition. Thus, in every moment, only one state exists. However, the classification of that state as motion or rest depends on what preceded it: if the previous state was in a different place, the current state is motion (zawāl); if the previous state was in the same place, it is rest (qarār). This shows the relational nature of motion and rest while affirming their unity as temporal states.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion and rest are continuous processes that unfold over time.
Correction: In Maturidi kalam, motion and rest are discrete atomic states (akwān); each moment contains exactly one state, and the classification depends on the preceding state's location.

Checkpoint
According to the verifiers, how many states (akwān) exist in each moment?
A) Two states: motion and rest
B) One state, either motion or rest
C) Motion is two states, rest is one state
D) Neither motion nor rest are states

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest are each a single state (kawn) in a given moment, classified by the contextual relationship to the preceding state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every moment of your life is a single state determined by what came before. Right now, what is the state of your heart? Is it 'motion' drawing closer to Allah, or 'rest' in spiritual stagnation? What one choice can you make in this very moment to change that state?
?
The argument shows that two contradictory states cannot coexist in one condition. Are you trying to hold onto both love of this world and love of the Hereafter simultaneously? If not, which one will you allow to define your next state?
?
If motion and rest are defined by the previous state, consider your actions today. Looking at your last action, is the present moment one of 'zawāl' leaving sin or 'qarār' settling into it? What will you do now to ensure your next state is one of movement toward Allah?

The Objection on Created Permanence and Its Answer
The author defines permanence, presents an objection, and refutes it with logical inversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the necessity of a created permanence for contingent beings against the objection that a body could exist without it.

Core Idea
Permanence is not self-sustaining; it must be created each moment.

This passage tackles a key objection in kalam: if a body is permanent by a created accident, couldn't it exist for multiple moments without that accident? The author provides a clear logical inversion to show that the objector's own assumptions lead to impossibility. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the Maturidi position on the createdness of permanence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the full translation. Pay attention to the definition of permanence and the structure of the objection and answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the definition of permanence
Find how the author defines permanence (baqā') and note that it is a single accident (ʻaraḍ) meaning duration. Also note the distinction between the first moment and subsequent moments.
Permanence is not a property of the first instant of existence.
3
Understand the objection
The objector argues: if a body is permanent by a created accident, it could exist for two times without that accident, because it could still have an accident of the same type. This would lead to a body existing without being permanent.
The objector is trying to show a contradiction in the idea of created permanence.
4
Follow the author's answer
The author replies: it is impossible for a body to exist for two times without being described by permanence, so the meaning (ma'nā) must be created. He uses an analogy with other accidents.
The analogy shows that just as a body cannot exist without accidents, it cannot exist without a permanence accident.
5
Examine the inversion argument
The author then inverts the objection: if permanence were intrinsic (by itself), the body could exist for two times without being described by permanence — which is impossible even for the objector. Thus the objection fails.
This inversion is powerful because it uses the objector's own framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما في البقاء فهو اسم لعرض واحد وهو الدوام واستمرار الوجود، ولن يوجد الأول في الثاني ولا يكون باقيًا في الوقت الأول بل يكون باقيًا في الثاني. ومما سألوا نحو قولهم : إن الجسم لو كان باقيًا ببقاء يخلق له لجاز أن يكون موجودًا وقتين فأكثر، ولا يخلق له البقاء، ويخلق فيه من جنس العرض الذي كان موجودًا في حال الحدوث، ويوجد أوقاتاً غير باق؟ والجواب عنه أن يقال: إذا استحال وجوده وقتين لا يوصف بالبقاء استحال أن لا يخلق فيه المعنى الذي من الوصف له به كالجسم لما استحال تعريه عن الأعراض استحال وجوده وقتًا ولا يخلق فيه شيء منه. ثم يعكس عليهم هذا السؤال فيقال : لو كان الجسم باقيًا لنفسه أو لا لنفسه أو لا لمعنى لجاز أن يكون موجودًا وقتين غير موصوف بهذا الوصف، ولما استحال ذلك عند من نفى البقاء بطل ما أوردوه من السؤال.
As for permanence (baqā'), it is a name for a single accident, which is duration (dawām) and continuation of existence. The first (the contingent) will not exist in the second (the permanent), nor is it permanent in the first time, but it is permanent in the second [time]. And among the questions they raised is their statement: If a body were permanent by a permanence created in it, it would be possible for it to exist for two times or more without permanence being created for it, and [yet] there would be created in it something of the same kind as the accident which existed in the state of origination, and it would exist at times without being permanent. The answer to this is to say: If its existence for two times without being described by permanence is impossible, then it is impossible that the meaning (al-ma'nā) by which it is described not be created in it, just as when a body's being devoid of accidents is impossible, its existence for a time without anything created in it is impossible. Then this question is inverted against them: If the body were permanent by itself or not by itself or not by a meaning, it would be possible for it to exist for two times without being described by this description. And since that is impossible according to the one who negates permanence, what they adduced of the question is invalidated.
Source pages p. 807

Commentary
This passage handles the concept of permanence (baqā') and an objection against the createdness of permanence. The author first defines permanence as a single accident (ʻaraḍ) meaning duration and continuation of existence. He notes that the contingent is not permanent in its first moment but becomes permanent in a subsequent moment. The objector argues: if a body is permanent by a created permanence, it could exist for two moments without that permanence being created, because it could still exist as an accident of the same type; this would lead to a body existing without being permanent. The answer: it is impossible for the body to exist for two moments without being described by permanence, so it is impossible that the principle (ma'nā) by which it is described not be created in it. This is analogous to the impossibility of a body existing at a moment without any accidents. The author then turns the question around: if the body were permanent by itself or by no meaning, it could exist for two moments without being described by permanence. Since this is impossible even for the one denying created permanence (i.e., the objector), the objection collapses. This shows the necessity of a created permanence for contingent existence over time.

Misconception: Permanence is an intrinsic property of matter and does not require a created accident.
Correction: The author argues that permanence is a created accident; otherwise, a body could exist without being described as permanent, which is impossible.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

?
The author argues that permanence must be an accident because without it, a body cannot exist even for two moments. If your very 'permanence' is not your own but is given to you, what rights do you have to claim anything as 'yours'—your time, your health, your faith? How should you respond to the One who holds your persistence in His hands?

Two Questions Against the Proof: Power and Act
The author enumerates two questions objecting to his proof, one concerning power and one concerning act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two possible objections to the kalam proof regarding the persistence of power and the continuity of the act across time.

Core Idea
Two objections test the consistency of allowing a time gap between power and act.

In theological reasoning, it is crucial to anticipate objections. The author here raises two critical questions that challenge his own proof. These questions probe the relationship between power and its act, and whether a temporal gap between them can be extended indefinitely. This lesson unpacks both objections and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two questions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the author introduces two distinct questions: one from the aspect of power and one from the aspect of act.
Pay attention to the structure: 'أحدهما من حيث القدرة' and 'والثاني: من حيث الفعل'.
2
Compare the two questions
The first question asks why power cannot be free of its act for multiple times if it can be free for one time. The second asks why an act cannot be separated from its power by more than one time.
Both questions challenge the idea that only a single time gap is permissible.
3
Reflect on the instrument analogy
The author reformulates the second question using an instrument like a hand or knife. If the instrument is absent, the act cannot occur whether the absence is for one time or ten.
This analogy shows that any non-existence of the cause makes the effect impossible, regardless of duration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويخرج هذا الكلام على طريق السؤال من وجهين: أحدهما من حيث القدرة، وهو أن القدرة لما لم تكن إلا للفعل وقد تخلو عنه وقتا لم لا يجوز أن تخلو عنه أوقاتا؟ وهذا السؤال يرد أيضا على من يقول ببقاء القدرة من البصريين. والثاني: من حيث الفعل، وهو أنه لما جاز الفصل بالقدرة في الوقت الثاني وإن كانت القدرة فيه منعدمة لم لا يجوز الفعل بها في العاشر وإن كانت منعدمة وتقرير السؤال بالآلة كاليد والسكين وغيرهما، أنه لما استحال حصول الفعل بهما بعد العدم في الوقت العاشر من زمان العدم، فكذا استحال في الثاني منه لاستواء الوقتين في العدم
And this statement comes out as a question in two ways: one of them is from the aspect of power, which is that since power exists only for an act and may be free of it at one time, why is it not permissible that it be free of it for multiple times? And this question also applies to those who say power persists among the Basrans. And the second is from the aspect of the act, which is that since separation by power in the second time is permissible even though power is non-existent in it, why is the act not permissible by it in the tenth time even though it is non-existent? And the settlement of the question by an instrument like the hand and the knife and others is that since the occurrence of an act by them after non-existence in the tenth time of the time of non-existence is impossible, so likewise it is impossible in the second of it because of the equality of the two times in non-existence.
Source pages p. 814

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of two questions that can be raised against the author's proof. The first question concerns power: if a power can be free of its act at one time (i.e., exist without producing the act at that moment), why can it not be free for multiple times? This question also applies to the Basran view that power persists. The second question concerns the act: if it is permissible for an act to be separated from the power that produced it by one time (i.e., the power is absent at the time of the act but was present earlier), why cannot it be separated by ten times? The author then reformulates the question in terms of an instrument (like a hand or knife): if the instrument is absent at the time of the act, it is impossible for the act to occur whether the absence is for one time or ten times, because the times are equal in non-existence. Thus the two questions challenge the consistency of allowing a single time gap but not multiple gaps.

فعل
آلة
يد
سكين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These questions are the author's own objections to his proof.
Correction: The author is raising possible objections to test his own position, not necessarily endorsing them.

Checkpoint
What is the second question about?
A) It concerns the persistence of power over time.
B) It concerns why an act cannot be performed by a power that was absent for multiple times if it can be performed by a power absent for one time.
C) It concerns the nature of the hand as an instrument.
D) It concerns the equality of all times in non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The two questions expose a tension: if a gap of one time is allowed between power and act, why not a gap of multiple times?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have the power to do a good deed now but delay it, what does that delay reveal about your sincerity? How does this lesson's focus on the necessity of power and act being simultaneous challenge your tendency to postpone obedience?
?
The author uses an instrument analogy: a knife cannot cut if absent. What 'instruments' in your life—like health, time, or wealth—are you assuming will be available later, while neglecting to use them now for what matters most?
?
Consider the question from the aspect of act: if an act can occur after the power is gone, then why delay any act of worship? Are you living as if the opportunity to act will always be there, or are you seized by the urgency of the present moment?

Two Distinctions on Time and the Author's Initial Reply
Opponents raise two distinctions; author begins reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinctions opponents make to differentiate between a single time and multiple times, and how the author begins his reply by redefining motion.

Core Idea
Opponents claim momentary freedom from motion/rest; the author replies by redefining motion.

This passage presents two objections from opponents who try to block the author's previous argument by distinguishing between a single moment and multiple moments. The author begins his reply by showing that the first objection only applies under certain definitions of motion, but his argument is cut short. We'll examine the two distinctions and the start of the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source Arabic once to get the rhythm, then read the translation. Notice the two distinctions clearly marked with 'أحدهما' and 'والآخر'.
2
Identify the two distinctions
The opponents raise two points: (1) a body can be free of motion and rest at the first moment but not later; (2) a healthy person is not capable of acting at the first moment but never becomes capable afterward. Write them down.
The second distinction is briefly stated; it may refer to an earlier argument about power.
3
Understand the author's reply strategy
The author says the first objection only applies to some opponents, not all. For those it appears to apply, it is either sophistry or ignorance. He then explains by analyzing the definition of motion.
The reply hinges on whether motion is defined as a single mode or two modes.
4
Examine the definition of motion
If motion is a single mode (kawn) in the second time with the condition of no mode in another place, then at the first moment the body has a mode of the same genus, so it is not free of motion-genus. Thus the objection fails.
The author's full reply is cut off; this is only the beginning.
5
Reflect on the incomplete argument
Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence. The author was about to conclude his response. Consider how this incomplete argument affects the overall discussion.
The source_needs_review flag indicates the answer continues on the next page.

فاعترضوا على هذا الكلام بفصلين في كل واحد منهما للتفرقة بين وقت واحد وبين أوقات كثيرة. أحدهما: أن الجسم يجوز خلوه عن الحركة والسكون بل يجب ذلك في أول أحوال وجوده ثم يستحيل ذلك في الوقت الثاني والثالث. والآخر أن الصحيح والسليم غير قادر على الفعل أول كونه ثم لم يجز أبدًا. والجواب: أن السؤال الأول يتوجه على بعض الخصوم دون البعض، ومن توجه عليه من حيث الظاهر فهو إما مغالطة منهم مع علمهم أنه غير لازم، وإما جهل بالحقائق. وبيان هذا أن من قال الحركة اسم لكون واحد وهو الكون الموجود في الزمان الثاني إلا أن شرط استحقاقه هذا الاسم بعدم كون في مكان آخر، والجسم في أول أحوال وجوده لا يخلو عن كون، وهو من جنس ما يسمى حركة إلا أنه لا يسمى به لفقد شرط وهو بعدم كون له في مكان آخر، فإذا لم يخل الجسم في أول أحوال الوجود عما هو من جنس الحركة فلم يكن هذا الاعتراض متوجها عليهم. وإنما يتوجه من حيث الظاهر على من يقول: إن الحركة اسم لكونين في مكانين والسكون اسم لكونين في مكان واحد، وشرط الكونين زمانان لأنهما فعلان فلا يوجدان إلا في زمانين، إذ في كل زمان يوجد فيه فصل واحد، والجسم في أول أحوال وجوده لا يخلو عن كون وهو فعل واحد، وهم ألزموا خلو القدرة عن
So they objected to this statement with two distinctions, in each one of them, to differentiate between one time and many times. One of them: that the body may be free of motion and rest, rather that is necessary in the first state of its existence, then that becomes impossible in the second and third time. The other: that the healthy and sound person is not capable of the act at the beginning of his being, then it is never permissible afterwards. The answer: that the first question applies to some of the opponents and not others, and the one to whom it applies in appearance, it is either a sophistry from them with their knowledge that it is not necessary, or ignorance of realities. And elucidation of this: that whoever says that motion is a name for one mode (kawn), which is the mode existing in the second time, except that the condition for its deserving this name is by the non-existence of a mode in another place, and the body in the first state of its existence is not free of a mode, and it is of the genus of what is called motion except that it is not called by it due to the loss of a condition, which is the non-existence of a mode for it in another place, so since the body is not devoid in the first state of existence of what is of the genus of motion, then this objection is not directed against them. Rather, it applies in appearance to those who say: motion is a name for two modes in two places, and rest is a name for two modes in one place, and the condition of the two modes is two times because they are two acts, so they exist only in two times, since in each time only one part exists, and the body in the first state of its existence is not free of a mode, which is one act, and they required that the power be empty of...
Source pages p. 815

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The opponents raise two distinctions to differentiate between one time and many times, trying to block the author's argument. First distinction: a body can be free of motion and rest (i.e., have no attributes) at the first moment of its existence, but that becomes impossible in subsequent moments. Second distinction: a healthy person is not capable of an act at the very beginning of his existence, but afterwards he becomes capable, and this capacity does not persist? Actually the text is brief. Then the author gives his answer: the first question (i.e., the opponents' distinction) only applies to some opponents, not all. For those to whom it appears to apply, it is either sophistry or ignorance. He explains by discussing the definition of motion: if motion is defined as a single mode (kawn) in the second time with the condition of no mode in another place, then at the first moment the body does have a mode (of the same genus as motion) so it is not devoid of motion-genus; thus the objection fails. But if motion is defined as two modes in two places, then the body at the first moment has only one mode, so it is not motion, but the author argues that the opponents' own position (as previously stated) required that power be empty of... (the text cuts off). Thus the answer is incomplete in this window.

Correction: The distinctions are about physical bodies and human capacity, not divine attributes. The author's reply hinges on technical definitions of motion in physical bodies.

What is the first distinction raised by the opponents?
A) A body can be free of motion and rest at the first moment but not later.
B) A healthy person is not capable at the beginning but becomes capable.
C) Power can be absent for many times if it can be absent for one time.
D) Motion and rest are defined by two modes in two places.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' attempt to differentiate one time from many times fails for those who define motion as a single mode at a specific time, but the full reply requires further reading.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponents argued that a person is not capable of an act at the very beginning of his existence. Is there an act of obedience or repentance that you have been postponing, telling yourself that you are not yet ready? Based on this lesson, what might be the real condition that makes an action possible, and are you truly incapable or simply unwilling?
?
The author calls the opponents' objection either sophistry or ignorance. Think of a time when you used a clever-sounding excuse to avoid a duty. Was it sophistry? What reality were you ignoring?
?

Have you ever tried to claim that a certain moment of your life was a 'blank slate' where you were free from accountability? What mode were you actually in?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila Objection on Motion and Rest
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila objection on motion/rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila objection by clarifying the definitions of motion and rest and exposing the faulty analogy with power (qudra).

Core Idea
Motion and rest are not simple states but require multiple akwan across time.

The Mu'tazila raised a clever objection against the view that the body always has a state (kun) at every moment. They argued that the body can be devoid of motion and rest in its first moment but not later, implying a contradiction. Nasafi's response is a masterclass in precision: he shows that the objection only applies to certain definitions of motion and rest, and that the analogy with divine power (qudra) is invalid. In this passage, you will see how careful attention to technical terminology resolves a theological puzzle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the two-part objection from the Mu'tazila and the distinction they try to make between first and later moments.
Note the two divisions: (1) body can be devoid of motion/rest in first moment, (2) healthy person not capable of action at first moment.
2
Analyze Nasafi's response
See how he divides the objection based on different definitions of motion and rest, and refutes each case.
Focus on the distinction between those who define motion as a single akwan vs. two akwan.
3
Examine the analogy with power
Understand why the Mu'tazila's comparison with power (qudra) fails because the body always has a single akwan, while power is initially empty.
Contrast the body's permanent possession of a single akwan with power's initial emptiness.

اعتراض للمعتزلة
فاعترضوا على هذا الكلام بفصلين في كل واحد منهما للتفرقة بين وقت واحد وبين أوقات كثيرة.
أحدهما: أن الجسم يجوز خلوه عن الحركة والسكون بل يجب ذلك في أول أحوال وجوده ثم يستحيل ذلك في الوقت الثاني والثالث.
والآخر أن الصحيح والسليم غير قادر على الفعل أول كونه ثم لم يجز أبدًا.
والجواب: أن السؤال الأول يتوجه على بعض الخصوم دون البعض، ومن توجه عليه من حيث الظاهر فهو إما مغالطة منهم مع علمهم أنه غير لازم، وإما جهل بالحقائق.
وبيان هذا أن من قال الحركة اسم لكون واحد وهو الكون الموجود في الزمان الثاني إلا أن شرط استحقاقه هذا الاسم بعدم كون في مكان آخر، والجسم في أول أحوال وجوده لا يخلو عن كون، وهو من جنس ما يسمى حركة إلا أنه لا يسمى به لفقد شرط وهو بعدم كون له في مكان آخر، فإذا لم يخل الجسم في أول أحوال الوجود عما هو من جنس الحركة فلم يكن هذا الاعتراض متوجها عليهم.
وإنما يتوجه من حيث الظاهر على من يقول : إن الحركة اسم لكونين في مكانين والسكون اسم لكونين في مكان واحد، وشرط الكونين زمانان لأنهما فعلان فلا يوجدان إلا في زمانين، إذ في كل زمان يوجد فيه فعل واحد، والجسم في أول أحوال وجوده لا يخلو عن كون وهو فعل واحد، وهم ألزموا خلو القدرة عن الفعل أصلاً في زمان واحد فعارضوا بخلو الجسم عن فعلين في زمان واحد، وخلو الجسم عن كونين جائز في كل وقت من أوقات البقاء بل واجب كما هو جائز بل واجب في أول أحوال وجوده. فإذا قط لا يوجد في زمان ما للجسم إلا كون واحد، ولا يخلو عنه في زمان ما لا زمان وجوده ولا زمان بقائه.
فأما القدرة فإنها خالية عن الفعل حال وجودها وإن تحقق ذاتها، ويستحيل بعد ذلك خلوها عن الفعل وإن لم تتغير ذاتها، فالمعترض بفعل خلو الجسم عن الحركة والسكون إن كان عالما بما بينا من حقيقة الحركة والسكون واستحالة خلو الجسم في أول أوقاته عن كون، واستحالة وجود كونين في حال ما وإن كانت حالة البقاء وثبوت المساواة بين الأحوال أجمع في وجوب وجود كون فيه واستحالة وجود كونين فيه مع علمه بذلك اعترض بهذا الاعتراض؛ فهو مغالط، وإن لم يكن عالما بذلك كان السؤال صادرا عن الجهل بالحقائق.
وأجاب الشيخ أبو منصور - رحمه الله - وقال : إنما لم يجز أن يكون متحركًا أو ساكنا في أول أحوال وجوده لما فيه من الإحالة، فإن كل واحد منهما اسم كونين، وجودهما في أول وجوده محال، ولا إحالة في حالة البقاء فوقع الفرق. فأما فيما نحن فيه فلا استحالة، وقياس ما لا استحالة في ثبوته على ما لثبوته استحالة في المنع قياس من غير علة جامعة.
وإن قال فيما نحن فيه استحالة، فالواجب عليه بيان الاستحالة دون الاشتغال بإيراد النظر.
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن الإنسان متى يكون قادرًا على الحركة، يقدر
Objection of the Mu'tazila
They objected to this statement with two divisions, in each of which there is a distinction between a single time and many times.
One of them: that the body can be devoid of motion and rest, rather it is necessary in the first of its states of existence, then that becomes impossible in the second and third times.
The other: that the sound and healthy one is not capable of action in the first of his existence, then it never becomes permissible.
The answer: that the first question applies to some opponents but not others, and whoever it applies to apparently is either being sophistical with the knowledge that it is not necessary, or ignorant of realities.
The explanation of this is that whoever says motion is the name of a single akwan (atom of existence) which is the akwan existing in the second time, except that the condition for it deserving this name is the nonexistence of an akwan in another place, and the body in the first of its states of existence is not devoid of an akwan, which is of the genus of what is called motion but is not called by it due to the lack of a condition—that there is no akwan for it in another place. So since the body in the first of the states of existence is not devoid of what is of the genus of motion, this objection does not apply to them.
It applies only apparently to whoever says: motion is the name of two akwan in two places, and rest is the name of two akwan in one place, and the condition for the two akwan is two times because they are two actions so they exist only in two times, since in each time only one action exists, and the body in the first of its states of existence is not devoid of an akwan which is a single action. They [the Mu'tazila] required that power be devoid of action entirely in a single time, so they objected by the body being devoid of two actions in a single time. And the body’s being devoid of two akwan is permissible in every time of the times of permanence, rather obligatory, just as it is permissible, rather obligatory, in the first of its states of existence. For indeed, never does there exist at any time for the body except a single akwan, and it is not devoid of it at any time—neither the time of its existence nor the time of its permanence.
As for power, it is devoid of action at the moment of its existence even if its essence is realized, and after that it is impossible for it to be devoid of action even if its essence does not change. So the one who objects by the body’s being devoid of motion and rest—if he is knowing of what we have explained concerning the reality of motion and rest and the impossibility of the body being devoid of an akwan in its first times, and the impossibility of the existence of two akwan at any moment even if it is a state of permanence, and the establishment of equality among all states in the necessity of the existence of an akwan in it and the impossibility of the existence of two akwan in it—if with that knowledge he objects with this objection, then he is a sophist; and if he is not knowing of that, then the question arises from ignorance of realities.
And Shaykh Abu Mansur—may Allah have mercy on him—answered and said: It was not permissible for it to be moving or resting in the first of its states of existence due to the impossibility involved, for each of them is the name of two akwan, and their existence in the first of its existence is impossible, while there is no impossibility in the state of permanence, so the difference occurs. As for what we are dealing with, there is no impossibility, and analogizing that which has no impossibility in its affirmation to that whose affirmation has impossibility is an analogy without a common cause.
If he says that in what we are dealing with there is impossibility, then he must explain the impossibility without busying himself with raising the argument.
And by this they are answered concerning their saying: That a person, when he is capable of motion, is able
Source pages p. 815

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The Mu'tazila raise an objection in two parts to an earlier argument (likely about the body's need for an akwan at every moment). First objection: the body can be devoid of motion and rest in its first moment, but then it becomes impossible in later moments, so there is a distinction between one time and many times. Second objection: a healthy person is not capable of action at the first moment of his existence, but then it never becomes permissible (i.e., he remains incapable).

Nasafi's response is detailed. He first says the first objection applies only to some opponents, not all. Those who define motion as a single akwan (existence) existing in the second time, with the condition that there be no akwan in another place, do not face the objection because the body in its first moment has an akwan that is of the genus of motion but does not meet the condition for being called motion (since there is no prior akwan in another place). So the body is not devoid of an akwan entirely; it has a single akwan, so the objection does not apply.

The objection does apply apparently to those who define motion as two akwan in two places and rest as two akwan in one place, requiring two times. In the first moment, only one akwan exists. The Mu'tazila tried to draw an analogy with power (qudra) being devoid of action at the moment of its existence and then never being devoid again. But the body is never devoid of a single akwan (kun) at any time, and having a single akwan is permissible in every time, not just the first. The difference is that power is initially devoid of action and then later must act, while the body always has a single akwan and never has two. So the analogy fails.

Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi gives a concise answer: in the first moment, having motion or rest is impossible because each requires two akwan, but in later moments it is possible because two akwan can exist over two times. The analogy is invalid because it compares a case where impossibility applies (first moment) with a case where it does not (later), without a common cause. Finally, if the opponent insists there is an impossibility in our case, he must prove it rather than just presenting the analogy. This answers the broader objection about human capability at the first moment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion and rest are simple, self-evident attributes of the body that can be present or absent independently.
Correction: The passage shows that motion and rest are defined as specific configurations of akwan (atoms of existence) across time, and the body is never devoid of an akwan at any moment.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's response, why does the Mu'tazila's first objection not apply to those who define motion as a single akwan in the second time?
A) Because the body in its first moment is devoid of any akwan.
B) Because the body in its first moment has an akwan that is of the genus of motion but lacks the condition for being called motion.
C) Because motion cannot exist at all in the first moment.
D) Because the Mu'tazila agree that motion is a single akwan.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objection fails because it does not respect the precise technical definitions of motion and rest as requiring multiple akwan over time and overlooks the fact that the body always possesses a single akwan.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue about religious matters, are you more concerned with appearing correct or with truly understanding the reality? The Mu'tazila were knowledgeable but fell into sophistry because of their assumptions. What assumptions are you holding onto that might be keeping you from truth?
?
Shaykh Abu Mansur said that if the opponent is ignorant of the true definitions of motion and rest, his objection arises from ignorance. In what area of your own life are you confidently asserting something without having examined its foundations? Will you humble yourself to learn the basics before arguing?
?
The body always has at least one akwan — it is never truly empty. Your soul, too, is never empty of actions or states. What 'akwan' (actions, thoughts, habits) are you currently filling your time with? Are they leading you toward motion (growth) or rest (stagnation)? And what change could you make starting from this moment?

The Maturidi Response on Qudra in the First State
Author refutes an objection about qudra in the first state and presents Maturidi's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi defense of how capability (qudra) relates to the first state of existence, including the distinction based on the definition of movement and the principle that capability accompanies action.

Core Idea
Capability is inseparable from its act, but the first state has no act—so capability is absent by necessity, not chance.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against the Maturidi position on human capability. The objection attempts to force a dilemma regarding whether capability exists in the first place or the second place. The author provides two Maturidi responses, one based on the definition of movement and another based on the necessary connection between capability and act.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening dilemma about capability in the first or second place. Notice how it tries to force a contradiction.
The objection assumes that capability must precede or accompany action.
2
See how the objection depends on the definition of movement
The answer changes depending on whether movement is defined as one 'being' (kawn) in the second place or two beings in two places.
3
Examine the two Maturidi responses
One view equates capability with health of limbs; the other distinguishes the first state from later states. Focus on the second response, which is attributed to Imam al-Maturidi.
The first response is attributed to al-Ash'ari's refutation of al-Ka'bi.
4
Understand the distinction in the second response
In the first state, no act (movement or rest) exists, so capability is impossible. In later states, acts exist, so capability is possible. This distinction is based on an evidence of difference.
5
Grasp the methodological conclusion
The objection is invalid because capability can be devoid of its act at one time (as seen in other states), so absence of act does not prove impossibility.
This final paragraph reinforces the consistency of the Maturidi position.

عليها وهو في المكان الأول أم يقدر وهو في المكان الثاني؟ فإن قلتم : إنه يقدر وهو في المكان الثاني فقد أثبتم الانتقال من المكان الأول إلى المكان الثاني بلا قدرة، وهذا محال - وإن قلتم : إنه يقدر وهو في المكان الأول فقد أثبتم الاستطاعة قبل الحركة. فيقال لهم : هذا السؤال بناء على أن الحركة في أي مكان توجد؟ فمن قال: توجد في المكان الثاني فالقدرة عليها توجد في المكان الثاني - ومن قال إنها توجد فيهما القدرة توجد فيهما، وكشفه ما بينا أن من زعم أن الحركة اسم للكون فهي المكان الثاني ويستحق الاسم بشريطة تقدم كون آخر في مكان آخر قبله يقول : توجد قدرة الحركة في المكان الثاني. ومن قال: إن الحركة اسم لكونين في مكانين يقول : توجد قدرة الكون الأول في المكان الأول، وقدرة الكون الثاني في المكان الثاني إذ هما فعلان لابد لكل فعل من قدرة تقارنه، والله الموفق. وما زعم أن الصحيح السليم يخلو عن القدرة على الفعل في أول خلقه ثم لا يجوز بعد ذلك. قلنا: من زعم من أصحابنا أن الإنسان لا يكون صحيح الجوارح سليمًا إلا إذا كان قادراً، كان هذا السؤال على أصله محالاً، لأنه لما وجدت صحة الجوارح وجدت القدرة ضرورة في أي حالة كانت، فاندفع هذا السؤال على أصله، اعتمد الأشعري في كتابه الذي رد فيه كتاب أوائل الأدلة لأبي القاسم الكعبي. ومن قال منهم : إن الصحة والسلامة غير القدرة فإنه يقول: إنه يخلق في أول أحواله إما صحيحًا سليمًا قادرًا على الفعل، وإما صحيحًا سلميًا عاجزا عن الفعل، ويجوز أن يكون في الحالة الثانية وما بعدها هكذا ، فسوى بين الأحوال ولم يفرق بين الحالة الأولى وبين غيرها من الأحوال. وإليه ذهب الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي - رحمه الله - في كتاب التوحيد وذكر في كتابه الذي ورد في كتاب وائل الأدلة على الكعبي الفرق بين الحالة الأولى وبين غيرها من الأحوال وقال: إنما لم يجز أن يكون قادرًا لأن القدرة تكون مع الفعل وفعل المحدث هو إما الحركة وإما السكون ، ووجود كل واحد منهما محال في تلك الحالة فيستحيل ثبوت القدرة عليهما، ولا استحالة في ثبوت الحركة والسكون في غير تلك الحالة فلم يستحل ثبوت القدرة عليهما في غير تلك الحالة، ففرقنا بينهما لقيام دليل المفارقة وأنتم فيما نحن فيه تفرقون عند وجود دليل المساواة وانعدام دليل التفرقة. ثم هذا الجواب من الشيخ رحمه الله على طريق المساهلة وبناء الأمر على ما هو المتعارف من الفعل وهو الحركة والسكون فأما الكلام على الحقيقة على قول من يجعل الحركة والسكون كل واحد منهما كونين فهو أن كل حركة فعلان وكذا كل سكون وهو في ابتداء أحوال وجوده يفعل كونًا واحدًا، فيخرج على أحد الجوابين المتقدمين وهو التسوية بين الأحوال كلها إما جوازاً وإما منعًا، والله الموفق. على أن هذا الاعتراض فاسد على طريق النظر فإنا سوينا بين حالة الوجود وبين غيرها من الأحوال في الجانبين جميعًا - أعني جانب القدرة وجانب الفعل- فإنا قلنا في جانب القدرة : إنها لما لم تكن إلا للفعل وقد تخلو عنه وقتا مع وجود ذاتها فلم لا تخلو عنه أوقاتاً؟ إذ خلوها عنه وقتاً دليل أن ثبوت الفعل ليس من
upon it and is in the first place, or is he capable and is in the second place? If you say: he is capable and is in the second place, you have affirmed the transition from the first place to the second place without capability, and this is impossible. And if you say: he is capable and is in the first place, you have affirmed capability before movement. So it is said to them: This question is based on where movement occurs. For whoever says: it occurs in the second place, then capability for it exists in the second place. And whoever says: it occurs in both, then capability exists in both. Its clarification is what we have shown: that whoever claims that movement is a name for a 'being' in the second place, and deserves the name with the condition of a preceding another being in another place before it, says: the capability of movement exists in the second place. And whoever says: movement is a name for two beings in two places says: the capability of the first being exists in the first place, and the capability of the second being exists in the second place, since they are two actions, and every action must have a capability that accompanies it. And God is the Grantor of success. And what he claimed that the sound, healthy person is devoid of capability for action at the beginning of his creation, and then it is not permissible after that? We said: Whoever among our companions claims that a person is not sound of limbs and healthy unless he is capable, this question is impossible according to his principle. Because when the health of the limbs is found, capability is necessarily found in whatever state it is, so this question is repelled according to his principle. Al-Ash'ari relied on this in his book in which he refuted the book of 'Awā'il al-Adilla by Abū al-Qāsim al-Ka'bī. And whoever among them says that health and soundness are other than capability, then he says: He (man) is created in his first state either sound, healthy, capable of action, or sound, healthy, incapable of action. And it is permissible that in the second state and after that it be so. So he equated between states and did not differentiate between the first state and other states. And this is the view of Shaykh Abū Manṣūr al-Māturīdī - may God have mercy on him - in the Book of Tawḥīd. And he mentioned in his book that he wrote against the book of 'Awā'il al-Adilla to al-Ka'bī the difference between the first state and other states, and said: He is not capable only because capability is with the act, and the act of the originated thing is either movement or rest, and the existence of each one of them is impossible in that state, so the affirmation of capability for them is impossible. And there is no impossibility in the existence of movement and rest in other than that state, so the affirmation of capability for them in other than that state is not impossible. So we made a distinction between them due to the existence of evidence of difference, while you in this matter make a distinction when evidence of equality exists and evidence of difference is absent. Then this answer of the Shaykh - may God have mercy on him - is by way of facilitation, and building the matter on what is customary of action, namely movement and rest. As for the speech according to reality, according to the one who makes movement and rest each one of them two beings, then every movement is two actions, and likewise every rest, and he in the beginning of his existence performs one being. So it results in one of the two preceding answers, which is the equivalence between all states, either permissibility or prohibition. And God is the Grantor of success. Moreover, this objection is invalid according to the method of consideration, for we equated between the state of existence and other states in both aspects - I mean the aspect of capability and the aspect of action. For we said concerning the aspect of capability: Since it is only for the act and may be devoid of it at a time while its essence exists, why may it not be devoid of it at other times? Since its being devoid of it at one time is evidence that the establishment of the act is not from
Source pages p. 817

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of an objection regarding whether a human has capability (qudra) in the first state of existence before performing any act. The author presents an objection from an opponent (likely al-Ash'ari against al-Ka'bī) and then gives two responses from the Māturīdī school.

First, the objection is stated: If a person is capable in the second place (after movement begins), then the transition from first to second place occurs without capability—impossible. If he is capable in the first place, then capability exists before movement (istiṭā'a before action). This seems to force a dilemma.

The author then clarifies that the objection depends on where movement occurs. If movement is defined as a single 'being' (kawn) in the second place, then capability for it exists in the second place. If movement is defined as two beings (one in first place, one in second), then each being has its own accompanying capability. This shows the objection is not a valid dilemma but depends on one's definition of movement.

Next, the objection claims that a healthy person lacks capability at the very beginning of creation, and then it becomes impossible (to have capability) afterward. The author gives two Māturīdī responses:
1) Some of our companions hold that health of limbs implies capability necessarily; so the question is impossible because capability is present from the start. This is the view al-Ash'ari relied on in his refutation of al-Ka'bī.
2) Others hold that health and soundness are distinct from capability; so in the first state, a person may be created either capable or incapable, just as in later states. This view, attributed to Imam al-Māturīdī in Kitāb al-Tawḥīd, equates all states. But then al-Māturīdī is also reported to have made a distinction: in the first state, capability is impossible because it must accompany an act, but movement and rest cannot exist in that state (no action yet), while in later states they can. So the distinction is based on an evidence of difference (dalīl al-mufāraqa). The author notes that the opponent in this matter makes distinctions when evidence of equality exists and evidence of difference is absent—i.e., they are inconsistent.

The author then clarifies that al-Māturīdī's first answer (equating all states) was by way of facilitation, based on the conventional understanding of action as movement/rest. On the more precise account (where each movement or rest is actually two beings), the beginner performs only one being in the first state, leading to either permissibility or prohibition of capability—i.e., one of the two earlier answers applies.

Finally, the author declares the objection invalid from a methodological standpoint: they have equated the state of existence (first state) with other states in both aspects—capability and action. Since capability can be devoid of its act at one time (as seen in other states), there is no reason it cannot be devoid at other times. The absence of act at one time does not prove that the presence of act is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection assumes that capability must always accompany action, so if capability is absent in the first state, it must be impossible forever.
Correction: The Māturīdī response shows that capability can exist without action in some states, as evidenced by its absence in later states when no act is performed. Thus, absence of act in the first state does not entail impossibility of capability.

Checkpoint
According to the Māturīdī response that distinguishes between the first state and later states, why is capability impossible in the first state?
A) Because capability only exists when there is a previous capability.
B) Because capability must accompany an act, and no act (movement or rest) exists in the first state.
C) Because the person is not yet created.
D) Because capability is only for God.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi response defends that capability is not present in the first state because no act exists, and this distinction is based on evidence of difference, not arbitrary choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the claim that you have no capability to act before the actual moment of action. When you plan a good deed, do you truly rely on Allah to give you the power at that moment, or do you assume you already possess it? How would your daily actions change if you internalized that your ability comes only with the act?
?
The passage distinguishes between health/soundness and capability. In your life, do you confuse your physical well-being with your spiritual ability to obey Allah? Could it be that you are healthy yet spiritually incapable of a specific act of worship? What would you do to seek that capability from Allah?
?
Al-Māturīdī argues that the first state lacks act, so it lacks capability. This reflects humanity's utter dependence on Allah for even the first movement. Are you aware of your absolute poverty before Allah at every moment? Does this knowledge make you more humble and reliant, or do you feel entitled to your abilities?

The Maturidi Proof that Action is Not a Necessary Consequent of the Self
Imam al-Nasafi argues that action is not entailed by the self and refutes the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi argument that action is not a necessary consequent of the self and how it refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
Action is not a necessary consequent; the self can lack it without contradiction.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi establishes a key Maturidi principle: actions are not entailed by the divine self in the way that necessary attributes are. He first clarifies what it means for something to be a necessary consequent of the self, then argues that if action were such, the self could never be without it—but we observe times when action is absent. He then addresses a Mu'tazili objection concerning the role of power (qudra), showing that their criticism ultimately rests on a misunderstanding of equality and differentiation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of necessary consequent
Read the first sentence carefully. Note that a necessary consequent is something the self cannot separate from. This sets the standard for the entire argument.
2
Trace the conditional argument
Follow the 'if...then...' logic: if action is not a necessary consequent, then the self can be devoid of it for many times. Conversely, if it cannot be devoid for many times, then it must be a necessary consequent.
This establishes the central conditional reasoning.
3
Apply the argument to the case of power (qudra)
The author extends the reasoning: if an action can occur without power at one time, it can at another time because both are equal in lacking power. This challenges the Mu'tazili view.
Equality (taswiya) is key here.
4
Examine the Mu'tazili objection and its refutation
The author argues that a valid objection would require showing a differentiation (tafriqa) that breaks the equality, not just asserting it elsewhere.
The Mu'tazila are criticized for building on appearances without evidence.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by highlighting the Mu'tazila's ignorance of real distinctions, building on appearances without evidence.
Consider how this refutation strengthens the Maturidi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقتضيات ذاتها إذ الذات لا تنفك عما تقتضيه، وإذا لم يكن الفعل من مقتضيات ذاتها جاز خلوها عنه أوقاتاً كثيرة، وإذا لم يجز خلوها عنه أوقاتا كثرة دل أنه من مقتضيات ذاتها فلا يتصور خلوها عنه وقتا. وقد قلنا في جانب الفعل : إن حصوله لما جاز مع عدم القدرة في الوقت الثاني جاز في الوقت العاشر لاستوائهما في العدم، فيكون الاعتراض الصحيح ببيان التفرقة وإبطال التسوية بين الحالة الأولى وبين غيرها من الأحوال في شيء آخر، فإنا نقول : لما ثبتت التسوية بما بينا فيما نحن فيه، ثبتت التسوية في الحكم وفي غيره من الأشياء لما ظهرت التفرقة في الحكم على التفرقة في المعنى، ولا حاجة بنا إلى بيان المعنى الموجب للتفرقة، وإنما يلزمنا ما اعترضوا به أن لو ادعينا التسوية بين أول الأحوال وبين غيرها من الأحوال مطلقا في المواضع أجمع، فأما إذا ادعينا ذلك في موضع مخصوص وأقمنا دلالة التسوية فالاشتغال بإثبات التفرقة في موضع آخر عن سنن الصواب وعدول عن طريقة النظر. وبهذا تبين جهل المعتزلة بالحقائق وبناء الأمر على ظواهر لا دليل عليها ولا معنى تحتها.
Necessary consequents of the self, since the self does not separate from what it necessitates. And if the act is not among the necessary consequents of the self, it is permissible for the self to be devoid of it for many times. And if it is not permissible for the self to be devoid of it for many times, that indicates that it is among the necessary consequents of the self, so it is inconceivable for the self to be devoid of it at any time. And we have said concerning the side of the act: because its occurrence is permissible with absence of power (qudra) in the second moment, it is permissible in the tenth moment due to their equality in non-existence. So the correct objection is to show the differentiation (tafriqa) and invalidate the equality (taswiya) between the first state and other states in some other respect. For we say: since equality is established by what we have shown in our present case, equality is established in the ruling and in other things, because the differentiation in the ruling appears based on differentiation in the meaning. And we have no need to explain the meaning that necessitates the differentiation. Rather, we would be required by their objection only if we claimed absolute equality between the first state and other states in all cases. But if we claim that in a specific case and establish evidence of equality, then engaging in proving differentiation in another case is a departure from correctness and a deviation from the method of inquiry. And by this, the ignorance of the Mu'tazila concerning realities becomes clear, and their building matters on appearances with no evidence nor meaning beneath them.
Source pages p. 819

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. It opens with a definition: a necessary consequent of the self (muqtada al-dhat) is that from which the self cannot be separated. The argument then proceeds conditionally: if the act is not a necessary consequent, then the self can be without it for many times. But if it is not permissible for the self to be without it for many times, that proves it is a necessary consequent, and the self can never be without it. The author then moves to a specific argument about the act occurring without power (qudra). He states that if the act can occur without power in the second moment, it can also occur in the tenth moment because both are equal in the absence of power. A valid objection would require showing a distinction (tafriqa) between the two states that breaks the equality (taswiya). The author argues that since equality is established by his own reasoning, equality in judgment follows unless a distinction in meaning is shown. He does not need to explain that meaning. However, if he had claimed absolute equality in all cases, the objection would apply; but he only claims it in this specific case with evidence. Thus, the Mu'tazila's criticism is misguided, as they build on superficial appearances without evidence or underlying meaning.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi position denies any relationship between the self and action.
Correction: The position is that action is not a necessary, always-present consequent; it does not negate all relationship.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows if action is not a necessary consequent of the self?
A) It cannot occur at all.
B) The self can be devoid of it for many times.
C) The self must always have it.
D) It proves the self is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Action is not a necessary consequent of the self, and the Mu'tazila's objection fails because it does not properly address the equality of states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that the self can lack an action for many times without it being a necessary consequent. Think of a specific righteous action you consistently neglect—prayer at its time, for example. If you tell yourself 'I'm just not that kind of person,' are you treating that negligence as a necessary consequent of your self? What step will you take tomorrow to prove that your self is not fixed, and that change is possible?
?
The Mu'tazila built their argument on appearances with no underlying evidence. Where in your life are you judging yourself or others based on surface-level appearances—like outward acts of worship versus inward state—without examining the deeper reality? Which assumption about yourself or someone else will you challenge today?
?
This lesson shows that equality of states means if you could do something without power at one time, you can at another. Think of a past sin or failure you use to justify giving up on an obligation. If you claim you have no power to change, does that contradict the logical equality argued here? What is one small action you will take this week that you previously thought was impossible for you?
