The Philosopher's Classification of States
Classification of act and human states regarding action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosopher's division of the act into two states (existence and non-existence) and of man into three states (existence, possibility, impossibility).

Core Idea
Acts have two states; humans have three – a logical tool for causality.

Imam al-Nasafi reports a philosopher's attempt to analyze human action through clear-cut states. Before engaging with theological debates, the philosopher sets up a framework: the act itself can be either existent or non-existent, but man can be in one of three relations to it. This passage introduces that framework, which will later be used to discuss power and incapacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part of the passage
Read from 'ذكر هذا' up to 'فقد تبين إذا أن الحالة الثالثة من الأحوال مقابلة للحالة الباقية من الحالتين'. This covers the classification of act states and human states.
The author begins by criticizing opponents then presents the philosopher's claim.
2
Identify the two states of the act
Note that the act has only two states: existence (وجود) and non-existence (عدم), with no in-between. This is a strict dichotomy.
This is foundational for the subsequent analysis.
3
Identify the three states of man
Man has three states regarding the act: existence of the act from him, possibility (إمكان), and impossibility (امتناع). Understand the meaning of each.
These are the human states relative to the act.
4
See how non-existence encompasses two human states
The passage argues that the state of non-existence of the act includes both possibility and impossibility. Both result in the act not existing at that moment.
This justifies why there are only three human states.
5
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider why the philosopher sets up this classification. How does it help in understanding human capacity and action?
This prepares for the next lesson on mapping to incapacity and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر هذا بعض من ينسب إلى الفلسفة (وهي العلم) بحقائق الأمور والوقوف على دقائق العلوم، ونسب مخالفيه في ذلك إلى العدول عن سبيل المعارف، وطول فيه وهول، وزعم أن الفعل له حالتان حالة عدم وحالة وجود ولا واسطة بينهما، والإنسان له أحوال ثلاثة وهي حالة وجود الفعل منه وحالة إمكانه وحالة امتناعه، وإحدى حالتي زمن الفعل تستغرق حالتين من الأحوال الثلاثة الأخر عدم الفعل، فإن حالة عدم الفعل تستغرق حالة امتناع الفعل من الإنسان وحالة إمكانه منه إذ الفعل ممن الفعل عنه ممتنع معدوم وكذا ممن الفعل منه ممكن، لأن الممكن أن يكون هو الممكن أن لا يكون، والفعل الموجود غير ممكن أن لا يكون، فالممكن إذا ما يكون معدومًا بعد، فإذا كانت إحدى حالتي الفعل وهي حالة العدم تستنفد هاتين الحالتين - أعني إمكان الفعل وامتناعه فقد تبين إذا أن الحالة الثالثة من الأحوال مقابلة للحالة الباقية من الحالتين - أعني أن الوجود مقابل الوجود – ثم حالة الامتناع حالة العجز، وحالة الإمكان حالة القدرة، فتبقى حالة وجود الفعل لا بمقابلة حالة القدرة لفوت صفة الإمكان عن الموجود، وحالة القدرة حالة الإمكان لا حالة الوجود، ولا بمقابلة حالة الامتناع التي هي حالة العجز لأن من يمتنع عنه الفعل يستحيل وجوده منه، وقد وجد في هذه الحالة فلم يكن
Some of those who attribute themselves to philosophy (which is knowledge of the realities of things and grasping the subtleties of sciences) mentioned this, and he accused his opponents in that of deviating from the path of knowledges, and he went on at length and exaggerated. And he claimed that the act has two states: a state of non-existence and a state of existence, with no intermediate between them. And man has three states: the state of the act's existence from him, the state of its possibility (imkan), and the state of its impossibility (imtina'). And one of the two states of the time of the act encompasses two of these three states: the other [state] of the act's non-existence. For the state of non-existence of the act encompasses the state of impossibility of the act from man and the state of its possibility from him, since the act from one from whom the act is impossible is non-existent, and likewise from one from whom the act is possible [it is non-existent], because what is possible can be and can not be, while the existing act cannot not be. So the possible, when it is, is not yet non-existent. So when one of the two states of the act, which is the state of non-existence, exhausts these two states—namely the possibility and impossibility of the act—then it becomes clear that the third state of the [human] states is opposite to the remaining state of the two [states of the act]—meaning that existence is opposite to existence. Then the state of impossibility is the state of incapacity ('ajz), and the state of possibility is the state of power (qudra). So the state of existence of the act remains not opposite to the state of power because the attribute of possibility is absent from the existent, and the state of power is the state of possibility, not the state of existence; nor opposite to the state of impossibility which is the state of incapacity, because for one from whom the act is impossible, its existence from him is impossible, but here it has existed from him in this state, so he is not…
Source pages p. 820

Commentary
This passage reports a philosopher's view on the classification of states. The philosopher begins by claiming the act has only two states: non-existence and existence, with no intermediate. He then posits that man has three states relative to the act: the state of the act's existence from him, the state of its possibility (imkan), and the state of its impossibility (imtina'). The state of non-existence of the act encompasses two of the human states: impossibility and possibility. This is because both impossibility and possibility result in non-existence of the act: the impossible cannot exist, and the possible may or may not exist, but when it does not yet exist, it is also non-existent. Since the state of non-existence exhausts these two, the remaining human state (existence of the act) is opposite to the remaining state of the act (existence). The philosopher then maps states: impossibility is incapacity ('ajz), and possibility is power (qudra). Consequently, the state of existence of the act is not opposite to the state of power because power is the state of possibility (not existence), and it is not opposite to the state of impossibility (incapacity) because one from whom the act is impossible cannot have the act exist from him, yet here the act does exist. The text cuts off before completing the conclusion.

Key Terms
حالة الوجود
حالة العدم
حالة الإمكان
حالة الامتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosopher denies human free will or agency.
Correction: The philosopher is simply analyzing logical states, not making a theological claim about determinism. He is setting up a framework for discussing power and action.

Checkpoint
How many states does the act have according to the philosopher?
A) Three
B) Two
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The philosopher classifies act into existence and non-existence, and human into existence, possibility, and impossibility, with non-existence covering the latter two.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that possibility and impossibility both fall under non-existence of the act. Think of a duty you keep postponing. Is it truly impossible, or merely possible? What does your answer reveal about the state of your heart toward that obligation?
?
The philosopher's classification leaves no middle ground: an act either exists or does not. Consider a sin you committed yesterday. Before you committed it, it was possible but non-existent. Afterward, it existed. How does this stark dichotomy press you to reflect on the moment you chose to bring that act into existence?
?
You now know that 'impossible' and 'possible' are both states of non-action. When you say 'I can't' to a good deed, are you honestly acknowledging impossibility, or are you hiding behind a label to avoid responsibility? What would change if you treated it as possible but unperformed?

Mapping States to Incapacity and Power
Mapping human states to incapacity and power, and discussing existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosopher maps impossibility to incapacity and possibility to power, and why the state of existence of the act is not directly opposed to power or incapacity.

Core Idea
Impossibility is incapacity; possibility is power; existence escapes this mapping.

Building on the classification of states, the philosopher now links the human states of impossibility and possibility to specific attributes: incapacity (عجز) and power (قدرة). He then argues that the state of the act's existence from man cannot be simply equated with either, leading to a nuanced conclusion. This passage completes his analysis and sets the stage for further theological discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the remaining passage
Read from 'ثم حالة الامتناع حالة العجز' to the end. This maps the states to incapacity and power and discusses the position of existence.
The text cuts off at the end; we infer the intended conclusion.
2
Understand the mapping
Note that the state of impossibility (امتناع) is identified with the state of incapacity (عجز), and the state of possibility (إمكان) with the state of power (قدرة).
This connects logical states to human capabilities.
3
Analyze the position of existence
The state of existence of the act is not opposite to power because power is a state of possibility, not existence. It is also not opposite to incapacity because that would imply the act cannot exist from that person, but here it does exist.
This shows that existence of the act is a distinct state not reducible to power or incapacity.
4
Reflect on the incomplete ending
The text cuts off. Consider what the philosopher might be leading to: perhaps that the state of existence is simply the actualization of power, or that it requires a different analysis.
This incomplete nature invites further thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر هذا بعض من ينسب إلى الفلسفة (وهي العلم) بحقائق الأمور والوقوف على دقائق العلوم، ونسب مخالفيه في ذلك إلى العدول عن سبيل المعارف، وطول فيه وهول، وزعم أن الفعل له حالتان حالة عدم وحالة وجود ولا واسطة بينهما، والإنسان له أحوال ثلاثة وهي حالة وجود الفعل منه وحالة إمكانه وحالة امتناعه، وإحدى حالتي زمن الفعل تستغرق حالتين من الأحوال الثلاثة الأخر عدم الفعل، فإن حالة عدم الفعل تستغرق حالة امتناع الفعل من الإنسان وحالة إمكانه منه إذ الفعل ممن الفعل عنه ممتنع معدوم وكذا ممن الفعل منه ممكن، لأن الممكن أن يكون هو الممكن أن لا يكون، والفعل الموجود غير ممكن أن لا يكون، فالممكن إذا ما يكون معدومًا بعد، فإذا كانت إحدى حالتي الفعل وهي حالة العدم تستنفد هاتين الحالتين - أعني إمكان الفعل وامتناعه فقد تبين إذا أن الحالة الثالثة من الأحوال مقابلة للحالة الباقية من الحالتين - أعني أن الوجود مقابل الوجود – ثم حالة الامتناع حالة العجز، وحالة الإمكان حالة القدرة، فتبقى حالة وجود الفعل لا بمقابلة حالة القدرة لفوت صفة الإمكان عن الموجود، وحالة القدرة حالة الإمكان لا حالة الوجود، ولا بمقابلة حالة الامتناع التي هي حالة العجز لأن من يمتنع عنه الفعل يستحيل وجوده منه، وقد وجد في هذه الحالة فلم يكن
Some of those who attribute themselves to philosophy (which is knowledge of the realities of things and grasping the subtleties of sciences) mentioned this, and he accused his opponents in that of deviating from the path of knowledges, and he went on at length and exaggerated. And he claimed that the act has two states: a state of non-existence and a state of existence, with no intermediate between them. And man has three states: the state of the act's existence from him, the state of its possibility (imkan), and the state of its impossibility (imtina'). And one of the two states of the time of the act encompasses two of these three states: the other [state] of the act's non-existence. For the state of non-existence of the act encompasses the state of impossibility of the act from man and the state of its possibility from him, since the act from one from whom the act is impossible is non-existent, and likewise from one from whom the act is possible [it is non-existent], because what is possible can be and can not be, while the existing act cannot not be. So the possible, when it is, is not yet non-existent. So when one of the two states of the act, which is the state of non-existence, exhausts these two states—namely the possibility and impossibility of the act—then it becomes clear that the third state of the [human] states is opposite to the remaining state of the two [states of the act]—meaning that existence is opposite to existence. Then the state of impossibility is the state of incapacity ('ajz), and the state of possibility is the state of power (qudra). So the state of existence of the act remains not opposite to the state of power because the attribute of possibility is absent from the existent, and the state of power is the state of possibility, not the state of existence; nor opposite to the state of impossibility which is the state of incapacity, because for one from whom the act is impossible, its existence from him is impossible, but here it has existed from him in this state, so he is not…

Commentary
This passage reports a philosopher's view on the classification of states. The philosopher begins by claiming the act has only two states: non-existence and existence, with no intermediate. He then posits that man has three states relative to the act: the state of the act's existence from him, the state of its possibility (imkan), and the state of its impossibility (imtina'). The philosopher then maps states: impossibility is incapacity ('ajz), and possibility is power (qudra). Consequently, the state of existence of the act is not opposite to the state of power because power is the state of possibility (not existence), and it is not opposite to the state of impossibility (incapacity) because one from whom the act is impossible cannot have the act exist from him, yet here the act does exist. The text cuts off before completing the conclusion.

Correction: The philosopher distinguishes: power is the state of possibility, not existence. The existence of the act is a separate state that follows from power but is not identical.

When you sin, do you quickly dismiss it as 'just something that happened' rather than owning it as your act of power? ?

Definition and Refutation of 'علة' and 'سبب'
Imam Nasafi defines and refutes Abu Hashim's distinction between 'علة' and 'سبب'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between 'علة' and 'سبب' according to Abu Hashim and Imam Nasafi's refutation of it.

Core Idea
Abu Hashim's causal terms fail because they imply necessary effects, contradicting Ash'ari occasionalism.

Imam Nasafi begins by reporting Abu Hashim's definitions of 'علة' (cause) and 'سبب' (reason). He then subjects these definitions to a rigorous critique, pointing out their logical inconsistencies and their failure to align with the Ash'ari view of causality. This lesson unpacks both the definitions and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the footnote markers in the Arabic.
2
Identify the definitions
Locate the definitions of 'علة' and 'سبب' in the passage. Note the example with motion and state.
The example is meant to illustrate the definitions.
3
Understand the refutation
Pay attention to why Nasafi calls this 'هذيان' (nonsense). He refers to a previous discussion on attributes. Consider what that implies.
This dismissal is strong language in kalam.
4
Analyze the power-act argument
Nasafi brings an additional argument about power (قدرة) and act (فعل). How does this further undermine the distinction?
Note that act is a substance, not a state.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final point: even if their view were accepted, they still admit that a reason necessitates its effect by essence, which leads to the same objections.
This ties back to earlier critiques of Abu Hashim's predecessors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلة(۱) ما توجب حالاً، والسبب ما توجب ذاتا ، وكذا سمعت بعضهم ويعنون(۲) بذلك أن الحركة علة لثبوت حال للجسم يفارق بها ذاتاً ليست بمتحركة، فكانت الحال مقتضاة للحركة، ومقتضية لكون الجسم متحركا على ما بينا هذا الهذيان في مسألة الصفات وكشفنا عن وجوه بطلانه. والقدرة توجب ذاتا وهو الفعل، والفعل في نفسه ليس بحال بل الحال من مقتضيات الفعل، وهذا كله مع بطلانه لا ينفعهم لأنهم أقروا أن السبب يوجب المسبب وإيجابه إياه باعتبار ذاته، فلا (۳) يجوز خلو عن ذلك الإيجاب، وفيه ما أوردنا على سلفه.
The cause (علة) is that which necessitates a state (حال), and the reason (سبب) is that which necessitates a substance (ذات). Likewise I heard some of them, and they mean by that that motion is a cause for the establishment of a state in the body by which it differs from a substance that is not moving. Thus the state is necessitated by the motion, and necessitates that the body be moving, as we have explained this nonsense in the matter of the attributes and uncovered the aspects of its invalidity. And power (قدرة) necessitates a substance, which is the act (فعل), and the act in itself is not a state, rather the state is among the requirements of the act. And all of this, despite its invalidity, does not benefit them because they acknowledged that the reason necessitates the effect (مسبب) and its necessitation of it is by virtue of its essence, so it is not permissible for it to be free from that necessitation. And in it is what we have objected to his predecessors.
Source pages p. 829

Commentary
This passage defines and refutes the distinction between 'علة' and 'سبب' as held by Abu Hashim. The author first reports the definitions: 'علة' is what necessitates a state (حال) in a substance, while 'سبب' is what necessitates a substance (ذات) itself. He gives an example: motion is a 'علة' for a state in the body that distinguishes it from a non-moving body. Then he argues that this view is invalid (هذيان) and refers to a previous discussion on attributes. He further argues that power (قدرة) necessitates an act (فعل), which is a substance, not a state; the state is a requirement of the act. Even if this view were true, he argues, it does not help them because they admit that a reason necessitates its effect by its essence, so it cannot be free from that necessitation. The author concludes that this contains the same objections raised against his predecessors.

سبب

فعل
مسبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'علة' and 'سبب' are distinct categories that produce real necessary effects in the world.
Correction: Nasafi refutes this, showing that such a view leads to necessary causation inconsistent with Ash'ari occasionalism, where Allah is the sole cause.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'علة' necessitate?
A) A substance (ذات)
B) A state (حال)
C) An act (فعل)
D) A power (قدرة)

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi refutes Abu Hashim's causal distinction by showing its logical flaws and affirming that only Allah is the true cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What is one practical step you can take today to shift your reliance from means (asbab) to the Creator of means?
?
The passage dismisses Abu Hashim's view as 'nonsense' because it implies that created things can necessitate their effects independently. Are there areas in your life where you feel self-sufficient or believe that your actions alone guarantee results? What would it look like to live in complete awareness that nothing occurs except by Allah's will and power?

Refuting Abu Hashim on Punishment Without Action
Imam Nasafi refutes Abu Hashim's claim that one can be punished for not doing a commanded act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Hashim's view that punishment can occur without a positive act is logically and doctrinally problematic.

Core Idea
Punishment without action contradicts both reason and consensus.

Abu Hashim's novel claim that a person can be deserving of punishment without performing any act raises fundamental questions about the nature of moral responsibility. In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents a penetrating objection that exposes the incoherence of this position, showing that even the very concept of 'abandonment' is itself an act. Let's follow the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu Hashim's position
Start by reading the passage and noting how Abu Hashim defines the state of the capable person who does nothing. He considers that person a sinner deserving punishment simply for not doing the commanded act.
Notice that he says 'not for an act'.
2
Follow the objection's logic
The objector challenges: if you don't do the commanded act, you have abandoned it, and abandonment is itself a forbidden act. So punishment is for that act, not for nothing. But if you neither do nor abandon, you're neutral.
The objector uses the Arabic terms 'ترك' (abandonment) as an act.
3
Recognize the absurd consequence
The objector points out that Abu Hashim's view implies eternal punishment for no act at all, which contradicts the consensus of the community and even the position of Abu Hashim's own predecessors.
This shows the inconsistency in Abu Hashim's school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن أبا هاشم كما أحدث هذا المذهب الفاسد، قيل له: أرأيت لو كان هذا القادر الخالي عن الفعل إيجابًا وتركّاً مدة عمره لو كان مأمورًا بفعل منهيًا عن فعل آخر ماذا يكون حاله ؟ فقال : يكون عاصيًا مستحقاً للذم والعقاب لا على فعل لأنه لم يفعل ما أمر به مع قدرته عليه وتوفر آلاته وارتفاع الموانع عنه. فقيل له: كيف صار مستحقاً للعقاب بأن لم يفعل ما أمر به، والمأمور بفعل إذا لم يفعله فقد تركه، وترك المأمور به حرام فاستحق العقاب على الفعل وهو ترك المأمور به إذ تركه فعل منهي عنه قبيح فقد ارتكبه التارك، فهذا إذا خلا عن الفعل المأمور به وعن تركه لم يصر مطيعا بفعله ولا عاصيًا بارتكاب تركه المنهي عنه فكيف صار مستحقاً للعقاب بل للخلود لا على فعل؟ وهل صار مطيعا مستحقاً للمدح والثواب بأن لم يفعل ما نهى عنه وهو الترك؟ وهل هذا منك إلا القول بأن الله تعالى يخلد أحدًا في النار لا على فعل حصل منه، والأمة أجمعت على خلاف هذا (٤) ، وسلفك يكفرون أهل السنة بقولهم باستحقاق العقاب على فعل خلقه الله تعالى، وإن كانوا يقولون ذلك بفعل العبد وكسبه وهو به عاص، فكان إكفار أولى إذ زعمت أن الله تعالى يعذب العبد لا على فعل محدث أو مكتسب.
Then, as Abu Hashim innovated this corrupt doctrine, it was said to him: 'Do you see if this capable person, free from act, affirmation, and abandonment for his entire life, if he was commanded to do an act and prohibited from another act, what would his state be?' He said: 'He would be a sinner, deserving blame and punishment not for an act, because he did not do what he was commanded to do despite his ability, the availability of his instruments, and the absence of obstacles.' It was said to him: 'How did he become deserving of punishment for not doing what he was commanded? The one commanded to do an act, if he does not do it, has abandoned it. Abandoning the commanded is forbidden, so he deserves punishment for the act, which is the abandonment of the commanded, since his abandonment is a forbidden ugly act that the abandoner has committed. So if he is free from both the commanded act and its abandonment, he does not become obedient by doing it, nor a sinner by committing the abandonment of the forbidden. How then does he become deserving of punishment, indeed of eternal punishment, without an act? And does he become obedient deserving praise and reward by not doing what he was forbidden, which is the abandonment? And is this from you except the statement that Allah will eternally punish someone in the Fire not for an act that occurred from him, while the community unanimously disagrees with this, and your predecessors declare the Ahl al-Sunna disbelievers for their saying that punishment is deserved for an act created by Allah, even though they said that by the servant's act and acquisition, and by it he is a sinner. So your declaring disbelief is more fitting, since you claim that Allah punishes the servant not for a newly originated or acquired act.'
Source pages p. 829

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and refutation of Abu Hashim's view on punishment. Abu Hashim claims that a person who is capable but does not perform a commanded act, and does not perform the prohibited act either, is still a sinner deserving punishment simply for omitting the commanded act. The objector argues that if a person does not do the commanded act, he has abandoned it, and abandonment is itself an act that is forbidden if the commanded act is obligatory. But if the person does not even abandon it (i.e., he is in a state of neither doing nor abandoning), then he cannot be called a sinner. The objector also points out that Abu Hashim's view implies that Allah would eternally punish someone without any act, which contradicts scholarly consensus. Furthermore, Abu Hashim's own predecessors declared Ahl al-Sunna disbelievers for saying that punishment is for an act created by Allah, but here Abu Hashim is saying punishment is without any act, which is even more extreme.

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that even the 'omission' of a commanded act is itself an act, and that punishment without any act contradicts reason and consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Abu Hashim's view let people off the hook by denying that omission is an act. Are there areas of your life where you subtly believe that what you don't do doesn't count? Before you finish this reflection, name one specific commanded act you will stop abandoning—and commit to a concrete action within 24 hours to begin doing it.

Double Punishment for Doing Evil Instead of Good
Nasafi critiques Abu Hashim's claim of double punishment for substituting good with evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Hashim's claim of double punishment for one omission is considered a departure from Islamic consensus.

Core Idea
Double punishment for one omission contradicts Islamic principles of justice.

Imam Nasafi presents a critical objection to Abu Hashim's theology. He asks: what if someone, instead of performing a commanded good act, commits an ugly act? Abu Hashim argues for two punishments, but Nasafi dismisses this as un-Islamic. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Get the literal meaning first.
Notice the question-answer structure.
2
Understand the scenario
The questioner sets a hypothetical: a person commanded to do a good act instead does an ugly act. Grasp this situation.
The word 'تغير تغيرا قبيحا' means 'changed in an ugly change' – i.e., committed a sin.
3
Analyze Abu Hashim's position
Abu Hashim says such a person deserves two portions of punishment: one for the ugly act done, another for the good act not done. Why does he think that?
He seems to treat omission and commission as separate grounds for punishment.
4
Grasp the author's critique
Imam Nasafi dismisses this view as a departure from the sayings of the people of Islam. That is a strong rebuke.
The author is aligning with mainstream Sunni theology that rejects double counting.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why would double punishment be problematic? How does Islamic theology typically view punishment for sin and omission?
This connects to larger issues of divine justice and accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قيل له: إن هذا المأمور لو لم يفعل المأمورية وتغير تغيرا قبيحا؟ قال: استحق قسطين من العذاب أحدهما للقبيح الذي فعله، والآخر للحسن الذي لم يفعله وهذا كله خروج عن أقاويل أهل الإسلام (۱).
Then it was said to him: 'If this commanded person did not do the commanded thing and changed in an ugly change?' He said: 'He deserves two portions of punishment: one for the ugly act he did, and the other for the good act he did not do.' And all of this is a departure from the sayings of the people of Islam.
Source pages p. 830

Commentary
This short passage presents another objection to Abu Hashim. The questioner asks: if a person who is commanded to do a good act instead does an ugly act (i.e., a sin), what is his ruling? Abu Hashim replies that such a person deserves two punishments: one for committing the ugly act, and another for failing to do the good act. The author of the text (Imam Nasafi) comments that this is a complete departure from the consensus of the Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that double punishment is logically consistent: you should be punished for both the sin committed and the good deed omitted.
Correction: Imam Nasafi shows that this contradicts the understanding of the righteous predecessors, implying that punishment for omission is not separate but part of the same breach.

Checkpoint
How many portions of punishment does Abu Hashim claim for a person who does an ugly act instead of the commanded good?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's claim of double punishment for one omission is rejected by Imam Nasafi as a departure from mainstream Islamic teachings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you were in the place of Abu Hashim, would you be willing to defend a view that adds extra punishment? What does this warn you about your own tendency to make religion harder than it is?
?
Are there good deeds you have left undone while committing wrongs? How can you, after reading this, turn to Allah without despair, knowing that the evil is what matters—and that the missed good is not held against you separately?

The Double Punishment Objection
Imam Nasafi presents the double punishment objection for zina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi uses the example of zina to expose an absurd consequence in Abu Hashim's rational theory of good and evil.

Core Idea
Prohibiting an act implies commanding its opposite, leading to double punishment.

Imam Nasafi continues his refutation of Abu Hashim's theory that good acts are good only due to an additional attribute. He now points out that if an act is prohibited, its omission is implicitly commanded. This leads to the contradiction that a person should be punished both for committing the act and for failing to perform the 'good' of omitting it. The example of zina makes this absurdity concrete.

Guided Path
1
Recall the principle
Remember that Abu Hashim claims acts are good only due to an additional attribute. This objection builds on that.
2
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage: prohibition of zina implies command to leave it, so commit zina leads to double punishment.
Notice the logical flow: prohibition → command to omit → punishment for both commission and omission.
3
Identify the absurdity
According to Abu Hashim, leaving zina would be a good act, but it is not commanded separately, so punishment for omission is unjust.
This shows the flaw in Abu Hashim's definition of good as a separate attribute.
4
Reflect on the implication
This objection challenges the coherence of Abu Hashim's entire framework by showing it leads to contradictory punishments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قيل له : إن المكلف لما نهى عن الزنا فقد أمر بتركه لا محالة، فينبغي أن يقال : إنه لما زنى يجب عليه حدان أحدهما للزنا الذي فعله، والآخر للحسن الذي لم يفعله وهو ترك الزنا. وكذا في جميع هذه الأفعال، فأما ترك هذه الأفعال فغير واجب عليه، ويلزم ألا يكون ترك الزنا وشرب الخمر واللواطة طاعة ويرتكب ذلك. أسباب الحدود، فزعم عنه توجه هذا الإلزام عليه أنه نهى عن وكذا زعم أن ترك (۲) الصلاة إذا لم يكن منهيًا عنه ليس بقبيح، ثم عنده ما ليس بقبيح بل هو مباح يكون حسنًا إذ من مذهبه أن كل مباح حسن، وإنما يجب بحسن زائد فيكون على هذا ترك الصلاة حسنًا، وهذا خروج عن الدين والانسلاخ منه بمرة، نعوذ بالله من قول هذا عقباه.
Then it was said to him: 'Since the responsible person is prohibited from zina, he is commanded to leave it necessarily. So it should be said: when he commits zina, two punishments are obligatory for him: one for the zina he did, and the other for the good he did not do, which is leaving zina. Likewise in all these acts. As for leaving these acts, it is not obligatory upon him, and it follows that leaving zina, drinking wine, and sodomy is not an act of obedience, and he commits that. The causes of the punishments... And he alleged that concerning this objection it was said to him that he forbade...' And likewise he alleged that leaving prayer, if it is not prohibited, is not ugly. Then according to him, what is not ugly but permissible is good, since his doctrine is that every permissible thing is good, and it is only required due to an additional goodness. So on this basis, leaving prayer is good. This is a departure from religion and stripping oneself of it completely. We seek refuge with Allah from the end result of this statement.
Source pages p. 830

Commentary
In this passage, the author raises two linked objections against Abu Hashim. First, using the example of zina: if a person is prohibited from zina, he is implicitly commanded to leave it. If he commits zina, according to Abu Hashim's previous principle, he should deserve two punishments: one for the act of zina and one for omitting the good of leaving zina. This leads to the absurdity that leaving zina is not an act of obedience, and thus the person would be punished for not doing a good that is not even required. The author then mentions that Abu Hashim responded (though the text is somewhat corrupt) and that Abu Hashim also claimed that leaving prayer, if not explicitly prohibited, is not ugly, and since whatever is not ugly is permissible, and all permissible acts are good according to his school, it follows that leaving prayer is good. This, the author says, is a complete departure from the religion.

Key Terms
زنا
حد
ترك
مباح
حسن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Abu Hashim's view is consistent and does not lead to double punishment.
Correction: The passage shows that if prohibition implies command to omit, then omitting becomes a good act, leading to two punishments.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why should a person who commits zina receive two punishments?
A) Because zina is both a sin and a crime
B) One for the act of zina and one for failing to perform the good of leaving it
C) Because the person intended to commit two sins
D) Because the person repeated the act twice

Key Takeaway
Prohibiting an act implies commanding its omission, leading to a logical contradiction in Abu Hashim's theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you avoid a sin, do you treat that avoidance as a positive act of obedience that Allah commands, or do you simply see it as the absence of wrongdoing? How would your motivation change if you saw every moment of leaving sin as an opportunity for reward?
?
The double punishment objection reveals that according to some theories, every sin carries an extra weight—a punishment for not doing the good of leaving it. Does this make you more careful about even small sins? How does it affect your view of Allah's mercy and justice?

Leaving Prayer Considered Good
Imam Nasafi refutes Abu Hashim by showing his view implies leaving prayer is good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how Abu Hashim's view that all permissible acts are good leads to the heretical conclusion that leaving obligatory prayer is good.

Core Idea
If every permissible act is good, then leaving prayer becomes good.

Having dealt with the double punishment objection, Imam Nasafi turns to another implication of Abu Hashim's doctrine. Since Abu Hashim classifies all permissible acts as good, and leaving prayer is not prohibited (hence permissible), it follows that leaving prayer is good. This directly contradicts the Islamic consensus that prayer is obligatory and its omission is sinful. The author condemns this as a complete departure from religion.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the argument
Recall Abu Hashim's position: every permissible act is good. The author now applies it to leaving prayer.
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic: leaving prayer is not prohibited, so it is permissible, hence good.
Notice the chain: not ugly → permissible → good.
3
Analyze the logic
Trace the steps: leaving prayer is not ugly (since not prohibited), so it is permissible, and according to Abu Hashim, every permissible act is good.
This shows the slippery slope of defining good based on permissibility.
4
Understand the refutation
The author calls this a complete departure from religion, showing the danger of such rationalist reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then it was said to him: 'Since the responsible person is prohibited from zina, he is commanded to leave it necessarily. Ignorance of the True Nature of Reward and Punishment
Criticizes opponent's misuse of 'reward' and 'punishment'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi critique of redefining reward and punishment exposes a fundamental ignorance of their true natures.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment are defined by their relation to actions, not by absence.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi attacks a rival theological view that redefines 'reward' and 'punishment' based on the absence of sin or command. By clarifying the true meanings, the author shows that the opponent's usage is confused and undermines the coherence of reward and punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the author's critique come through first.
Pay attention to the words 'thawab' and 'iqab'.
2
Identify the opponent's definitions
Find where the author states how the opponent uses 'reward' and 'punishment': reward = what is given for absence of sins, punishment = what is inflicted for absence of commanded act.
3
Contrast with correct definitions
Note the correct definitions: reward = recompense for preceding good deeds, punishment = painful recompense following evil deeds. See how the opponent's error misplaces the basis from action to absence.
The correct definitions tie directly to actions performed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأعجب من ذلك كله أن يسمى ما أعطى لانعدام المعاصي منه ثوابًا وإن كان الثواب جزاء ما سبق منه من الفعل الحسن سمى به لعود منفعة عمله إليه، ويسمى ما أولم به لانعدام المأمور به عقابًا، وإن كان العقاب ما يتعقب الفعل القبيح من الجزاء المؤلم سمى عقابًا لتعقبه ذلك الفعل، وهذا منه جهل بحقيقة الثواب والعقاب.
And stranger than all that is that he calls what is given due to the absence of acts of disobedience 'reward,' although reward is recompense for what has preceded from him of good deeds (called reward because of the return of its benefit to him), and he calls what is inflicted due to the absence of the commanded act 'punishment,' although punishment is what follows the evil deed as painful recompense (called punishment because of its following that deed). And this from him is ignorance of the true nature of reward and punishment.
Source pages p. 832

Commentary
This passage criticizes the opponent's misuse of the terms 'reward' (thawab) and 'punishment' ('iqab). The opponent calls what is given due to the absence of sins 'reward,' but the true meaning of reward is recompense for a good deed that returns benefit to the doer. Similarly, he calls what is inflicted due to the absence of a commanded act 'punishment,' but punishment is the painful recompense that follows an evil deed. By redefining these terms without reference to actual actions, the opponent shows ignorance of the true nature (haqiqa) of reward and punishment.

Key Terms
ثواب
عقاب
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reward is what God gives when sins are absent, and punishment is what God inflicts when commands are not performed.
Correction: Reward is recompense for good deeds, and punishment is painful recompense following evil deeds.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the proper definition of punishment ('iqab)?
A) Painful recompense following an evil deed.
B) What is inflicted for not doing a commanded act.
C) Any pain from God.
D) Absence of reward.

Key Takeaway
Reward and punishment must be defined by their relationship to actual actions, not by the absence of opposite acts.

The Maturidi Doctrine: Command as Prohibition of Its Opposite
States the Maturidi agreement and contrast with Mu'tazila on command-prohibition relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that a command for a thing is simultaneously a prohibition of its opposite, and how this differs from the Mu'tazilite view.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is one; His command inherently prohibits the opposite.

This lesson explores a key Maturidi principle: when Allah commands something, that command itself constitutes a prohibition of its opposite. This is not a separate prohibition but an entailment of the unity of divine speech. We'll see how this contrasts with the Mu'tazilite understanding of divine speech as composed of distinct forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two parts: the Maturidi view and the Mu'tazilite view.
Pay attention to the basis given for the Maturidi view: unity of divine speech.
2
Identify the core claim
Identify the Maturidi claim: command for a thing is prohibition of its opposite. Ask: what is the argument for this?
3
Understand the reasoning
The reasoning is that Allah's speech is one. Since it is one, it is both command (for what it commands) and prohibition (of what it prohibits). Thus, the command itself entails a prohibition of the opposite.
Contrast with Mu'tazilite view where forms are distinct.
4
Contrast with Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila hold that command and prohibition have distinct linguistic forms. So a command cannot be a prohibition. However, they still agree that the opposite of the commanded is prohibited, but not because the command itself is a prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن أصحابنا مع أوائلهم اتفقوا أن كل مأمور به كان تركه وهو فعل مضادة منهيًا عنه، وكل منهي عنه (۱) تركه وهو فعل مضادة مأمورا به، إذا كان لكل واحد منهما ترك مخصوص وضد متعين ، وكذا عندنا في كل ما له أضداد من الجانين جميعًا، وعندهم فيما له أضداد تقسيم يطول ذكره يعرف ذلك في(٢) أصول الفقه، أن عندنا كان الأمر بالشيء نهيًا عن ضده، والنهي عن الشيء أمرًا بضده، إذ كلام الله تعالى عندنا واحد وهو بنفسه أمر بما أمر نهي عما نهى، فكان ما هو الأمر بالشيء نهيًا عن ضده.
غير
وعند المعتزلة كلام الله تعالى (هذه العبارات) (۳) المتركبة من الحروف على هذه الصيغات وللأمر صيغة مخصوصة وكذا للنهي صيغة مخصوصة لا يتصور كون النهي أمرًا ولا كون الأمر نهيًا . ولا شك أن ضد المأمور منهي وضد المنهي مأمور.
Then our companions, along with their early authorities, agreed that every commanded act, its abandonment (which is the act of its opposite) is prohibited, and every prohibited act, its abandonment (which is the act of its opposite) is commanded, since for each of them there is a specific abandonment and a specific opposite. And likewise with us in everything that has opposites from both sides. And according to them regarding what has opposites, there is a division whose length would be too long, known in Usul al-Fiqh. That with us, the command for a thing is a prohibition of its opposite, and the prohibition of a thing is a command for its opposite, because the speech of Allah with us is one, and it is in itself a command for what it commands and a prohibition of what it prohibits. So that which is the command for a thing is a prohibition of its opposite.
Otherwise
And according to the Mu'tazila, the speech of Allah is these expressions composed of letters in these forms, and the command has a specific form, and likewise the prohibition has a specific form; it is inconceivable for a prohibition to be a command or a command to be a prohibition. And there is no doubt that the opposite of the commanded is prohibited and the opposite of the prohibited is commanded.
Source pages p. 832

Commentary
This passage states the Maturidi position on the relationship between command and prohibition. The author says that all Maturidi scholars (ashabuna ma'a awwali'im) agreed that for every commanded act, its abandonment (which is actually performing the opposite act) is prohibited, and for every prohibited act, its abandonment (performing the opposite) is commanded. This holds when each has a specific opposite and a specific abandonment. Then he contrasts this with the Mu'tazilite view. The Maturidi basis for this is that Allah's speech (kalam Allah) is one, and it is in itself both a command for what it commands and a prohibition of what it prohibits. Therefore, the command for a thing is necessarily a prohibition of its opposite. In contrast, the Mu'tazila hold that divine speech consists of expressions composed of letters in specific forms, and the command has a specific form different from the prohibition, so it is inconceivable that a command could be a prohibition or vice versa. However, the author adds that both sides agree that the opposite of the commanded is prohibited and the opposite of the prohibited is commanded.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
ضد
كلام الله
صيغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidis claim that command and prohibition are the same thing.
Correction: No, they say that the same divine speech is both a command for one thing and a prohibition for its opposite. The speech is one, but its relation to different acts differs.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidis, why does the command for something prohibit its opposite?
A) Because God's speech is multiple
B) Because God's speech is one and simultaneously commands and prohibits
C) Because of human reasoning
D) Because of consensus

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi view hinges on the unity of divine speech: what is a command for one thing is necessarily a prohibition of its opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a command of Allah, do you immediately feel the weight of its opposite being prohibited? Or do you treat commands as isolated items, neglecting that to leave a command is to engage in its opposite? Examine your daily life: which commands do you habitually neglect, and what opposite are you therefore committing?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted on separate forms; the Maturidis insisted on unity. Which mindset dominates your understanding of Allah's speech—do you see His commands as detached instructions or as a single, all-encompassing will that leaves no room for selective obedience? Be honest: do you compartmentalize your obedience?
?
If Allah's speech is one and His command inherently prohibits its opposite, then every moment of your life is either obedience or disobedience—there is no neutral ground. How does that reality confront your comfort zones? What is one action today that you know is commanded, but you have been avoiding, and can you now see its opposite as something you must flee from?

Divine Power Precedes, Human Power Accompanies: The Maturidi View of Kasb
Nasafi distinguishes divine from human power and explains the basis of kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidis hold that human power (qudra) is simultaneous with the act, not prior, and how this relates to the doctrine of kasb (acquisition).

Core Idea
Divine power creates; human power acquires — and that changes everything about timing.

Nasafi contrasts the operation of divine power with that of human power. While Allah's power must precede its object to bring it from non-existence, human power is not creative; rather, it is the capacity for acquisition (kasb). This difference determines whether power precedes or coincides with the act. Understanding this distinction is key to the Maturidi position on free will and divine determinism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key contrast between divine and human power.
Pay attention to the terms ikhtira' (invention) and kasb (acquisition).
2
Identify the two conditions
Identify the condition for divine power (precedence) and the condition for human power (simultaneity).
The text says: 'فلم يكن من شرطها التقدم ... بل من شرطها وجود المخترع'.
3
Connect to the broader issue
Understand why this distinction makes the issue of istita'a closely related to the creation of acts, and why theologians discuss it right after that.
The Mu'tazila require precedence; Maturidis require simultaneity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدور، وذلك يوجب سبق القدرة للمقدور ليصبح تأثيرها في العدم، والقدرة المحدثة غير صالحة للاختراع بل هي صالحة لكون المخترع كسبًا للقادر فلم يكن من شرطها التقدم على المقدور، بل من شرطها وجود المخترع ليتعلق بها فيكون كسبًا له، وعن هذا المعنى قيل: إن مسألة الاستطاعة قريبة لمسألة خلق الأفعال، فإن عندهم لما كانت قدرة العبد للاختراع لابد من أن تكون سابقة، وعندنا لما لم تكن للاختراع لم تكن سابقة بل كانت مقارنة لما وجد بالاختراع، ولهذا المعنى جرى العرف من المتكلمين بإيراد هذه المسألة عقيب مسألة خلق أفعال العباد.
The act (al-maqdūr) requires the precedence of power (al-qudra) over the act so that its effect occurs in non-existence. But the created power (al-qudra al-muḥdatha) is not fit for invention (al-ikhtirāʿ); rather, it is fit for the invented thing to be an acquisition (kasb) for the capable one. So it is not a condition for it to precede the act; rather, it is a condition for it that the invented thing exist so that it (the power) may attach to it and it becomes an acquisition for him. This is why it has been said: The issue of capability (al-istiṭāʿa) is close to the issue of the creation of acts. For according to them (the Muʿtazila), since the servant's power is for invention, it must be prior. According to us, since it is not for invention, it is not prior but rather simultaneous with what exists by invention. For this reason, it has become the custom of theologians to raise this issue immediately after the issue of the creation of the acts of servants.
Source pages p. 846

Commentary
This passage defines the Maturidi position on the relationship between power and act. It begins by contrasting divine power, which must precede its object to create from nothing, with created human power, which is not creative (lā ṣāliḥa li-l-ikhtirāʿ) but rather a capacity for acquisition (kasb). Therefore, human power does not need to precede the act; rather, the act must exist for the power to attach to it as an acquisition. This is why the issue of istiṭāʿa is closely tied to the issue of the creation of acts. The Muʿtazila, who believe human power is creative, require it to precede the act; Maturidis, denying creative power, affirm simultaneity. Hence, it is customary to discuss this after the issue of creation of acts.

Key Terms
قدرة
كسب
اختراع
استطاعة
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think human power precedes action like divine power, but Maturidis hold that human power is simultaneous with the act.
Correction: The text clearly states: 'لم تكن سابقة بل كانت مقارنة لما وجد بالاختراع' (it was not prior but rather simultaneous with what exists by invention).

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi position, what is the condition for created power (al-qudra al-muḥdatha) regarding its object?
A) It must precede the act
B) It must be simultaneous with the act
C) It must exist after the act
D) It is not related to the act

Key Takeaway
In Maturidi theology, human power does not create but acquires, and therefore it is simultaneous with the act, not prior.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your power does not create your actions but merely acquires what God creates, can you honestly take credit for any good deed? How does this reshape your gratitude and humility?
?
When you sin, do you blame your own 'weakness' or do you realize that even your sin is created by God and you only acquired it? How does this realization affect your repentance and reliance on Him?
?
Your power is simultaneous with your act—never before, never after. Does this truth make you more present in each moment, knowing that you cannot 'store up' capability for later? How will you live today differently because of this?

Why 'Shay'' is Not a Common Noun for God and Creation
Imam Nasafi proves that the word 'shay'' is not a common noun encompassing God and creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the word 'shay'' (thing) is not a common noun (ism jins) that encompasses both God and creation, and how this argument safeguards divine transcendence.

Core Idea
'Thing' is not a genus: the gap between Creator and creation is absolute.

Imam Nasafi tackles a subtle but critical point in Islamic theology: can we use the word 'thing' (shay') as a common term for both God and the world? At first glance, both are 'things' since both exist. However, Nasafi argues that this would imply a shared genus, which undermines God's utterly unique nature. In this lesson, we will follow his proof that 'shay'' is actually a homonym, not a genus term, and why claiming otherwise leads to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without pausing for analysis. Let the flow of the argument settle in your mind.
Notice that the passage begins with a general rule about word application.
2
Identify the key contrast
Look for the comparison between necessary existence (wājib al-wujūd) and contingent existence (jā'iz al-wujūd). Nasafi says their difference is far greater than any difference among created things.
3
Understand the homonym argument
See how Nasafi argues that 'shay'' behaves like a homonym (mushtarak): when used for God it excludes creation, and vice versa. This is unlike a common noun.
4
Grasp the theological consequence
Note that if 'shay'' were a common noun, God and the world would share a genus, leading to anthropomorphism (tashbīh). Nasafi declares that such a claim is kufr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مما لا يجوز تناول اللفظ إياه لولا دخول غيره فيه، وفي العموم يجوز دخول كل فرد في اللفظ وإن دخل أغياره من أشخاص نوعه فيه، وبعد الوقوف على هذه الجملة نقول : لفظة الشيء وإن كانت عبارة عن الموجود عندنا، والله تعالى بصفاته موجود والعالم حديث فيكون جائز الوجود، واختلاف ما بين واجب الوجود وجائز الوجود فوق اختلاف ما بين عين الشمس وينبوع الماء والآلة الباصرة بجواز الشبه والمساواة بين هذه الأشياء بكثير من المعاني، واستحالة الشبه بين القديم والمحدث بمعنى من المعاني، فكانت لفظة الشيء إذا أريد بها القديم لا يجوز دخول المحدث تحته، وإذا أريد بها المحدث يمتنع دخول القديم تحته كما في الأسماء المشتركة، وعن هذا قلنا: إن الشيء ليس باسم جنس، والله تعالى وإن أطلقت عليه لفظة الشيء ليس من جنس العالم ولو كانت لفظة الشيء عامة في القديم والحديث لكانت اسم جنس لما تحتها وكان القديم نوعاً منه والمحدث نوعًا آخر، فيختلفان نوعًا ويستويان جنسًا، والقول بإثبات المجانسة بين القديم والمحدث اذا كفر
It is that the word cannot be applied to it without the inclusion of something else in it, and in generality, the inclusion of every individual in the word is allowed, even if others from the individuals of its type enter it. After stopping at this summary, we say: The word 'thing' (shay'), even if it is an expression for the existent in our view, and God Most High with His attributes is existent, and the world is temporally originated (ḥādith), thus it is contingent existence (jā'iz al-wujūd). The difference between necessary existence (wājib al-wujūd) and contingent existence is far greater than the difference between the sun itself, a spring of water, and the seeing instrument, in terms of the possibility of similarity and equality among these things in many meanings, and the impossibility of similarity between the eternal (al-qadīm) and the temporally originated (al-muḥdath) in any meaning whatsoever. Thus the word 'thing', if it is intended to mean the eternal, the inclusion of the temporally originated under it is not permissible, and if it is intended to mean the temporally originated, the inclusion of the eternal under it is prevented, as in homonyms (al-asmā' al-mushtaraka). Because of this we said: 'Thing' is not a common noun (ism jins). God Most High, even if the word 'thing' is applied to Him, is not of the same genus (jins) as the world. If the word 'thing' were general over the eternal and the temporally originated, it would be a common noun for what is under it, and the eternal would be a species (naw') of it and the temporally originated another species, so they would differ in species and be equal in genus. The statement affirming similarity of genus (mujānasa) between the eternal and the temporally originated is, if said, disbelief (kufr).
Source pages p. 864

Commentary
This passage is a definitional proof that the word 'shay'' (thing) is not a genus term encompassing both God and creation. The author begins by establishing the rule that a word cannot be applied to something without including others only in certain contexts. Then he states the core argument: the word 'shay'' denotes existence, and both God and the world are existent, but God is necessarily existent (wājib al-wujūd) while the world is contingently existent (jā'iz al-wujūd). The difference between these two modes of existence is far greater than any difference among created things (like the sun, a water spring, and a seeing tool), which may share similarities in some aspects. No similarity whatsoever can be postulated between the eternal (al-qadīm) and the originated (al-muḥdath). Therefore, the word 'shay'', when used for the eternal, cannot include the originated, and vice versa—it functions like a homonym (mushtarak) rather than a univocal term. Consequently, 'shay'' is not a common noun (ism jins). If it were, it would imply that God and the world share a genus, differing only as species, which would entail anthropomorphism (tashbīh). The author concludes that claiming such a common genus between the eternal and the originated amounts to disbelief (kufr).

Key Terms
الشيء (shay')
القديم (al-qadim)
المحدث (al-muhdath)
اسم جنس (ism jins)
المجانسة (mujānasa)
الأسماء المشتركة (al-asmā' al-mushtaraka)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'thing' (shay') can be applied in the same sense to both God and creation because both exist.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the word 'shay'' is a homonym: its meaning when applied to God excludes creation and vice versa. The difference between necessary and contingent existence is absolute, so no univocal term can be used.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Nasafi say that the word 'shay'' is not a common noun?
A) Because it is used only for God
B) Because it would imply a shared genus between the eternal and the originated, leading to anthropomorphism
C) Because it has no meaning for the originated
D) Because it is a homonym with many meanings

Key Takeaway
The word 'shay'' is not a common noun between God and creation; it functions as a homonym, maintaining the absolute transcendence of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God as 'existent', do you ever unconsciously imagine a 'thing' among other things, merely greater in power or size? What does this lesson demand you change in your conception of God's existence?
?
You regularly say 'God is the Creator' and 'creation is dependent'. Has that truth penetrated your heart to the point that you feel the absolute incomparability between His being and yours? If not, what specific mental habit has been shielding you from this awe?
?
The passage declares that claiming any shared genus between God and creation is disbelief. Is there any area of your life—your understanding of God's attributes, His actions, or His names—where you have unconsciously treated Him as like a superior version of a created being? Will you repent of that subtle tashbīh today?

Calling the Slave a Creator When Paired with Allah
Discusses the permissibility of applying 'khāliq' to the servant when associated with Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological and linguistic rationale for applying the term 'creator' to the servant only when paired with Allah, and how this preserves tawhid.

Core Idea
Naming a pair does not name each part individually.

This lesson explores a subtle but important point in Islamic theology: while the servant is an agent (fāʿil) but not a creator (khāliq) independently, it is permissible to call the servant a creator when the term is used in conjunction with Allah. The passage uses two clear analogies from Arabic usage to justify this, showing that language allows such pairing without implying independent power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the distinction between fāʿil and khāliq, and the rule that follows.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage explicitly states: 'the servant is an agent but not a creator.' Understand why this distinction is crucial—it preserves Allah's exclusive attribute of creation.
The term 'fāʿil' is broader than 'khāliq'.
3
Examine the analogies
The passage gives two analogies: 'the two Umars' (for Abu Bakr and Umar) and 'two moons' (for sun and moon). Consider how these analogies demonstrate that pairing a name can be applied even when each individual does not hold that name alone.
Reflect on the linguistic principle: attributing a label to a pair does not attribute it to each individually.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكانت الآية محمولة عليه دفعًا للتناقض عن الآيات والدلائل، على أن العبد يكون فاعلاً ولا يكون خالقًا، والله تعالى هو الخالق، ويجوز إطلاق اسم الخالق على العبد وإن لم يكن خالقا إذا ضم إلى الخالق كما يقال: سنة العمرين في أبي بكر رضي الله عنهما – وإن كان أبو بكر لا يسمى عند الانفراد عمر، وكذا يقال للشمس والقمر قمران) قال القائل: أخذنا بآفاق السماء عليكم لنا قمراها والنجوم الطوالع وإن كان اسم القمر لا يطلق على الشمس عند الانفراد فكذا هذا،
So the verse is interpreted upon it to avoid contradiction between the verses and proofs, that the servant is an agent (fāʿil) but not a creator (khāliq), and Allah Most High is the Creator, and it is permissible to apply the name 'Creator' to the servant even if he is not a creator when paired with the Creator, just as it is said: 'the way of the two Umars' regarding Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with them both) – even though Abu Bakr is not called Umar when alone – and likewise it is said of the sun and moon as 'two moons'.) The poet said: 'We took with the horizons of the sky against you, / for us are its two moons and the shining stars.' And even though the name 'moon' is not applied to the sun when alone, so it is this.
Source pages p. 871

Commentary
This passage argues for the permissibility of calling the servant a creator in a restricted sense. It begins by stating that the verse is interpreted to prevent contradiction. The key distinction is that the servant is an agent but not a creator; Allah alone is the Creator. However, when the servant is paired with Allah, it is linguistically acceptable to apply the term 'khāliq' to the servant, as in the idiomatic expression 'the two Umars' for Abu Bakr and Umar, even though Abu Bakr alone is not called Umar. Similarly, the sun and moon are together called 'two moons' in poetry, but the sun alone is not called a moon. Thus, the term is used in a relative or figurative sense when paired.

Key Terms

خالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That applying 'Creator' to the servant implies independent creative power.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'Creator' is applied to the servant only in a paired usage with Allah, not individually, and does not imply independent creation.

Checkpoint

D) Using the word 'creator' for both Allah and the servant equally

Key Takeaway
The term 'creator' can be applied to the servant in a paired expression without implying independent creation, as shown by Arabic linguistic conventions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Cause of Similarity and Difference is the Essence
Defines that similarity and difference are based on essence, not accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that similarity and difference between things are rooted in their essences, not in accidental attributes, and how this principle refutes a Mu'tazili objection.

Core Idea
Essence alone determines similarity and difference; accidents do not change the essence.

In this lesson, we delve into a foundational principle of Maturidi ontology: the cause of similarity and difference between entities is their very essence (dhat). The author explains that accidents (what is beyond the essence) do not affect the essence's identity. This principle is then used to critique al-Ka'bi's error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Note the technical terms like al-dhat (essence) and al-ishtibah (similarity).
The passage is concise; pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage states that the cause (illa) of similarity and difference is the essence itself. This is the main thesis.
Contrast this with views that locate cause in accidents.
3
Understand the negative claim
The passage argues that differences or unions in accidents do not change the essences. Similarity and difference are inherent in the essences.
This establishes that accidents are irrelevant to identity.
4
See the refutation of al-Ka'bi
The author uses this principle to expose al-Ka'bi's error: either he is ignorant of this principle or deceiving others.
Notice how the principle serves as a criterion for truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علة (۱) الاشتباه والاختلاف لما كان هو الذات، وباختلاف ما وراء الذاتين(۲) في المعاني لا تتغير الذاتان ولا باتحاد ما وراء الذاتين من المعنى يتحدان (۳)، ولا يتغير ما هو من مقتضيات الذاتين من التشابه والاختلاف. وعرف بهذا حيد الكعبي في الاعتراض عن الحقائق إما جهلاً منه بذلك، وإما تمويها على الضعفة.
The cause of similarity and difference is that it is the essence, and by the difference of what is beyond the two essences in meanings, the two essences do not change, nor by the union of what is beyond the two essences in meaning do they become united, and that which is from the requirements of the two essences of similarity and difference does not change. And by this al-Ka'bi's deviation in objecting to truths is known, either due to his ignorance of that, or out of deceit towards the weak.
Source pages p. 894

Commentary
This passage defines the cause of similarity and difference. It states that the essence (dhat) itself is the cause of similarity and difference. What lies beyond the essences (i.e., accidents) do not affect the essences; the essences remain unchanged whether the accidents differ or unite. Therefore, similarity and difference are inherent in the essences themselves. The author then notes that al-Ka'bi (a Mu'tazili scholar) deviated in objecting to truths because he either was ignorant of this principle or was deliberately deceiving the weak-minded.

Key Terms
الذات (al-dhat)
الاشتباه (al-ishtibah)
الاختلاف (al-ikhtilaf)
مقتضيات الذاتين (muqtadayat al-dhatayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Similarity and difference are caused by accidental attributes rather than the essence itself.
Correction: The passage clarifies that similarity and difference are inherent in the essences; accidents do not alter the essences or their relations.

Checkpoint

A) The accidents that accompany the essence
B) The essence itself
C) The external circumstances
D) The names and descriptions

Key Takeaway
The essence itself is the sole determinant of similarity and difference; accidental attributes do not alter the essence.

The Dilemma of Similarity Between Opposites
A logical dilemma about similarity and difference from obedience

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that arises when attributing effects like obedience to essences, showing how it leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
If obedience causes similarity, then opposites become similar—a contradiction.

This short passage presents a powerful logical dilemma used by Imam al-Nasafi to refute an opponent's position. The opponent claims that obedience or some attribute creates similarity between acts. Al-Nasafi then shows that this leads to an impossible situation: either you must admit that opposites can be similar (which defies their essences) or you deny the causal role of obedience, which undercuts your own premise. Let's explore the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Notice the two conditional branches: 'If he says: Yes ... If he says: No ...'.
Pay attention to the key terms: اشتباه, تضاد, ذات, طاعة.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The opponent is forced into a contradiction. If he affirms that obedience causes similarity, then opposites must be similar (contradicting their essences). If he denies it, he gives up his claim that obedience causes similarity altogether.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the logical force
Why does this dilemma undermine the opponent's worldview? The opponent cannot consistently hold that obedience creates similarity while maintaining the distinctness of essences.
The argument does not prove the author's own view, but exposes the opponent's inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : نعم، أوجب الاشتباه بين المتضادين مع اقتضاء ذاتيهما الاختلاف بينهما والذات المقتضى لذلك موجود
وإن قال : لا ، أبطل الاتفاق من حيث الطاعة أن يكون موجبا للتشابه عند الاختلاف في الذات، وكذا اختلافهما من حيث الطاعة لا يوجب الاختلاف بينهما عند استواء الذاتين و اقتضائهما تشابهها، والله والموفق.
If he says: Yes, it necessitates similarity between the two opposites despite their essences requiring difference between them, and the essence that necessitates that exists. And if he says: No, he nullifies the agreement from the aspect of obedience that it be a cause for similarity when there is difference in essence, and likewise their difference from the aspect of obedience does not cause difference between them when the two essences are equal and require their similarity. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 895

Commentary
This passage presents a logical dilemma to the opponent. If he acknowledges that obedience (or some attribute) causes similarity between acts, then he must admit similarity between opposites despite their essences demanding difference—a contradiction. If he denies that obedience causes similarity, then he invalidates the very agreement (that obedience brings similarity) when essences differ. The argument shows that the opponent's position is self-undermining. The author concludes with a prayer: 'And God is the grantor of success.'

Key Terms

تضاد
ذات
طاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the dilemma is about actions alone.
Correction: It targets the logical consequence of attributing effects like obedience to essences, not actions per se.

Checkpoint

D) It proves that obedience is non-essential.

Key Takeaway
This dilemma shows that attributing effects like obedience to essences leads to logical contradictions, undermining the opponent's position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Consider your own good deeds: do you subconsciously attribute their value to your own nature rather than to Allah's command? What would it mean for you to truly act only for His sake, without any reliance on your own essence?
?
The opponent's position collapses because of an internal inconsistency. Are there areas in your understanding of faith where you hold incompatible ideas—for example, about Divine decree and free will—that you have not fully reconciled? What would it cost you to examine them as rigorously as this dilemma examines its subject?

Refutation by Evil and Lowly Creatures
Objection that God's acts include evil, undermining claimed distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the opponent's claim of a distinction between divine and human creation by pointing out that God's acts include evil and lowly things according to the opponent's own principles.

Core Idea
If God's acts include evil, the distinction between divine and human creation collapses.

The opponent argues that divine creation is fundamentally different from human creation. But the author turns the tables: according to the opponent, God creates even evil and lowly beings. This undermines any claim of a clean distinction. Let's see the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the rhetorical question at the end.
2
Identify the opponent's claim being refuted
The opponent claims a clear distinction between the creation of God and the creation of others. Find where this is referenced in the passage.
3
List the lowly things the author cites
The author lists specific examples of created beings that are considered lowly or evil. Write them down and consider their nature.
These include shaytan, evil, trial, corruption, stench, filth, impurity.
4
Understand the rhetorical question
The author ends with a rhetorical question: 'how can the difference mentioned be valid?' Reflect on why this question effectively undermines the opponent's position.
The opponent cannot consistently claim a distinction when their own principles attribute evil to God.
5
Reflect on the refutation's effectiveness
Consider why this argument is powerful: it uses the opponent's own premises to reach a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن عندهم قد يكون فعل الله تعالى ما هو ذلك وخضوع لأن خلق الله مع تعالى عندهم المخلوق، ولا شك أن كل مخلوق فيه (ذلة وخضوع) وحاجة، ومن المخلوقات ما هو شيطان وشر وفتنة وابتلاء وفساد ونتن وخبث وقذرة، وكل ذلك عندهم فعل الله تعالى، فأنى يصح ما ذكر من الفرق وإبطال التشابه بين خلق الله تعالى وبين خلق غيره؟!
That according to them, the act of God, may He be exalted, may be that which is lowly and submissive because the creation of God - exalted is He - according to them is the created being, and there is no doubt that every created being contains lowliness and submission and need. And among created beings are that which is satan, evil, trial, affliction, corruption, stench, filth, and impurity, and all of that according to them is the act of God, so how can the difference mentioned be valid, and the nullification of similarity between the creation of God and the creation of other than Him?
Source pages p. 895

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by the author. He points out that according to the opponent's own principles, God's acts include things that are lowly, evil, and defective (e.g., Satan, evil, trial, stench). Since all these are considered acts of God, the opponent cannot legitimately claim a clear distinction between divine and human creation that would prevent similarity. The rhetorical question 'how can the difference mentioned be valid?' undermines the opponent's position.

Correction: The author is not attributing evil to God; rather, he is using the opponent's own view to show inconsistency. The opponent claims a distinction, but their view implies God creates evil, which undermines that distinction.

C) Only acts of obedience.

Key Takeaway
The presence of evil among God's acts, according to the opponent, refutes any claim of a unique distinction between divine and human creation.
