Similarity of Actions Does Not Imply Similarity of Essences
Objection and answer on similarity of actions leading to similarity with God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam theologians argue that similarity in actions between God and humans does not necessitate similarity in essences, and how the distinction between eternal and temporal knowledge resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Similar actions do not make similar essences — knowledge's nature differs.

This lesson presents a key objection from those who argue that if human actions resemble God's in bringing things into existence, then God and humans would be similar. The author responds by distinguishing the type of knowledge (eternal vs. temporal) and showing that accidental attributes like effort do not affect essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the opponent's claim that similarity in actions would imply similarity between God and creation because both are knowers.
The opponent uses a parity argument from actions to essences.
2
Understand the response
The author first recalls that some actions (involuntary movements) are attributed to God, yet they resemble voluntary actions, so the opponent's premise fails. Then he distinguishes God's eternal knowledge from human temporal knowledge to break the similarity.
The author uses both a refutation of the premise and a distinction between types of knowledge.
3
Analyze the Najjāriyya principle
According to the Najjāriyya, similarity is by essence, not by an added meaning. God is knowing by Himself, but humans are not, so no similarity.
This theological principle is invoked to strengthen the argument.
4
Reflect on the blackness analogy
The author compares the situation to two instances of blackness, one owned and one not. Ownership does not affect the essence of blackness, just as effort does not affect the essence of temporal origination.
The analogy illustrates that accidental attributes do not change essential similarity or difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لو كان يقع الاشتباه بين فعلنا وفعل الله تعالى لأن كل واحد من الفعلين إخراج من العدم إلى الوجود وإحداث لكان الاشتباه بين الله وبين الخلق ثابتا لأنه تعالى عالم والعبد أيضا عالم.
قيل له: هذا الكلام فاسد، فإن الكلام قد سبق أن حركة المرتعش وحركات العروق النابضة فعل الله تعالى عندهم والحركة الاختيارية فعل العبد، وبينهما متشابهه ولا يمكن إثبات مخالفة بينهما في ذاتيهما على ما قررنا.
فأما الله تعالى فهو عالم بعلم أزلي والعبد عالم بعلم محدث، فلا يكون بين العالمين مشابهة، وعلى أصل النجارية المشتبهان مشتبهان بأنفسهما لا باشتباه هو معنى وراء المشتبهين والباري عالم بنفسه وليس المحدث عالما بنفسه، فلا يكون بينهما مشابهة على أصلهم ، فأما المحدثان فلم يكونا ثم كانا فاتفقا بأنفسهما في معنى الحدوث، وقول القائل : أحدهما حدث لا بعلاج ولا تعب، والآخر حدث بعلاج وتعب، ليس بنفي الاشتباه عن المحدثين بأنفسهما إذ ليس الحدوث إلا الوجود من العدم، وهذا المعنى لا يختلف في حق حادث وحادث وإن كان أحدهما حدث بعلاج وتعب والآخر حدث لا بعلاج وتعب، إذ العلاج والتعب معنى وراء نفس الحادث ونفي معنى وراء نفس الحادث وإثبات ذلك المعنى في حق حادث لا يوجب تغيرا في ذاتيهما.
ألا ترى أن من قال: هذا سواد لزيد، وهذا سواد ليس لزيد لا الاختلاف بين السوادين ولا زوال الاشتباه، فكذا هذا.
If they say: If similarity were to occur between our action and the action of God because each of the two actions is an extraction from non-existence to existence and origination, then similarity would be established between God and creation because He is knowing and the servant is also knowing. It was said to him: This speech is corrupt, for the discourse has already preceded that the movement of the tremulous and the movements of the pulsating veins are the act of God according to them, and the voluntary movement is the act of the servant, and between them there is similarity, and it is not possible to establish a difference between them in their essences according to what we have established. As for God, He is knowing with eternal knowledge, and the servant is knowing with temporal knowledge, so there is no similarity between the two knowers. And according to the principle of the Najjāriyya, the two similar things are similar by themselves, not by a similarity that is a meaning beyond the two similar things. The Creator is knowing by Himself, and the temporal being is not knowing by itself, so there is no similarity between them according to their principle. As for the two temporal beings, they were not and then they came to be, so they agreed by themselves in the meaning of temporal origination. The statement of the one who says: one of them came to be without effort and toil, and the other came to be with effort and toil, does not negate similarity between the two temporal beings in themselves, since temporal origination is nothing but existence from non-existence, and this meaning does not differ in regard to one temporal being and another, even if one came to be with effort and toil and the other came to be without effort and toil, for effort and toil are a meaning beyond the temporal being itself, and negating a meaning beyond the temporal being itself and affirming that meaning in regard to a temporal being does not cause a change in their essences. Do you not see that the one who says: This is blackness belonging to Zayd, and this is blackness not belonging to Zayd – there is no difference between the two blacknesses nor removal of similarity? So likewise this.
Source pages p. 895

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The opponent argues that if similarity occurs between human and divine actions because both bring from non-existence to existence, then God and humans would be similar since both are knowers. The author responds by first recalling that some actions (like involuntary movements) are divine according to the opponent, yet those and voluntary actions share similarity, so the opponent's premise fails. Then he distinguishes: God's knowledge is eternal, human knowledge is temporal, so no similarity. He invokes the Najjāriyya principle that similar things are similar by themselves, not by an added meaning; God is knowing by Himself, while the temporal being is not, so no similarity. For two temporal beings, they agree in the meaning of temporal origination (existence from non-existence). The addition of effort or toil does not change the essence, just like ownership does not change blackness. The example of two blacknesses (one owned, one not) shows that accidental attributes do not affect essential similarity.

Key Takeaway
Similarity in action does not necessitate similarity in essence because the nature of knowledge (eternal vs temporal) and accidental attributes do not change essence.

Motion as General Name vs. Obedience as Specific Attribute
Objection and answer on motion as general name and obedience/disobedience as specific attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why obedience or disobedience is not an essential attribute of motion, but a name derived from divine command.

Core Idea
Obedience and disobedience are legal names, not essential attributes of actions.

This passage presents an objection and answer regarding the relationship between motion and its moral classification. The opponent argues that similarity of movements depends on specific attributes like obedience/disobedience. The author refutes this by showing these are extrinsic, not essential, attributes, using the example of stillness and the analogy of blackness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the opponent's claim: motion is a general name; similarity occurs by specific attribute; obedience/disobedience are specific attributes differentiating movements.
Notice the opponent assumes moral attributes are essential.
2
See the author's two-part response
First, recall the earlier refutation in the tashbīh discussion. Then, the positive argument: obedience/disobedience do not refer back to the essence; they are names based on command.
The key phrase: 'بل ذلك اسم ثبت لورود الأمر به'
3
Examine the examples given
The author gives the example of stillness, which can be both obedience and disobedience, showing these are extrinsic. Then the analogy of blackness—two blacknesses similar despite different owners.
These examples reinforce that essential similarity remains despite accidental differences.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal implication
Understand that moral qualities are not essential to actions; they are legal designations. This preserves the idea that Allah's attributes are not subject to change.
This connects to broader issues of divine attributes and tashbīh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إن قولنا : حركة اسم عام والاشتباه يكون بالصفة الخاصة، وكونها طاعة ومعصية صفة خاصة في الحركة، لأنها تتنوع إلى الطاعة والمعصية. قلنا: قد مر إبطال هذا في مسألة التشبيه ، ثم نقول: إن كون الحركة طاعة أو معصية ليس براجع إلى الذات بل ذلك اسم ثبت لورود الأمر به، فلا تغير به الصفة الراجعة إلى الذات لأن الذات لا تتغير به.
ألا ترى أن ضد الحركة وهو السكون قد يوصف بهما.
والذي يحقق هذا : اشتباه سواد زید بسواد ،عمرو، ولا يقال السواد اسم عام
If they say: Our statement that 'movement' is a general name and similarity occurs by the specific attribute, and its being obedience or disobedience is a specific attribute in movement, because it is divided into obedience and disobedience. We say: The refutation of this has already passed in the issue of tashbīh, then we say: That movement is obedience or disobedience does not refer back to the essence, rather that is a name established due to the command coming with it, so the attribute referring back to the essence is not changed by it, because the essence is not changed by it. Do you not see that the opposite of movement, which is stillness, may be described by both. And that which verifies this is the similarity of the blackness of Zayd to the blackness of 'Amr, and it is not said that 'blackness' is a general name.

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The opponent claims that 'movement' is a general name; similarity depends on specific attributes, and obedience/disobedience are specific attributes that differentiate movements. The author responds by recalling an earlier refutation (in the issue of tashbīh) and then argues: being obedience or disobedience is not an essential attribute; it is a name derived from a command, so it does not alter the essence. He gives an example: stillness (the opposite of movement) can be described as both obedience and disobedience (in different contexts), showing that these are extrinsic attributes. Then he uses the analogy of two blacknesses: one belonging to Zayd, one to 'Amr; they are similar despite different ownership, and we do not say 'blackness' is merely a general name while similarity depends on ownership. The passage cuts off, but the argument is that essential similarity remains despite accidental differences.

Accidental Attributes Do Not Affect Essential Similarity
Nasafi refutes the objection about motion and specific attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidental attributes like obedience or disobedience do not affect the essential similarity of motions according to Nasafi.

Core Idea
Obedience does not change the essence of motion.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles an objection that attempts to deny essential similarity among motions using the idea that specific attributes like obedience create divisions. He argues that such attributes are merely legal designations, not essential properties, and illustrates with examples. Let's see how he refutes the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the objectors claim that motion is a general name and similarity depends on specific attributes like obedience or disobedience.
The objectors argue that the division of motion into obedience and disobedience prevents essential similarity.
2
Identify Nasafi's response structure
Notice that Nasafi first references a previous discussion (tashbīh) and then presents a new argument. The new argument is that obedience/disobedience are not essential to motion but are names given by command.
This is a two-part response: reference to prior refutation, then original reasoning.
3
Analyze the analogies
Nasafi gives two analogies: stillness can be described as obedience/disobedience, and blackness of two individuals is essentially similar despite different substrates. Understand how these support his point.
The blackness analogy is especially powerful to show that division by substrate does not negate essential similarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إن قولنا: حركة اسم عام والاشتباه يكون بالصفة الخاصة، وكونها طاعة ومعصية صفة خاصة في الحركة، لأنها تتنوع إلى الطاعة والمعصية. قلنا: قد مر إبطال هذا في مسألة التشبيه، ثم نقول: إن كون الحركة طاعة أو معصية ليس براجع إلى الذات بل ذلك اسم ثبت لورود الأمر به، فلا تغير به الصفة الراجعة إلى الذات لأن الذات لا تتغير به. ألا ترى أن ضد الحركة وهو السكون قد يوصف بهما. والذي يحقق هذا: اشتباه سواد زيد بسواد عمرو، ولا يقال السواد اسم عام ينقسم باعتبار انقسام المحل فلا يكونان مشتبهين بعمومهما بل باشتباههما عندكم لما أن الانقسام لأجل المحل ليس براجع إلى الذات، فلم يوجب عمومه، فكذا هذا.
If they say: 'Our saying: motion is a general name, and similarity occurs by the specific attribute, and its being obedience or disobedience is a specific attribute of motion, because it is divided into obedience and disobedience.' We say: The refutation of this has already passed in the question of tashbīh. Then we say: The fact that motion is obedience or disobedience does not refer back to the essence; rather, that is a name established due to the command concerning it, so the attribute referring back to the essence is not changed by it, because the essence is not changed by it. Do you not see that the opposite of motion, which is stillness, may be described by both? And what verifies this: the similarity (ishtibāh) of Zayd's blackness to 'Amr's blackness; and it is not said that blackness is a general name that is divided according to the division of the substrate, so that the two are not similar by virtue of their generality but by virtue of their similarity according to you, because division due to the substrate does not refer back to the essence, so it does not necessitate its generality. So likewise this.
Source pages p. 896

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objectors claim that motion (ḥaraka) is a general name, and similarity (ishtibāh) occurs due to a specific attribute (al-ṣifa al-khāṣṣa), such as being obedience or disobedience. They imply that motion cannot be similar in essence because it is divided into different types. Nasafi responds by first noting that this point has already been refuted in the discussion of tashbīh (anthropomorphism). Then he argues that being obedience or disobedience is not an essential attribute of motion; rather, it is a name given based on divine command (al-amr). Therefore, it does not change the essence. He gives an analogy: the opposite of motion, stillness (sukūn), can also be described as obedience or disobedience, showing that these attributes are accidental. He further strengthens this by the example of blackness (sawād): Zayd's blackness and 'Amr's blackness are similar in essence even though they belong to different substrates. The division of blackness due to different substrates does not affect the essential similarity. Thus, the division of motion into obedience and disobedience does not negate essential similarity among motions.

الحركة (al-ḥaraka)
السكون (al-sukūn)
السواد (al-sawād)
المحل (al-maḥall)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Specific attributes like obedience/disobedience change the essence of motion.
Correction: Nasafi argues they are legal designations based on divine command, not essential attributes, so they do not affect essential similarity.

B) The similarity of motion and stillness

Key Takeaway

Refuting the Impossibility of Two Wills Moving One Stone
The author refutes the opponent's claim that two wills cannot coexist to move one stone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the opponent's claim that two wills cannot move one object, using the distinction between necessary and inferential knowledge.

Core Idea
Two wills can move one object; spatial impossibility is the real issue, not conflict of powers.

This lesson examines a classic kalam argument about the coexistence of two wills for a single act. The author begins with a premise conceded by the opponent and gradually exposes a confusion between necessary and inferential knowledge. Reading this passage will sharpen your understanding of how Maturidi theologians construct refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the complete Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the dialectical style: the author directly addresses the opponent with 'Then we say to him'.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the opponent's stance and the author's starting point
The opponent concedes that God can create two equal men but denies their wills can move one stone. The author questions why, if each can will individually, both cannot will together.
The author does not argue from scratch; he uses a conceded premise.
3
Analyze the example of the intermediate part
The opponent brings an example of two wills aiming for the same intermediate part. The author explains the impossibility is due to spatial occupation, not conflict of wills.
The will to move to that part is inseparable from the movement itself.
4
Understand the distinction between necessary and inferential knowledge
The author argues that the impossibility of two bodies occupying one space is known necessarily, while the impossibility of one object of power under two powers is not necessary; if it were, it would be inferential.
This distinction is crucial for the refutation.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the author uses logical distinctions to dismantle the opponent's analogy. The passage ends with a reminder that necessary knowledge is infallible.
This is a classic example of Maturidi dialectic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول له: إنك سلمت أن تخليق الله رجلين مستويين في القوة والبنية من جملة الممكنات، وإنما جعلت المستحيل وجود إرادتهما لتحريك ذلك الجسم ويجوز وجود إرادة كل واحد منهما لتحريك الجسم والاعتماد عليه للتحريك على الانفراد فبعد ذلك نقول: إذا وجدت من أحدهما إرادة تحريك الحجر لماذا امتنع وجود إرادة تحريك ذلك الآخر، ولا مضادة بين الإرادتين والفعلين لتفرق محليهما، ولا فساد لمحل قدرة أحدهما عند ورود إرادة الآخر، فإن تعلق بالمثل الذي ضربه قلنا هناك : إنما استحال وجود إرادة الاثنين لأن إرادتهما للتحرك إلى الجزء المتوسط لا تقع إلا مع حكمها وهو التحرك، ولما استحال شغلهما ذلك الجزء المتوسط استحال وجود ما لا يقع إلا مع حركتيهما وهو الإرادة ولا استحالة في وجود الاعتماد من الاثنين على ذلك الجسم وتحريكهما إياه، فإن نوزعنا في كون إرادة التحرك التحرك مع نثبت ذلك بالدليل على أنا بنينا ذلك على أصلنا لدفع الإلزام للإلزام على الخصم وهو مستقيم، ولأن العلم باستحالة شغل اثنين جزءًا واحدًا من الممكن ضروري والعلم باستحالة دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين ليس بضروري لو كان لكان استدلاليًا وبإفضائه إلى جعل ما هو ممكن ممتنعا علم بطلان الاستدلال، وفيما استشهد به الأمر بخلافه، لأن ما علم ضرورة لا يتصور خطأه وبطلانه، فلا يمكن جعل أحدهما نظير للآخر، والله الموفق.
Then we say to him: You have conceded that God creating two men equal in strength and constitution is among the possible things, and you only deemed impossible the existence of their will to move that body, while the existence of the will of each one of them to move the body and reliance upon it for movement individually is possible. So after that we say: If from one of them the will to move the stone occurred, why is it impossible for the will to move that stone to occur from the other? There is no opposition between the two wills and the two acts because their loci are separate, nor is there corruption of the locus of the power of one of them upon the occurrence of the other's will. If he adheres to the example he gave, we say there: The existence of the will of both is impossible only because their willing to move to the intermediate part does not occur except with its concomitant, which is movement. And since their occupying that intermediate part is impossible, the existence of that which does not occur except with their two movements, namely the will, is impossible. But there is no impossibility in the existence of reliance from both upon that body and their moving it. If we are disputed about whether the will to move is [concomitant with] movement, we will prove that by evidence, because we built that upon our principle to repel the objection against the opponent, and that is sound. And because the knowledge of the impossibility of two occupying one part is necessary, while the knowledge of the impossibility of one maqdur falling under the power of two agents is not necessary; if it were, it would be inferential, and by leading to making what is possible impossible, the invalidity of the inference is known. And regarding what he adduced, the matter is otherwise, because what is known necessarily cannot be imagined to be erroneous or invalid, so one cannot be made analogous to the other. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 909

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent (likely a Mu'tazili) who claims that it is impossible for two wills to exist to move a single stone. The author begins by stating a premise the opponent already accepts: God can create two men equal in strength, and it is possible for each to will to move the stone individually. The opponent argues that two wills for the same movement cannot coexist. The author counters by noting that there is no opposition between the two wills because they reside in different loci, and no corruption occurs in the locus of one power when the other wills. If the opponent brings up the example of two wills both aiming for an intermediate part, the author explains that the impossibility is not due to conflict of wills but because the intermediate part cannot be occupied by both bodies simultaneously; the will to move to that part is inseparable from the movement itself. Since the movement cannot occur due to spatial impossibility, the will also cannot occur. However, if the two agents are simply relying on the stone from different sides without aiming for the same point, there is no impossibility. The author then strengthens his argument by asserting that the impossibility of two bodies occupying one space is known necessarily, while the impossibility of one object of power (maqdur) being under two powers is not necessarily known; if it were, it would be inferential and lead to absurdities. Thus the two cases are not analogous. The passage ends with 'And God is the Grantor of success.'

Key Terms
إرادة
قدرة
مقدور
جزء متوسط
علم ضروري
استدلالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two wills cannot coexist for a single object because powers conflict.
Correction: The author shows that conflict is not due to powers but to spatial constraints; two wills can coexist if their loci are separate.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible for two wills to move to the same intermediate part?
A) Because two wills cannot coexist
B) Because their wills conflict
C) Because they cannot both occupy that part
D) Because it would require two powers on one object

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two wills moving one object is not due to conflict of wills but to spatial impossibility, and necessary knowledge cannot be analogized to inferential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed two opposing desires cannot coexist in your heart, only to later realize the real conflict was elsewhere? This lesson distinguishes between genuine impossibility and mere assumption. Where in your life have you labeled something as impossible when it was only uncomfortable, and what would change if you reexamined it?
?
The author argues that necessary knowledge cannot be analogized to inferential. Are there beliefs you hold as 'certain' that are actually based on weak analogies? Take a moment to identify one such belief and test it: is it truly necessary or merely inferred?
?
This passage shows that two wills need not conflict if their loci are separate. In your relationships, do you assume your will must conflict with another's because you want the same thing? Could there be a different locus—a shared goal from separate paths—that allows both wills to coexist without opposition?

The Objection of Two Men and the Indivisible Particle
An objection using an indivisible particle to argue for joint agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the kalam argument from preference (tarjih) is used to challenge the idea that a single act can have two agents.

Core Idea
Without a reason for preference, no act can be assigned to one equal agent over another.

This passage presents a specific kalam objection. Kalam scholars often used illustrative examples to test logical consistency. Here, the example of an indivisible particle on a board moved by two men is used to force the opponent to either accept joint agency or face a contradiction. Let's explore the logic behind this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and the literal English translation. Let the wording of the objection sink in before analyzing it.
The passage is one sentence but dense with logical implications.
2
Understand the scenario
Visualize the situation: an indivisible particle on a board, two men each holding one end of the board, both equal in all attributes. They move the board together, dislodging the particle.
The particle is indivisible, so motion is an all-or-nothing property.
3
Grasp the logical conclusion
Since the two men are equal in every relevant aspect, there is no reason (no preferentiating factor) to attribute the movement of the particle solely to one rather than the other. Therefore, the movement must be attributed to both equally.
This conclusion challenges the opponent's assumption that an act can only have one agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألزموه أيضا أن جزءًا لا يتجزأ لو وضع على لوح وقبض على طرفي اللوح رجلان فحركاه فأزعج الجزء وكان الجزء مع أجزاء اللوح فردًا لابد أن حركة الجزء كانت بينهما لانعدام دليل ترجيح أحد الرجلين لاستوائهما في المعاني كلها.
And they also obligated him that if an indivisible particle is placed on a board and two men grasp the two ends of the board and move it, dislodging the particle, and the particle together with the parts of the board is one, then necessarily the movement of the particle would be between them due to the absence of evidence favoring one of the two men, since they are equal in all attributes.
Source pages p. 909

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the opponent's position using the example of an indivisible particle (part) placed on a board. Two men hold the ends of the board and move it, causing the particle to move. Since the particle is indivisible and the men are equal in all relevant attributes, there is no reason to attribute the movement of the particle to one rather than the other. Therefore, the movement must be attributed to both equally. This objection pressures the opponent to either accept that an act can be from two agents or face a logical difficulty.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ (indivisible particle)
لوح (board)
ترجيح (preference)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That indivisible particles are physical atoms in the modern sense.
Correction: In kalam, 'indivisible particle' (juz' la yatajazza') is a theoretical entity that cannot be divided further, used for logical analysis, not necessarily corresponding to physical atoms.

Checkpoint
What is the significance of the particle moving when two men move the board?
A) It shows the act is due to one agent
B) It shows the act must be attributed to both
C) It proves the particle is divisible
D) It shows the board is not real

Key Takeaway
The objection demonstrates that if two agents are identical in all respects, their action cannot be exclusively attributed to one, challenging the notion of independent agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, when a success occurs, do you honestly examine whether you alone deserve the credit or whether others (e.g., family, teachers, colleagues) were equally or more instrumental? How does the lesson's logical demand for a 'reason for preference' challenge your natural tendency to claim exclusive agency?
?
When you make a mistake, do you ever consider that the failure might be jointly produced by your circumstances, upbringing, or others' actions? Are you willing to accept shared responsibility, even when it's easier to blame yourself or someone else entirely? Let this lesson probe your heart: can you point to clear evidence that the error was solely yours?

The Servant's Act and Freedom from Compulsion
The author concludes that proving kasb is unnecessary and focuses on showing the servant's act and freedom from compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why proving the essence of kasb is not necessary and that the focus should be on affirming the servant's act and his freedom from compulsion.

Core Idea
The servant acts freely without being compelled, and kasb's essence is secondary.

After establishing the two proofs that the servant cannot have creative power yet still has an act, Imam al-Nasafi concludes that it is unnecessary to delve into the exact nature of kasb. Instead, he emphasizes the need to demonstrate that the servant is not compelled, that he genuinely acts, and that his power is connected to his own act. This lesson clarifies this pivotal turning point in the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly and then read the literal translation. Notice the transition from the previous proofs to the new focus.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا حاجة بنا إلى بيان مائية الكسب'.
2
Identify the author's conclusion about kasb
The author says there is no need to explain the essence of kasb. Why does he say that? Because the proofs already establish that the servant has an act without needing to analyze kasb's nature.
This is a surprising turn for those expecting a detailed theory of kasb.
3
List the three affirmations
The author then lists three things to show: the servant is not compelled, he has an act, and his power is connected to his act. Write them down and consider why each is important.
These affirmations are the core of the Maturidi position on free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بإحاطة العلم على ما ذكرت من مجموع الدليلين وثبوت المعرفة باستحالة ثبوت قدرة التخليق للعبد وثبوت الفعل له ظهر أن لا حاجة بنا إلى بيان مائية الكسب بنا بل غنية عن الاشتغال به، وعلينا أن نبين أنه ليس بمجبور، وأن له فعلاً وأن قدرته متعلقة بمقدوره وهو فعله وإن كان لا وقوع لذلك في أوهامنا على ما قررنا، ثم نشتغل ببيان ذلك ليعلم المسترشد المراد بذلك فنقول :
Then, by encompassing knowledge of what I mentioned from the two proofs combined and the establishment of knowledge through the impossibility of establishing the power of creation for the servant and the establishment of action for him, it became apparent that we have no need to explain the essence of kasb (acquisition), but rather we are independent of occupying ourselves with it. And it is incumbent upon us to show that he is not compelled, and that he has an act, and that his power is connected to his object of power, which is his act, even if that does not occur in our estimations as we established. Then we occupy ourselves with explaining that so that the seeker of guidance may know the intended meaning, and we say:
Source pages p. 917

Commentary
This passage is a transitional conclusion and a statement of intent. The author (Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi) argues that after establishing both proofs—that the servant cannot have creative power and that he nevertheless has an act—it is unnecessary to delve into the exact nature (mā'iyya) of kasb. Instead, the focus should be on demonstrating that the servant is not compelled (majbūr), that he genuinely acts, and that his power (qudra) is related to his own act (fi'l). The author emphasizes that this is sufficient for the seeker of guidance. The passage ends by introducing the subsequent discussion with 'we say' (fa-naqūl), setting up the following definitions.

Correction: The author does not deny the reality of kasb; rather, after establishing the proofs, he considers it unnecessary to further analyze its essence. The focus is on affirming that the servant has a genuine act and is not compelled, which is sufficient for the seeker of guidance.

Three Definitions of Kasb and Khalaq
Three definitions distinguishing kasb from khalq from the Ashab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three definitions used by the Ash'ari/Māturīdī scholars to distinguish between human acquisition (kasb) and divine creation (khalq), and why the servant is called an acquirer rather than a creator.

Core Idea
Three complementary definitions clarify that creation belongs to Allah alone, while human acts are acquired.

In this passage, the author presents three definitions from the school that each attempt to draw a clear line between human action and divine creation. These definitions are not contradictory; they approach the same distinction from different angles — locus of power, presence of instruments, and independence of the agent. Understanding them helps us grasp the classical Sunni position that Allah is the sole Creator while humans genuinely acquire their acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the definitions and their conclusion.
Notice the phrase 'اختلفت عبارات أصحابنا' – the school had multiple expressions.
2
Identify the three definitions
Look for the three definitions introduced by 'فقال بعضهم', 'وقيل', and 'وقيل'. Note the different criteria: locus (محل), instrument (آلة), and independence (انفراد).
3
Apply the definitions to the servant
Read the part starting with 'والعبد لا يصح انفراده'. See how the third definition is used to argue that the servant cannot be a creator because he cannot be independent.
The key is that the servant lacks power of invention (اختراع).
4
Conclude the doctrine
Understand the final statement: Allah alone is independent in bringing into existence (متفرد في الإيجاد), so He is the Creator. The servant is an acquirer (مكتسب).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلفت عبارات أصحابنا رحمهم الله - في ذلك وفي الفرق بينه وبين الخلق. فقال بعضهم : كل مقدور وقع في محل قدرته فهو كسب ، وما وقع لا في محل قدرته فهو خلق، واسم الفعل يشملهما . وقيل: ما وقع بآلة لهو كسب ، وما وقع لا بآلة فهو خلق. وقيل: ما وقع المقدور به من حيث يصح انفراد القادر به فهو خلق، وما وقع مقدوره به مع تعذر انفراد القادر به فهو كسب، واسم الفعل يقع على مطلق ما وقع مقدوره به من غير اختصاص بما يصح الانفراد به والعبد لا يصح انفراده بتحصيل مقدوره على ما قررنا من استحالة ثبوت قدرة الاختراع له فلم يكن خالقا بل كان مكتسبا، والله تعالى متفرد في الإيجاد، لا حاجة به إلى غيره فيما يوجده فكان خالقًا.
The expressions of our companions—may God have mercy on them—differed regarding that and regarding the distinction between it and creation. Some of them said: Every object of power that occurs in the locus of his power is kasb, and what occurs not in the locus of his power is creation, and the name 'act' encompasses both. And it was said: What occurs with an instrument is kasb, and what occurs without an instrument is creation. And it was said: What occurs as an object of power by which it is valid for the powerful one to be independent is creation, and what occurs as his object of power by which it is impossible for the powerful one to be independent is kasb. And the name 'act' applies to the absolute occurrence of his object of power without specification of what is valid for independence. And the servant cannot be independent in producing his object of power according to what we established from the impossibility of establishing the power of invention for him. Thus he is not a creator but rather an acquirer. And God Almighty is alone in bringing into existence, having no need of another in what He brings into existence, so He is the Creator.
Source pages p. 917

Commentary
This passage presents three definitions from the Ash'ari/Māturīdī school (Aṣḥābinā) to distinguish between kasb (acquisition) and khalq (creation). The first definition focuses on the locus (maḥall) of power: if the act occurs in the locus of the agent's power, it is kasb; if it occurs outside that locus, it is khalq. The name 'act' (fi'l) covers both. The second definition uses the presence or absence of an instrument (āla): acts performed with an instrument are kasb; those without are khalq. The third definition emphasizes independence (infirād): if the agent can produce the act independently, it is khalq; if the agent cannot be independent in producing it, it is kasb. The author then applies these definitions to the servant: since the servant cannot independently produce his object of power (as established by the impossibility of creative power), he is not a creator (khāliq) but an acquirer (muktasib). God alone is independent in bringing into existence, so He is the Creator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three definitions are contradictory or inconsistent.
Correction: They approach the same distinction from different angles – locus, instrument, and independence – and all lead to the same conclusion: creation is exclusive to Allah, and human acts are acquired.

Checkpoint
According to the first definition, what is khalaq?
A) What occurs in the locus of the agent's power
B) What occurs not in the locus of the agent's power
C) What occurs with an instrument
D) What occurs independently

Key Takeaway
The three definitions consistently affirm that creation is exclusively Allah's, while human action is acquisition that depends on Allah's power.

Definitions of Khalq and Kasb in Maturidi Theology
Nasafi defines khalq and kasb with two definitions and concludes God alone is Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definitions of creation (khalq) and acquisition (kasb) and why the servant is not a creator.

Core Idea
Creating requires independent power; the servant has none, so only God creates.

In this passage, Nasafi sets down the precise meanings of khalq and kasb, two terms essential for understanding human action in the Maturidi school. By distinguishing actions that require independent power from those that do not, he guards against attributing any creative power to the servant while still affirming human responsibility. Let's examine the definitions and their conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the two definitions and the concluding argument.
Notice the two 'qila' phrases introducing the definitions.
2
Identify the two definitions
The first definition uses the presence or absence of an instrument. The second uses the possibility of the agent's independence. Both lead to the same distinction.
3
Understand the conclusion
Nasafi applies the second definition to the servant and God. The servant cannot be independent, so he is not a creator but an acquirer. God is independent, so He alone creates.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is it important that only God creates? How does kasb preserve human responsibility without giving humans creative power?
This connects to the broader Maturidi doctrine of qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: ما وقع بآلة لهو كسب، وما وقع لا بآلة فهو خلق. وقيل: ما وقع المقدور به من حيث يصح انفراد القادر به فهو خلق، وما وقع مقدوره به مع تعذر انفراد القادر به فهو كسب، واسم الفعل يقع على مطلق ما وقع مقدوره به من غير اختصاص بما يصح الانفراد به والعبد لا يصح انفراده بتحصيل مقدوره على ما قررنا من استحالة ثبوت قدرة الاختراع له فلم يكن خالقا بل كان مكتسبا، والله تعالى متفرد في الإيجاد، لا حاجة به إلى غيره فيما يوجده فكان خالقًا.
And it was said: What occurs with an instrument is kasb (acquisition), and what occurs without an instrument is khalq (creation). And it was said: What occurs by the power (al-maqdur bihi) from the aspect of the possibility of the agent being independent with it is khalq, and what occurs by his power with the impossibility of the agent being independent with it is kasb. And the name 'act' (al-fi'l) applies to the absolute what occurs by his power without specification to what is possible to be independent with. And the servant is not valid for his independence in producing his power - based on what we established of the impossibility of establishing power of invention (qudrat al-ikhtira') for him - so he is not a creator (khaliq) but rather an acquirer (muktasib). And God Almighty is unique in bringing into existence, having no need of another in what He brings into existence, so He is the Creator.
Source pages p. 918

Commentary
This passage defines the terms khalq (creation) and kasb (acquisition) and distinguishes them. Nasafi begins by citing two definitions. The first definition: an action that occurs with an instrument is called kasb, while an action that occurs without an instrument is called khalq. The second, more detailed, definition: an action that occurs by an agent's power (qudra) such that the agent can independently bring it about is khalq; an action that occurs by the agent's power but the agent cannot independently bring it about is kasb. The term 'act' (fi'l) applies broadly to both, without specification. Then Nasafi draws the conclusion: the servant (al-'abd) cannot independently bring about his actions because he lacks the power of invention (qudrat al-ikhtira') - a power that creates ex nihilo. Therefore, the servant is not a creator (khaliq) but rather an acquirer (muktasib). God Almighty, on the other hand, is unique in bringing into existence (al-ijad) and has no need of any other, so He is the true Creator. This establishes the Maturidi doctrine of kasb, affirming human responsibility while denying independent causal power.

Key Terms
خلق
كسب
قدرة الاختراع
مكتسب
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kasb means the servant creates his own actions.
Correction: Kasb denies creation by the servant; the servant cannot create.

Checkpoint
According to the definitions given, what distinguishes khalq from kasb?
A) Khalq occurs without an instrument, kasb with an instrument.
B) Khalq is the act of God, kasb is the act of man.
C) Both are the same.
D) Khalq is older than kasb.

Key Takeaway
In Maturidi theology, creation requires independent power, which only God possesses; human actions are acquired (kasb), not created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something good, do you truly believe that only God created that success? If you are just an acquirer, how must your gratitude change starting today?
?
You claim to believe that only God creates. But do you act as if your own efforts create outcomes? What step can you take tonight to show you understand you are merely an acquirer?
?
If you truly internalized that you cannot independently produce anything, would you still fear failure as much? What would change in your daily struggles if you saw every result as God's creation?

Principle of Attribution: Names Refer to the One with the Attribute
Nasafi establishes the rule that names derived from actions return to the one in whom the action inheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that names derived from actions or attributes refer to the one in whom that action or attribute inheres, not to the one who creates it.

Core Idea
Names follow inherence, not creation.

In theological debates, a common question arises: if Allah creates disbelief, why is He not called a disbeliever? Imam Nasafi resolves this by establishing a fundamental rule: the name derived from a meaning always refers to the entity in which that meaning inheres, regardless of who caused it. This principle applies to all attributes and actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the key terms: 'الاسم المقدر عن المعنى' (the name derived from the meaning) and 'من قام به المعنى' (the one in whom the meaning inheres).
2
Understand the principle
The principle states that the name belongs to the subject that possesses the attribute, not to the one that brings it into existence. This applies whether the action is voluntary (kasb) or involuntary.
3
Apply to the objection
The objection 'why not call Allah a disbeliever since He creates disbelief?' is invalid because disbelief inheres in the human, not in Allah. This resolves the theological problem.
This principle is crucial for understanding kasb and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل : أن الاسم المقدر عن المعنى يكون راجعا إلى من قام به المعنى كان ذلك كسبًا له كالحركة الاختيارية أو لم يكن كالحركة الضرورية لا إلى موجد المعنى فكان هذا السؤال باطلاً.
And the conclusion: that the name derived from the meaning refers to the one in whom the meaning inheres, whether that is an acquisition for him like voluntary movement or not like involuntary movement, not to the creator of the meaning. So this question is invalid.
Source pages p. 924

Commentary
This passage is a general principle summarizing the previous discussion. It says that when we attribute a name like 'mover' or 'dead' to something, it refers to the subject in which that attribute or action inheres, not to the one who brings it into existence. This applies whether the action is voluntary (kasb, acquired) or involuntary (like heat). Therefore, calling a person a 'disbeliever' is based on the fact that disbelief inheres in him, not that he creates it. Hence the objection 'why not call Allah the disbeliever since He creates disbelief?' is invalid.

Key Terms
الاسم المقدر
المعنى
الكسب
الحركة الاختيارية
الحركة الضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah creates an attribute in a person, then Allah should be named by that attribute.
Correction: The name derived from an attribute refers to the one in whom the attribute inheres, not to the one who creates it. Creation does not imply inherence.

Checkpoint
According to the principle, to whom does the name 'mover' belong?
A) The one who creates movement
B) The one in whom movement inheres
C) The one who causes movement indirectly
D) Both the creator and the inherer

Key Takeaway
Names derived from attributes always refer to the subject in which the attribute inheres, not to the creator of that attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you recognize that the sin inheres in you, even though Allah creates it? In what ways do you still take full responsibility for your actions, knowing that He is the Creator of your deeds?
?
If Allah creates disbelief in a person, and you call that person a disbeliever, does that make you unjust? How should this principle change the way you judge others, knowing that names return to inherence not creation?
?
Consider a time when you were praised for a good deed. Did you attribute that goodness to yourself as if you created it, or did you realize it inheres in you only by Allah's creation? How does this principle humble you in moments of pride?

The True Meaning of 'Directions of Action'
Nasafi explains the intended relational meaning of 'جهات الفعل' and refutes Mu'tazila denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the intended meaning of 'directions of action' according to the Maturidi school and why the Mu'tazila's denial was based on a misunderstanding.

Core Idea
Directions of action are relational, not physical—a key distinction in kalam.

Nasafi clarifies a subtle term often misunderstood by opponents. The phrase 'جهات الفعل' (directions of action) does not refer to physical directions like north or south, but to relational descriptions that depend on the agent. He then explains why the Mu'tazila's rejection of this concept reveals their ignorance of the opponents' real position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the passage carefully. Nasafi states that by 'directions' they mean relational descriptions, not physical directions that can be sensed.
The key phrase is 'من حيث العبارة والمعنى'.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila objection
The Mu'tazila argued that directions are a 'being' for bodies and that an accident cannot have multiple directions. Find this in the text.
Their claim is based on a literal reading of 'directions'.
3
See the Maturidi response
Note that al-Maturidi mentioned directions of action only as a concession to correct the school and refute falsehood, not because it was necessary.
This shows the method of refutation within kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن شمال وخلف وقدام، بل يعنون ما بينا من وجود الجهات من حيث العبارة والمعنى دون الجهات التي يصح منها محاسة ما يلاقيها وبمعرفة تفسير الجهات تظهر جهالة المعتزلة بإنكارهم ذلك، وزعمهم أن الجهات كون للأجسام وكل جهة تغاير صاحبتها، والعرض شيء واحد فلا وجه لجعله أشياء متغايرة مع أنه في نفسه شيء واحد كما شنع الإسكافي وجعفر بن حرب وغيرهما حيث صنفوا تصانيف لإبطال جهات الفعل وكل ذلك لإنكارهم الحقيقة وجهلهم بحقائق مذاهب الخصوم، هذا تقرير مذهبهم، وإن كنا لا نحتاج إلى القول بجهات الفعل على ما قررنا. وشيخنا أبو منصور الماتريدي ذكر جهات الفعل لا لحاجته إلى ذلك بل على طريق المساهلة، وتصحيحا للمذهب وإبطالا للباطل من جميع الوجوه.
And [they mean] north, south, behind, and front, but they intend what we have explained of the existence of directions in terms of expression and meaning, not the directions from which sensation of what meets them is possible. And by knowing the interpretation of directions the ignorance of the Mu'tazila appears in their denial of that, and their claim that directions are a 'being' for bodies and every direction differs from its companion, and that the accident is one thing so there is no face to make it multiple things while it is in itself one thing, as al-Askafi and Ja'far ibn Harb and others harangued, where they composed treatises to nullify the directions of action, and all of that is due to their denial of reality and their ignorance of the realities of the doctrines of opponents. This is the exposition of their school, although we have no need to affirm directions of action as we have established. And our shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi mentioned the directions of action not out of need for that, but by way of leniency, and to correct the school and to nullify falsehood from all aspects.
Source pages p. 935

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'directions of action' (جهات الفعل) as used by proponents like al-Maturidi. Nasafi clarifies that they do not mean physical directions like north or south that can be sensed; rather, they refer to relational descriptions. He then criticizes the Mu'tazila for denying this concept due to ignorance, claiming that directions are a property of bodies and that an accident cannot have multiple directions. He mentions al-Askafi and Ja'far ibn Harb who wrote works refuting directions of action. Nasafi states that their denial stems from ignorance of reality and opponents' doctrines. He concludes that the school's position is as stated, though he notes no need to affirm directions of action as previously established. He adds that al-Maturidi mentioned directions of action only as a concession and to correct the school and refute falsehood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazila believed that 'directions of action' refer to physical directions that are properties of bodies, and that an accident cannot have multiple such directions.
Correction: Nasafi explains that 'directions' are meant relationally, not physically, and the concept is valid as a relational description.

The Five Linguistic Meanings of al-Qada'
The author enumerates five linguistic meanings of the term qada'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five linguistic meanings of the term qada' as used in Arabic, and which meaning is relevant to the theological discussion of qada and qadar.

Core Idea
The term 'qada' has five distinct meanings; the key one for theology is 'action'.

Before diving into the theological concept of divine decree, the author clarifies what the word 'qada' means in Arabic. He lists five distinct usages, supported by Quranic verses and poetry. This linguistic groundwork prevents confusion and shows that the theological term is rooted in common language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to get a sense of the five meanings listed.
Notice how each meaning is introduced with 'يذكر ويراد به' (it is mentioned and intended by it).
2
Identify each meaning and its evidence
Go through each of the five meanings: judgment, command, completion, action, and informing. Note the Quranic verse or poetic line that supports each.
The author uses both linguistic examples and scriptural quotations.
3
Find the theological meaning
Look for the sentence where the author says which meaning is intended in the theological discussion. Underline the phrase 'وهو المراد في المسألة' (and that is what is intended in the question).
4
Reflect on the method
Ask yourself why the author begins with linguistic clarification. How does this approach help in kalam discussions?
This shows the importance of defining terms before debating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت أن الله تعالى هو الذي خلق الأفعال، ثبت أنه تعالى قضى تكونها وقدرها على ما عليه من حسن وقبح، فوقعت الغنية عن التكلم في هذه المسألة ابتداء، فتكلم في معنى القضاء والقدر.

[معنى القضاء]:
القضاء يذكر ويراد به الحكم يقال : قضى القاضي على فلان بكذا أي حكم عليه، ويذكر ويراد به الأمر، قال الله تعالى: ﴿ وَقَضَى رَبُّكَ أَلَّا تَعْبُدُوا إِلَّا إِيَّاهُ ﴾ أي أمر ربك وحكم وألزم ، ويذكر ويراد به الفراغ يقال قضيت أمر كذا أو انقضى الأمر أي فرغت عنه، وصار الأمر مفروغا عنه، إذ هو انفعال من القضاء، ومنه - والله أعلم - قضيت حاجة فلان أي فرغت عن دفعها، وقضيت الدين أي فرغت عن أدائه أو فرغت ذمتي، ويذكر ويراد به الفعل وهو المراد في المسألة قال أبو ذؤيب الهذلي: وعليهما مسرودتان قضاهما داود أو صنع السوابغ تبع أي صنعهما وأحكم صنعتهما ، وكان أصله من الإحكام، وقال ابن عرفة: قضاء الشيء إحكامه وإمضاؤه والفراغ منه، ويذكر أيضا ويراد به الإعلام والإخبار قال الله تعالى: ﴿ وَقَضَيْنَا إِلَى بَنِي إِسْرَاءِيلَ فِي الْكِتَب ﴾ الآية أي أعلمناهم، وقيل : القضاء أصله انقطاع الشيء وتمامه والمراد من قولنا الطاعات والمعاصي
And when it is established that Allah—exalted is He—is the one who created actions, it is established that He—exalted is He—decreed their occurrence and ordained them according to what they are of good and evil. So the need for speaking about this question initially is removed, so we speak about the meaning of al-qada' and al-qadar.

[Meaning of al-qada']:
Al-qada' is mentioned and intended by it: judgment (al-hukm). It is said: the judge decreed (qada) against so-and-so such-and-such, meaning he judged against him. And it is mentioned and intended by it: command (al-amr). Allah—exalted is He—said: "And your Lord has decreed (qada) that you worship none but Him" (17:23), meaning your Lord commanded, judged, and obligated. And it is mentioned and intended by it: completion/al-faragh. It is said: I completed (qadaytu) such-and-such matter, or the matter was concluded (inqada), meaning I finished it, and the matter became finished, because it is a passive state from al-qada'. And from this—and Allah knows best—is: I discharged (qadaytu) the need of so-and-so, meaning I finished relieving it; and I paid off (qadaytu) the debt, meaning I finished paying it or I cleared my liability. And it is mentioned and intended by it: action (al-fi'l), and that is what is intended in the question. Abu Dhu'ayb al-Hudhali said: "And upon them are two coats of mail, which Da'ud made (qadāhumā) or made (sana'a) the lengthy coats of mail of Tabba‘" meaning he made them and perfected their craftsmanship, and its origin is from precision (al-ihkām). Ibn 'Arafa said: The qada' of a thing is its precision, its execution, and finishing it. And it is also mentioned and intended by it: informing and notification (al-i‘lām wa al-ikhbār). Allah—exalted is He—said: "And We decreed (qadaynā) to the Children of Israel in the Book" (17:4), the verse, meaning We informed them. And it is said: al-qada' originally means the cutting off of a thing and its completion, and what is intended by our saying: acts of obedience and disobedience
Source pages p. 984

Commentary
This passage is a transition and a definition. First, the author states a premise: since it has been established that Allah creates all actions, it follows that He decrees and ordains them. Thus, the author moves to clarifying the meaning of the terms 'al-qada' and 'al-qadar', focusing first on 'al-qada'. The definition is linguistic: the author enumerates five distinct meanings of the word 'qada' as used in Arabic, each supported by evidence from language, the Quran, or poetry. Meaning 1: judgment (al-hukm), exemplified by a judge's ruling. Meaning 2: command (al-amr), supported by Quran 17:23 where 'qada' is interpreted as commanding and obligating. Meaning 3: completion/finishing (al-faragh), illustrated by phrases like 'qadaytu al-amr' (I finished the matter) and derived uses like discharging a need or paying a debt. Meaning 4: action (al-fi'l), which is the intended meaning in the theological discussion. This is supported by a line of poetry from Abu Dhu'ayb al-Hudhali, where 'qada' means 'made' or 'crafted with precision'. The root notion here is precision (al-ihkām). Meaning 5: informing/notification (al-i‘lām), supported by Quran 17:4 where 'qadaynā' means 'We informed'. Finally, the author notes that 'al-qada' originally means the cutting off of a thing and its completion, and that in the context of acts of obedience and disobedience, it refers to... (the text ends). This systematic enumeration shows that the theological term 'qada' is not a novel concept but draws on established Arabic usage, particularly the sense of action and precise determination.

حكم
أمر
فراغ
فعل
إعلام
إحكام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'qada' in theological contexts only means judgment or command.
Correction: The author shows that 'qada' has multiple linguistic meanings, and the intended meaning in divine decree is 'action' (al-fi'l), not judgment or command alone.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which linguistic meaning of 'qada' is the one intended in the theological discussion about qada and qadar?
A) Judgment (al-hukm)
B) Command (al-amr)
C) Completion (al-faragh)
D) Action (al-fi'l)

Key Takeaway
The word 'qada' has five linguistic meanings, but in the discussion of divine decree it specifically refers to action (al-fi'l).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear 'qada', do you immediately think only of fate and predestination? How does knowing that it also means 'action' change the way you understand Allah's qada in your own life, especially regarding your actions?
?
The author shows that words have multiple layers. Is there a concept in your own belief that you may have misunderstood due to taking a single meaning? How can you ensure you are understanding divine terms correctly, just as the passage roots qada' in its linguistic origins?
?
The root of qada' is precision (ihkam) and completion. In what areas of your life are you lacking precision in your obedience to Allah? How does the fact that Allah's qada' is precise and complete challenge you to be more diligent and thorough in your worship?

The Linguistic Meanings of Qada' (Decree) and Its Theological Application
Nasafi explains the multiple linguistic meanings of qada' and its theological application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple linguistic meanings of the term qada' (decree) and how Nasafi identifies its theological meaning as creation by Allah.

Core Idea
Qada' means command, completion, action, and announcement—but in theology it means Allah's creation of actions.

The Arabic term qada' carries several distinct meanings in the language. Imam al-Nasafi carefully enumerates these meanings, drawing on Quranic verses and poetry to illustrate each. He then clarifies which meaning is intended when we speak of divine decree in relation to human actions. This lesson unpacks these meanings and shows how the theological usage is grounded in the linguistic root.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to grasp the overall flow.
Notice how Nasafi uses scriptural and poetic evidence.
2
Identify the four linguistic meanings
The passage lists four meanings: command, completion, action, and announcement. Find each one in the text.
Each meaning is introduced by the phrase 'ويذكر ويراد به'.
3
Examine the evidence for each meaning
For each meaning, Nasafi provides a Quranic verse or a line of poetry as proof. Note how the evidence supports the meaning.
The meaning 'action' is illustrated by poetry, not Quran.
4
Understand the theological conclusion
Nasafi states that in theology, qada' related to human actions means 'creation' (khalq) and 'bringing into existence' (takwin). He also mentions that proof for this has been established.
This is the key takeaway for Islamic doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه، ويذكر ويراد به الأمر، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَقَضَى رَبُّكَ أَلَّا تَعْبُدُوا إِلَّا إِيَّاهُ﴾ أي أمر ربك وحكم وألزم، ويذكر ويراد به الفراغ يقال قضيت أمر كذا أو انقضى الأمر أي فرغت عنه، وصار الأمر مفروغا عنه، إذ هو انفعال من القضاء، ومنه - والله أعلم - قضيت حاجة فلان أي فرغت عن دفعها، وقضيت الدين أي فرغت عن أدائه أو فرغت ذمتي، ويذكر ويراد به الفعل وهو المراد في المسألة قال أبو ذؤيب الهذلي: وعليهما مسرودتان قضاهما داود أو صنع السوابغ تبع أي صنعهما وأحكم صنعتهما، وكان أصله من الإحكام، وقال ابن عرفة: قضاء الشيء إحكامه وإمضاؤه والفراغ منه، ويذكر أيضا ويراد به الإعلام والإخبار قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَقَضَيْنَا إِلَى بَنِي إِسْرَاءِيلَ فِي الْكِتَب﴾ الآية أي أعلمناهم، وقيل: القضاء أصله انقطاع الشيء وتمامه والمراد من قولنا الطاعات والمعاصي كلها بقضاء الله تعالى أي بخلقه وتكوينه، وقد أقمنا الدلالة عليه بحمد الله تعالى.
It is mentioned and intended by it (qada') the command. Allah Almighty said: 'And your Lord has decreed that you worship none but Him' meaning your Lord commanded, judged, and obliged. It is mentioned and intended by it completion; one says 'I finished such-and-such matter' or 'the matter ended' meaning I finished it, and the matter became finished, for it is a passive from qada'. From it – and Allah knows best – 'I fulfilled so-and-so's need' meaning I finished fulfilling it, and 'I paid the debt' meaning I finished paying it or I cleared my liability. It is mentioned and intended by it the action, and that is what is meant in the issue. Abu Dhu'ayb al-Hudhali said: 'And upon them are two coats of mail, which David fashioned or the maker of the wide coats of mail, Tabba'' meaning he made them and perfected their making. Its origin is from perfection. Ibn 'Arafa said: 'Qada' of a thing is its perfection, execution, and completion of it.' It is also mentioned and intended by it announcement and informing. Allah Almighty said: 'And We decreed to the Children of Israel in the Book' meaning We informed them. It is said: qada' originally means termination of a thing and its completion. And what is meant by our statement that acts of obedience and disobedience are all by the qada' of Allah Almighty is by His creation and bringing into existence. And we have established the proof for that, praise be to Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 985

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and theological analysis of the term 'qada'' (decree). The author begins by listing the various meanings of the word in Arabic. First, it is used to mean 'command,' as in the Quranic verse where Allah commands that none be worshipped except Him. Second, it means 'completion' or 'finishing,' as in finishing a task or paying a debt. Third, it means 'action' or 'making,' illustrated by a line of poetry where David is said to have 'made' coats of mail, and the root connotes perfection. Fourth, it means 'announcement' or 'informing,' as in Allah informing the Children of Israel in the Book. The author then clarifies that the theological meaning of 'qada'' in the context of human actions is 'creation' and 'bringing into existence,' and that this is the intended sense when saying all acts are by Allah's qada'. The passage concludes by asserting that proof for this has been established.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada')
الأمر (al-amr)
الفراغ (al-faragh)
الفعل (al-fi'l)
الإعلام (al-i'lam)
الخلق (al-khalq)
التكوين (al-takwin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'qada'' is often misunderstood as only meaning 'predestination' in a fatalistic sense, without recognizing its multiple linguistic meanings.
Correction: Nasafi shows that qada' has several meanings in Arabic (command, completion, action, announcement), and its theological meaning in the context of human actions is specifically 'creation' (khalq/takwin), not a blind fatalism.

Checkpoint
Which meaning of 'qada'' does Nasafi identify as the intended theological meaning in the context of human actions?
A) Command
B) Completion
C) Action
D) Announcement

Key Takeaway
The term qada' in Islamic theology refers specifically to Allah's creation and bringing into existence of all acts, as established by linguistic analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that every act of obedience and disobedience is created by Allah (qada'). Think of a recent sin you committed. Do you truly believe that Allah created that action? How does that affect your sense of personal responsibility? Are you using this belief as an excuse to not repent, or does it drive you to seek His mercy more earnestly?
?
The passage highlights that qada' also means 'completion'—Allah finishes and perfects His creation. When you struggle with a task, do you rely on Allah to bring it to completion, or do you trust only your own efforts? In what area of your life right now do you need to acknowledge that qada' belongs to Allah alone?
?
Allah has 'decreed' (qada') that you worship none but Him. Are you fulfilling this command in your daily life, or are there hidden forms of shirk (like seeking people's approval) that contradict this decree? What specific step will you take today to align your actions more perfectly with this fundamental qada'?

Linguistic Meaning of 'Akfiruni' and Cognate Verbs
Nasafi explains 'akfiruni' as 'they called me a disbeliever' and cites Tarafa's poetry for analogous verbal forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic verb form 'akfiruni' means 'they called me a disbeliever' and that similar patterns can mean 'he called someone X' or 'he found someone X'.

Core Idea
Arabic verb patterns can express calling someone something or finding them to be such.

Before we can engage in kalam arguments about concepts like 'guidance' and 'misguidance', we need to understand the precise linguistic forms used in the Qur'an and Arabic poetry. This short lesson clarifies the meaning of 'akfiruni' (they called me a disbeliever) and similar constructions. The poet Tarafa provides a vivid example of how these verbs convey calling someone by an attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the short Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own words establish the baseline.
The passage is only four lines; read carefully.
2
Understand the primary meaning of 'akfiruni'
Notice that the author explicitly states 'akfiruni' means 'they called me a disbeliever' (سموني كافراً). This is a direct lexical clarification.
This will be important for later theological arguments.
3
Analyze Tarafa's poetic line
Examine the line from Tarafa. The verb 'asharrani' follows the same pattern and means 'he called me evil'. See how the verbal form functions in context.
Tarafa's example reinforces the 'calling' meaning.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
The author mentions that some linguists interpret these patterns as 'finding someone to be such'. For example, 'anhaltu fulanan' means 'I found him emaciated'. Note this alternative.
This alternative will be relevant when discussing 'guidance' and 'misguidance' in later lessons.
5
Connect to broader kalam discussions
Reflect on how this linguistic precision affects interpretations of Quranic verses. If verbs like 'adhalla' (led astray) can mean 'called misguided' or 'found misguided', the Mu'tazilite claim of 'making' misguided is weakened.
This lesson builds the linguistic foundation for that argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: أكفروني أي سموني كافراً. وقال طرفة: وما زال شربي الراح حتى أشرني خليلي وحتى ساءني بعض ذلك أي سماني بشرير، أو ربما يقولون: معناه وجده ضالاً يقال: أنحلت فلانا وأجبنته أي وجدته نحيلاً جباناً.
His saying: 'Akfiruni' means they called me a disbeliever. And Tarafa said: 'My drinking of wine did not cease until my friend called me evil and until some of that displeased me' meaning he called me evil. Or perhaps they say: its meaning is 'he found him misguided'; it is said: 'I emaciated so-and-so and I cowardiced him' meaning I found him emaciated and cowardly.
Source pages p. 991

Commentary
This passage provides lexical notes on the verb forms derived from 'kufr' and 'dalal'. The author explains that 'akfiruni' means 'they called me a disbeliever'. He then quotes a line from the pre-Islamic poet Tarafa ibn al-'Abd, where 'asharrani' means 'he called me evil'. The author notes that some linguists explain these forms as meaning 'he found someone to be such', as in 'anhaltu fulanan' meaning 'I found him emaciated'. This linguistic background will be used later to challenge Mu'tazilite claims that 'guidance' and 'misguidance' in the Qur'an merely mean 'calling someone guided/misguided' or 'finding them so'.

Key Terms
أكفروني
أشرني
أنحلت
أجبنته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'akfiruni' means 'they made me a disbeliever' or 'they caused me to disbelieve'.
Correction: The passage clearly states it means 'they called me a disbeliever' (سموني كافراً), not that they caused disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the word 'أكفروني' mean?
A) I made them disbelievers
B) They called me a disbeliever
C) I found them disbelieving
D) They made me a disbeliever

Key Takeaway
The verb form 'akfiruni' means 'they called me a disbeliever', not 'they made me a disbeliever'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone use strong religious language like 'kafir', do you stop to consider whether they mean 'calling' or 'making'? How might this lesson change the way you listen to such accusations?
?
Have you ever been quick to label someone based on their actions or words, only to later realize you misunderstood their intent? How does the distinction between 'calling' and 'finding' remind you to be cautious in judgment?
?
This lesson shows that even a single verb pattern can carry multiple nuances. Where in your own understanding of faith might you be oversimplifying complex truths? Will you commit to investigating the deeper meanings before forming conclusions?
